$$\vec{v}(t) = \frac{d\vec{r}}{dt} = -8\sin(2t)\,\hat{i} + 8\cos(2t)\,\hat{j}$$

At $t = \pi/4$: $2t = \pi/2$, so $\sin(\pi/2) = 1$, $\cos(\pi/2) = 0$:
$$\vec{v}(\pi/4) = -8\,\hat{i} + 0\,\hat{j} = -8\,\hat{i} \text{ m/s}$$

**(b):** Speed $= |\vec{v}| = \sqrt{64 + 0} = 8$ m/s (or $R\omega = 4 \times 2 = 8$ m/s ✓)

**(c):** $\vec{r} \cdot \vec{v} = (4\cos 2t)(-8\sin 2t) + (4\sin 2t)(8\cos 2t)$
$= -32\sin 2t\cos 2t + 32\sin 2t\cos 2t = 0$ ✓

The dot product is zero at all times — position and velocity are always perpendicular in uniform circular motion. This confirms that velocity is always tangent to the circle.

---

**Example 3 (JEE Advanced style):** A particle moves along the x-axis with velocity $v(t) = 6 - 3t$ m/s.

(a) Find the position as a function of time, given $x(0) = 2$ m.
(b) Find the time when the particle is momentarily at rest.
(c) Find the total distance covered from $t = 0$ to $t = 4$ s.
(d) Find the displacement from $t = 0$ to $t = 4$ s.

**(a):**
$$x(t) = x_0 + \int_0^t v\,dt' = 2 + \int_0^t(6-3t')\,dt' = 2 + \left[6t' - \frac{3t'^2}{2}\right]_0^t = 2 + 6t - \frac{3t^2}{2}$$

**(b):** $v = 0 \Rightarrow 6 - 3t = 0 \Rightarrow t = 2$ s.

**(c):** The particle reverses at $t = 2$ s. Split:

$x(0) = 2$ m, $x(2) = 2 + 12 - 6 = 8$ m, $x(4) = 2 + 24 - 24 = 2$ m.

Distance $= |x(2) - x(0)| + |x(4) - x(2)| = |8 - 2| + |2 - 8| = 6 + 6 = 12$ m.

**(d):** Displacement $= x(4) - x(0) = 2 - 2 = 0$ m.

The particle ends where it started. Distance = 12 m; displacement = 0.

---

### 6.11 Instantaneous Velocity in Non-Inertial Frames

Recall from Section 2: velocity is frame-dependent. If a particle has instantaneous velocity $\vec{v}_P$ in frame S, and frame S moves at velocity $\vec{v}_0$ relative to frame S', then in S':

$$\vec{v}'_P = \vec{v}_P - \vec{v}_0$$

This is the Galilean velocity transformation — the classical (non-relativistic) rule for converting instantaneous velocities between frames. Instantaneous velocity transforms the same way as average velocity.

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

- Instantaneous velocity = $\vec{v}(t) = d\vec{r}/dt$ = limit of average velocity as $\Delta t \to 0$
- Geometrically: instantaneous velocity = slope of tangent to $x$-$t$ graph
- Direction: always tangent to the trajectory
- Instantaneous speed = $|\vec{v}|$ — always non-negative
- $v = 0$ at turning points; does NOT imply $a = 0$
- Position from velocity: $x(t) = x_0 + \int v\,dt$ = area under $v$-$t$ graph (signed)
- For constant $a$: velocity at time-midpoint = average velocity; velocity at position-midpoint = $\sqrt{(u^2+v^2)/2}$ (RMS, not average)
- Velocity is frame-dependent: $\vec{v}'_P = \vec{v}_P - \vec{v}_0$ (Galilean transformation)
</Callout>

---

*— Section 6 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 7: Acceleration.*

---

## SECTION 7 — Acceleration

---

### 7.1 Why Acceleration Matters More Than Velocity

Velocity tells you how fast an object moves and in what direction. But velocity can change — a car brakes, a ball curves through the air, a planet bends its orbit around the Sun. The quantity that describes how velocity changes is **acceleration**.

In the hierarchy of kinematics, acceleration is the most dynamically important quantity. Newton's Second Law is $\vec{F} = m\vec{a}$ — it does not say "force causes velocity," it says "force causes acceleration." Forces act on acceleration directly; position and velocity are consequences.

Understanding acceleration deeply — especially its direction, its independence from the velocity vector, and its behaviour in 2D — is what separates students who solve JEE Advanced mechanics problems from those who cannot.

---

### 7.2 Definition: Instantaneous Acceleration

<Formula label="Instantaneous Acceleration">
$$\vec{a}(t) = \frac{d\vec{v}}{dt} = \frac{d^2\vec{r}}{dt^2}$$
</Formula>

<Callout type="info">
**Instantaneous Acceleration**

Acceleration is the **rate of change of velocity** — the derivative of the velocity vector with respect to time.

Since $\vec{v} = d\vec{r}/dt$, it follows that $\vec{a} = d^2\vec{r}/dt^2$: acceleration is the second derivative of position.

In components (2D):

$$\vec{a} = \frac{dv_x}{dt}\,\hat{i} + \frac{dv_y}{dt}\,\hat{j} = \frac{d^2x}{dt^2}\,\hat{i} + \frac{d^2y}{dt^2}\,\hat{j} = a_x\,\hat{i} + a_y\,\hat{j}$$
</Callout>

The three quantities are linked in a chain:

$$\vec{r}(t) \xrightarrow{\frac{d}{dt}} \vec{v}(t) \xrightarrow{\frac{d}{dt}} \vec{a}(t) \xrightarrow{\frac{d}{dt}} \vec{j}(t) \text{ (jerk)}$$

And in reverse by integration:

$$\vec{a}(t) \xrightarrow{\int dt} \vec{v}(t) \xrightarrow{\int dt} \vec{r}(t)$$

---

### 7.3 What Acceleration Really Means: Three Physical Cases

Acceleration acts whenever **anything about velocity changes** — its magnitude, its direction, or both.

#### Case 1: Only Speed Changes (1D motion)

In straight-line motion, the velocity has only one component and no direction change is possible. Acceleration simply makes the object move faster or slower.

- $\vec{a}$ in the same direction as $\vec{v}$: object **speeds up**
- $\vec{a}$ opposite to $\vec{v}$: object **slows down** (decelerates)

#### Case 2: Only Direction Changes (uniform circular motion)

An object moves at constant speed $v$ around a circle. Its speed never changes — yet it has acceleration. This acceleration is entirely devoted to changing the direction of velocity.

$$a_c = \frac{v^2}{R} \quad \text{directed inward (centripetal)}$$

The speed is constant, but the velocity vector rotates. The centripetal acceleration is always perpendicular to $\vec{v}$ — it changes direction without changing speed.

#### Case 3: Both Magnitude and Direction Change (general curved motion)

In projectile motion, circular motion with changing speed, or any general curve, both the magnitude and direction of velocity change simultaneously.

The acceleration vector in this case has two components:
- **Tangential component** $a_t = dv/dt$: changes the speed (along the direction of motion)
- **Normal/centripetal component** $a_c = v^2/\rho$: changes the direction (perpendicular to the direction of motion, toward the centre of curvature)

<Formula label="Tangential and Normal Acceleration">
$$a_t = \frac{dv}{dt} \qquad a_c = \frac{v^2}{\rho} \qquad |\vec{a}| = \sqrt{a_t^2 + a_c^2}$$
</Formula>

where $\rho$ is the **radius of curvature** of the path at that point.

<Callout type="info">
**The Tangential-Normal Decomposition**

For any curved motion, the acceleration decomposes into:

$$\vec{a} = a_t\,\hat{T} + a_c\,\hat{N}$$

where $\hat{T}$ is the unit tangent (direction of motion) and $\hat{N}$ is the unit normal (toward the centre of curvature).

- $a_t$ and $a_c$ are always perpendicular to each other.
- $a_t$ is responsible for changes in **speed**.
- $a_c$ is responsible for changes in **direction**.
- If $a_t = 0$: uniform circular motion (constant speed).
- If $a_c = 0$: straight-line motion (no curvature).
</Callout>

---

### 7.4 The Most Important Misconception: Sign of Acceleration

<Callout type="warning">
**"Negative acceleration = deceleration" is WRONG.**

The sign of acceleration (in 1D) tells you its **direction** relative to your chosen positive axis — nothing more. Whether the object is speeding up or slowing down depends on the **relationship between the signs of $v$ and $a$**.

**Correct rule:**
- If $v$ and $a$ have the **same sign** → object is **speeding up**
- If $v$ and $a$ have **opposite signs** → object is **slowing down** (decelerating)

**Example:** Choose downward as positive. A ball thrown upward has:
- Initial velocity $v_0 = -20$ m/s (upward = negative direction)
- Acceleration $a = +g = +10$ m/s² (gravity acts downward = positive direction)

On the way up: $v < 0$, $a > 0$ → opposite signs → **slowing down** ✓
At the top: $v = 0$, $a = +10$ m/s² → acceleration is nonzero even though $v = 0$
On the way down: $v > 0$, $a > 0$ → same sign → **speeding up** ✓

If you had chosen upward as positive: $v_0 = +20$, $a = -10$. On the way up: $v > 0$, $a < 0$ → slowing down ✓. Same physics, different signs.
</Callout>

---

### 7.5 Acceleration from Position: Three Equivalent Methods

Given position $x(t)$, there are three equivalent ways to find acceleration:

**Method 1 (Direct double differentiation):**
$$a(t) = \frac{d^2x}{dt^2}$$

Differentiate position twice. Fastest for polynomial and sinusoidal $x(t)$.

**Method 2 (Via velocity):**
$$v(t) = \frac{dx}{dt}, \qquad a(t) = \frac{dv}{dt}$$

Differentiate twice in two steps. Good when you also need the velocity explicitly.

**Method 3 ($a$ as a function of $x$ — using the chain rule):**

$$a = \frac{dv}{dt} = \frac{dv}{dx}\cdot\frac{dx}{dt} = v\frac{dv}{dx}$$

<Formula label="Chain Rule Form of Acceleration">
$$\vec{a} = v\,\frac{dv}{dx}\,\hat{i} \quad \text{(in 1D)}$$
</Formula>

This form is essential when $a$ is given as a function of $x$ (position-dependent force problems in JEE Advanced):

$$a(x) = v\frac{dv}{dx} \implies v\,dv = a(x)\,dx \implies \int_{v_0}^{v}v'\,dv' = \int_{x_0}^{x}a(x')\,dx'$$

$$\frac{v^2 - v_0^2}{2} = \int_{x_0}^{x}a(x')\,dx'$$

This is the work-energy theorem in kinematic disguise.

---

### 7.6 Getting Velocity and Position from Acceleration: Integration

Given $a(t)$, integrate to find $v(t)$; integrate again to find $x(t)$.

**General formula:**

$$v(t) = v_0 + \int_0^t a(t')\,dt'$$

$$x(t) = x_0 + \int_0^t v(t')\,dt' = x_0 + v_0 t + \int_0^t\!\int_0^{t'} a(t'')\,dt''\,dt'$$

**The meaning:** The area under the $a$-$t$ curve from $0$ to $t$ equals the **change in velocity** $\Delta v$. The area under the $v$-$t$ curve gives displacement.

**When $a$ is constant:** The integrals are trivial:

$$v(t) = v_0 + at \qquad x(t) = x_0 + v_0 t + \tfrac{1}{2}at^2$$

These are the familiar equations of motion — derived rigorously from the definition of acceleration.

---

### 7.7 Variable Acceleration: The Three Fundamental Types

JEE Advanced regularly tests variable acceleration. The technique depends on how $a$ is expressed.

#### Type 1: $a$ as a function of time $t$

$$a = f(t) \implies \int dv = \int f(t)\,dt \implies v(t) = v_0 + \int_0^t f(t')\,dt'$$

**Example:** $a = 3t^2 - 2t$. Find $v(t)$ given $v(0) = 5$ m/s.

$$v(t) = 5 + \int_0^t (3t'^2 - 2t')\,dt' = 5 + [t'^3 - t'^2]_0^t = 5 + t^3 - t^2 \text{ m/s}$$

#### Type 2: $a$ as a function of velocity $v$

$$a = f(v) \implies \frac{dv}{dt} = f(v) \implies \int\frac{dv}{f(v)} = \int dt$$

**Example:** $a = -kv$ (viscous drag). Find $v(t)$ and stopping time.

$$\frac{dv}{-kv} = dt \implies -\frac{1}{k}\ln v = t + C \implies v(t) = v_0 e^{-kt}$$

Speed decays exponentially — never reaches zero in finite time. (Compare with $a = -k\sqrt{v}$ which does stop in finite time, as shown in Section 4 examples.)

#### Type 3: $a$ as a function of position $x$

Use $a = v\,dv/dx$:

$$v\,dv = f(x)\,dx \implies \frac{v^2 - v_0^2}{2} = \int_{x_0}^{x}f(x')\,dx'$$

**Example:** $a = -\omega^2 x$ (simple harmonic motion, to be studied later). Then:

$$v\,dv = -\omega^2 x\,dx \implies \frac{v^2}{2} = -\frac{\omega^2 x^2}{2} + C \implies v^2 = v_0^2 - \omega^2 x^2$$

This gives the velocity-position relationship for SHM without needing to solve the differential equation.

---

### 7.8 The Acceleration Due to Gravity

For motion near Earth's surface, the dominant acceleration is gravity:

$$g = 9.8 \text{ m/s}^2 \approx 10 \text{ m/s}^2 \text{ (JEE convention)}$$

**Direction:** Vertically downward, always (regardless of the direction of motion).

**Key facts about $g$:**
- $g$ is the same for all objects, regardless of mass, shape, or material (in the absence of air resistance — Galileo's result)
- $g$ varies slightly with altitude: $g \approx 9.8 - 3.1\times 10^{-6}h$ m/s² where $h$ is altitude in metres
- $g$ is not constant over astronomical distances — it is Newton's law of gravitation that gives $g = GM/r^2$
- At the poles: $g \approx 9.83$ m/s²; at the equator: $g \approx 9.78$ m/s² (centrifugal effect of Earth's rotation)
- In JEE problems: use $g = 10$ m/s² unless specifically instructed otherwise

<Callout type="warning">
**Free fall vs weightlessness**

An object in free fall has acceleration $g$ — it is NOT weightless in the sense of gravity not acting. Gravity acts fully ($F = mg$ downward). The object merely has no surface pushing up on it (no normal force), so it cannot feel its weight. See Section 2.5 (elevator case).

True weightlessness requires being far from all gravitational sources. Astronauts in orbit are NOT truly weightless — they are in free fall, which merely feels like weightlessness.
</Callout>

---

### 7.9 Jerk: The Derivative of Acceleration

The derivative of acceleration with respect to time is called **jerk** (or jolt):

$$\vec{j} = \frac{d\vec{a}}{dt} = \frac{d^3\vec{r}}{dt^3}$$

Jerk is what you physically feel when a car's brakes are applied suddenly (discontinuous acceleration = infinite jerk) vs smoothly applied (gentle, finite jerk). Good engineering design minimises jerk for passenger comfort.

In JEE, jerk appears in:
- Problems asking for the shape of $a$-$t$ graphs (slope of $a$-$t$ = jerk)
- Constraint problems where the third time derivative of position is required
- Advanced dynamics (variable thrust rockets)

---

### 7.10 Acceleration in Different Physical Scenarios

| Scenario | Acceleration | Direction |
|---|---|---|
| Free fall | $g = 10$ m/s² | Vertically downward |
| Car braking from $v$ to 0 over distance $d$ | $v^2/(2d)$ | Opposite to motion |
| Projectile (horizontal) | 0 | N/A |
| Projectile (vertical) | $g$ | Downward |
| Uniform circular motion | $v^2/R = R\omega^2$ | Toward centre |
| SHM at displacement $x$ from equilibrium | $\omega^2 x$ | Toward equilibrium |
| Satellite in circular orbit at radius $r$ | $GM/r^2$ | Toward Earth's centre |
| Charged particle in magnetic field $B$ | $qvB/m$ | Perpendicular to $v$ |

---

### 7.11 The Radius of Curvature

In general 2D motion, the **radius of curvature** $\rho$ at any point on the trajectory relates the speed and centripetal acceleration:

$$\rho = \frac{v^2}{a_c} = \frac{v^2}{|\vec{a}|\sin\phi}$$

where $\phi$ is the angle between $\vec{v}$ and $\vec{a}$.

**For projectile motion** — the radius of curvature at any point:

$$\rho = \frac{v^3}{v_x\,g} = \frac{(v_x^2 + v_y^2)^{3/2}}{v_x\,g}$$

**At the top of the trajectory** ($v_y = 0$, $v = v_x = u\cos\theta$):

$$\rho_{\min} = \frac{(u\cos\theta)^2}{g}$$

The trajectory is most curved at the top (smallest $\rho$) because the speed is smallest there while the full gravitational acceleration acts perpendicularly.

**At launch** ($v = u$, with $\vec{v}$ at angle $\theta$ above horizontal):

At launch, $\vec{v}$ makes angle $\theta$ above horizontal while $\vec{a} = g$ points downward, so the angle between $\vec{v}$ and $\vec{a}$ is $90° + \theta$.

$$a_c = g\cos\theta \implies \rho_{\text{launch}} = \frac{u^2}{g\cos\theta}$$

Since $\cos\theta < 1$ for $\theta > 0$: $\rho_{\text{launch}} > \rho_{\min}$ — the trajectory is less curved at launch than at the top. Surprising but correct.

<Callout type="tip">
**JEE Application: Finding Radius of Curvature**

Method: find the component of acceleration perpendicular to velocity, then use $\rho = v^2/a_\perp$.

At any point of a projectile:
$$a_\perp = g\cos\phi \quad \text{where } \phi = \text{angle of velocity with horizontal}$$
$$\rho = \frac{v^2}{g\cos\phi}$$

At the top: $\phi = 0$, $v = u\cos\theta$, so $\rho_{\min} = u^2\cos^2\theta/g$.
</Callout>

---

### 7.12 Worked Examples

**Example 1:** A particle's position is $x(t) = t^4 - 4t^3 + 6t^2$ metres. Find:
(a) velocity and acceleration at $t = 1$ s
(b) when acceleration is zero
(c) whether the particle is speeding up or slowing down at $t = 1$ s

**(a):**
$$v(t) = 4t^3 - 12t^2 + 12t$$
$$v(1) = 4 - 12 + 12 = 4 \text{ m/s}$$

$$a(t) = 12t^2 - 24t + 12 = 12(t^2 - 2t + 1) = 12(t-1)^2$$
$$a(1) = 0 \text{ m/s}^2$$

**(b):** $a = 0 \Rightarrow (t-1)^2 = 0 \Rightarrow t = 1$ s.

**(c):** At $t = 1$: $a = 0$, so neither speeding up nor slowing down instantaneously. Check $a$ just before and after:
- $a(0.9) = 12(0.9-1)^2 = 12(0.01) = 0.12 > 0$; $v(0.9) = 4(0.729) - 12(0.81) + 12(0.9) = 2.916 - 9.72 + 10.8 = 3.996 > 0$. Same sign → speeding up just before $t = 1$.
- $a(1.1) = 12(0.1)^2 = 0.12 > 0$; $v(1.1) = 4(1.331) - 12(1.21) + 12(1.1) = 5.324 - 14.52 + 13.2 = 4.004 > 0$. Same sign → speeding up just after $t = 1$.

The particle is momentarily neither speeding up nor slowing down at $t = 1$ s (it's an inflection point in speed), but speeds up on both sides.

---

**Example 2:** An object starts with velocity 10 m/s and has acceleration $a = -2v$ m/s² (velocity-dependent drag). Find:
(a) velocity as a function of time
(b) how long it takes for the velocity to halve
(c) does the object ever stop?

**(a):**
$$\frac{dv}{dt} = -2v \implies \frac{dv}{v} = -2\,dt \implies \ln v = -2t + C \implies v(t) = 10e^{-2t} \text{ m/s}$$

**(b):** $v = 5 \Rightarrow 10e^{-2t} = 5 \Rightarrow t = \ln(2)/2 \approx 0.347$ s.

**(c):** $v = 10e^{-2t} \to 0$ as $t \to \infty$ but never equals zero in finite time. The object asymptotically approaches rest.

---

**Example 3 (JEE Advanced):** A particle moves in a plane with constant acceleration $\vec{a} = 2\,\hat{i} + 3\,\hat{j}$ m/s². At $t = 0$: $\vec{r}_0 = \hat{i} + 2\,\hat{j}$ m, $\vec{v}_0 = 3\,\hat{i} - \hat{j}$ m/s. Find:
(a) velocity at $t = 2$ s
(b) position at $t = 2$ s
(c) the angle between $\vec{v}$ and $\vec{a}$ at $t = 2$ s

**(a):**
$$\vec{v}(2) = \vec{v}_0 + \vec{a}t = (3\,\hat{i} - \hat{j}) + (2\,\hat{i} + 3\,\hat{j})(2) = (3+4)\,\hat{i} + (-1+6)\,\hat{j} = 7\,\hat{i} + 5\,\hat{j} \text{ m/s}$$

**(b):**
$$\vec{r}(2) = \vec{r}_0 + \vec{v}_0 t + \tfrac{1}{2}\vec{a}t^2 = (\hat{i}+2\hat{j}) + (3\hat{i}-\hat{j})(2) + \tfrac{1}{2}(2\hat{i}+3\hat{j})(4)$$
$$= (\hat{i}+2\hat{j}) + (6\hat{i}-2\hat{j}) + (4\hat{i}+6\hat{j}) = 11\,\hat{i} + 6\,\hat{j} \text{ m}$$

**(c):**
$$\cos\theta = \frac{\vec{v}\cdot\vec{a}}{|\vec{v}||\vec{a}|} = \frac{7(2)+5(3)}{\sqrt{49+25}\cdot\sqrt{4+9}} = \frac{14+15}{\sqrt{74}\cdot\sqrt{13}} = \frac{29}{\sqrt{962}}$$

$$\theta = \cos^{-1}\!\left(\frac{29}{\sqrt{962}}\right) \approx \cos^{-1}(0.935) \approx 20.7°$$

Since $\cos\theta > 0$, the angle between $\vec{v}$ and $\vec{a}$ is acute — the object is **speeding up** at $t = 2$ s (the component of $\vec{a}$ along $\vec{v}$ is positive).

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

- Acceleration $\vec{a} = d\vec{v}/dt = d^2\vec{r}/dt^2$: rate of change of velocity, second derivative of position.
- Acceleration exists whenever **anything** about velocity changes — magnitude, direction, or both.
- **Tangential component** $a_t = dv/dt$: changes speed. **Normal component** $a_c = v^2/\rho$: changes direction.
- Sign of $a$ encodes direction relative to your axis choice, not whether object is speeding up or slowing down — that depends on the relative signs of $v$ and $a$.
- $v = 0$ does NOT imply $a = 0$ (ball at peak of throw).
- Three integration types for variable $a$: $a(t)$ → separate and integrate directly; $a(v)$ → use $dv/dt = a(v)$; $a(x)$ → use $v\,dv/dx = a(x)$.
- Radius of curvature $\rho = v^2/a_c$; minimum at the top of a projectile trajectory.
- Jerk = $d\vec{a}/dt$ = slope of $a$-$t$ graph.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 7 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 8: Motion in One Dimension (Equations of Motion).*

---

## SECTION 8 — Motion in One Dimension: Equations of Motion

---

### 8.1 The Special Case of Constant Acceleration

When acceleration is constant — the same value at every instant throughout the motion — the kinematic equations take a beautifully simple closed form. This is not just a mathematical convenience; constant acceleration is physically real in many important situations:

- Free fall near Earth's surface ($a = g = 10$ m/s² downward)
- A car braking with locked wheels on a flat road (approximately constant friction force)
- A charged particle between parallel plates in a uniform electric field
- A ball rolling down a smooth inclined plane

For all of these, once you know the initial conditions ($x_0$, $v_0$) and the acceleration $a$, you can determine every kinematic quantity at any time — position, velocity, displacement, time between events — using just four equations.

---

### 8.2 Deriving the Four Equations of Motion

Let:
- $t = 0$: initial instant; position $x_0$ (often set to 0), velocity $u$
- $t = t$: general instant; position $x$, velocity $v$
- $a$ = constant acceleration (positive or negative, depending on direction)
- $s = x - x_0$ = displacement from initial position

#### Equation 1 — Velocity-Time Relation

By definition of constant acceleration:

$$a = \frac{dv}{dt} = \text{constant}$$

Integrating both sides from $t = 0$ to $t$:

$$\int_u^v dv = \int_0^t a\,dt' \implies v - u = at$$

<Formula label="First Equation of Motion">
$$\boxed{v = u + at}$$
</Formula>

**Eliminated:** displacement $s$. Use this when $s$ is not needed.

**Geometric derivation:** On the $v$-$t$ graph, $a$ = constant means a straight line with slope $a$. Starting at $v = u$ at $t = 0$: $v = u + at$. This is the equation of that line.

#### Equation 2 — Displacement-Time Relation

Since $v = ds/dt = u + at$, integrate to find $s(t)$:

$$s = \int_0^t v\,dt' = \int_0^t (u + at')\,dt' = \left[ut' + \frac{1}{2}at'^2\right]_0^t$$

<Formula label="Second Equation of Motion">
$$\boxed{s = ut + \frac{1}{2}at^2}$$
</Formula>

**Eliminated:** final velocity $v$. Use this when $v$ is not needed.

**Geometric derivation:** $s$ = area under the $v$-$t$ graph = area of the trapezoid with parallel sides $u$ (at $t=0$) and $v = u+at$ (at time $t$), width $t$:
$$s = \frac{1}{2}(u + u + at)\cdot t = ut + \frac{1}{2}at^2 \checkmark$$

**Physical interpretation:** $ut$ is the displacement the object would have had if it continued at speed $u$. The $\tfrac{1}{2}at^2$ term is the *additional* displacement due to acceleration — it always has the sign of $a$ and grows quadratically.

#### Equation 3 — Velocity-Displacement Relation

Eliminate time $t$ between Equations 1 and 2. From Eq. 1: $t = (v-u)/a$. Substitute into Eq. 2:

$$s = u\cdot\frac{v-u}{a} + \frac{1}{2}a\cdot\frac{(v-u)^2}{a^2} = \frac{u(v-u)}{a} + \frac{(v-u)^2}{2a}$$

$$= \frac{2u(v-u) + (v-u)^2}{2a} = \frac{(v-u)[2u + (v-u)]}{2a} = \frac{(v-u)(v+u)}{2a} = \frac{v^2 - u^2}{2a}$$

<Formula label="Third Equation of Motion">
$$\boxed{v^2 = u^2 + 2as}$$
</Formula>

**Eliminated:** time $t$. Use this when time is neither given nor required.

**Geometric derivation:** $v^2 - u^2 = (v-u)(v+u) = at \cdot 2\bar{v} = a \cdot (2s/t) \cdot t$... or more elegantly: $s$ = area under $v$-$t$ graph = $\tfrac{(u+v)}{2}\cdot t$. And $v - u = at$, so $t = (v-u)/a$. Therefore $s = \tfrac{(u+v)(v-u)}{2a} = \tfrac{v^2-u^2}{2a}$.

#### Equation 4 — Average Velocity Form

The velocity-time graph for constant $a$ is a straight line. The area under a straight-line $v$-$t$ graph is a trapezoid:

$$s = \frac{u + v}{2} \cdot t$$

<Formula label="Fourth Equation of Motion">
$$\boxed{s = \frac{u+v}{2} \cdot t}$$
</Formula>

**Eliminated:** acceleration $a$. Use this when $a$ is not needed.

**Note:** This is equivalent to "average velocity × time" — valid only for constant acceleration, where the average velocity equals the arithmetic mean of initial and final velocities.

---

### 8.3 The Complete Summary

| Equation | Variables present | Variable eliminated |
|---|---|---|
| $v = u + at$ | $v, u, a, t$ | $s$ |
| $s = ut + \tfrac{1}{2}at^2$ | $s, u, a, t$ | $v$ |
| $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$ | $v, u, a, s$ | $t$ |
| $s = \tfrac{u+v}{2}\cdot t$ | $s, u, v, t$ | $a$ |

**Strategy:** In any constant-acceleration problem, identify the three known quantities and choose the equation that contains those three plus the one unknown.

<Callout type="tip">
**Selecting the Right Equation**

Given: $u$, $a$, $t$. Want: $v$ → use $v = u + at$
Given: $u$, $a$, $t$. Want: $s$ → use $s = ut + \tfrac{1}{2}at^2$
Given: $u$, $v$, $a$. Want: $s$ or $t$ → use $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$ or $t = (v-u)/a$
Given: $u$, $v$, $t$. Want: $s$ → use $s = (u+v)t/2$
Given: $u$, $s$, $t$. Want: $a$ → use $s = ut + \tfrac{1}{2}at^2$

If you have two unknowns, you'll need two of the four equations simultaneously.
</Callout>

---

### 8.4 Displacement in the $n$-th Second

The displacement during the $n$-th second (between $t = n-1$ and $t = n$ seconds) is:

$$s_n = s(n) - s(n-1) = \left[un + \tfrac{1}{2}an^2\right] - \left[u(n-1) + \tfrac{1}{2}a(n-1)^2\right]$$

$$= u + a\cdot\frac{2n-1}{2}$$

<Formula label="Displacement in the nth Second">
$$\boxed{s_n = u + \frac{a(2n-1)}{2}}$$
</Formula>

**The ratio for motion from rest ($u = 0$):**

$$s_1 : s_2 : s_3 : \cdots : s_n = 1 : 3 : 5 : \cdots : (2n-1)$$

Displacements in successive seconds are in the ratio of consecutive odd numbers — discovered experimentally by Galileo in the 17th century and now a standard JEE formula.

**Total displacement up to $n$ seconds** (from rest): $s_{total} = \tfrac{1}{2}an^2$, which grows as $1:4:9:16:…$ (ratio of perfect squares).

<Callout type="tip">
**JEE Shortcut: $n$-th second formula**

In problems that give the displacement in two specific seconds and ask for $u$ or $a$, write the formula for each and subtract:

$$s_{n_2} - s_{n_1} = a(n_2 - n_1)$$

This elegantly eliminates $u$ and directly gives $a$ from the difference of displacements in two specified seconds.
</Callout>

---

### 8.5 Free Fall: The Most Important Special Case

In free fall (no air resistance), the only acceleration is gravity: $a = g = 10$ m/s² downward.

#### Choosing Sign Convention

**Convention 1 (recommended for upward throws):** Upward = positive, downward = negative.
- $a = -g = -10$ m/s²
- Initial velocity $u > 0$ if thrown upward

**Convention 2 (recommended for dropped objects):** Downward = positive.
- $a = +g = +10$ m/s²
- Initial velocity $u = 0$ if dropped

**Either works** — choose whichever makes the displacement positive (avoids negative answers that confuse signs).

#### Key Results for an Object Thrown Upward with Speed $u$

(Taking upward as positive, $a = -g$)

| Quantity | Expression |
|---|---|
| Velocity at time $t$ | $v = u - gt$ |
| Height at time $t$ | $y = ut - \tfrac{1}{2}gt^2$ |
| Time to reach peak | $t_{top} = u/g$ |
| Maximum height | $H = u^2/(2g)$ |
| Time to return to launch height | $T = 2u/g$ |
| Velocity on return to launch height | $-u$ (same magnitude, downward) |
| Speed at any height $h$ above launch | $v = \sqrt{u^2 - 2gh}$ |

<Callout type="info">
**Time-Symmetry of Free Fall**

The upward and downward portions of the trajectory are mirror images in time:
- Time to rise = time to fall back to same height = $u/g$ each
- Speed at any height is the same going up and coming down
- The $v$-$t$ graph is a straight line passing through $v = 0$ at $t = u/g$

This symmetry is a direct consequence of the constant, time-reversal-symmetric acceleration $g$. Exploit it in JEE problems: if you know how long the ball takes to fall from the top, that's also how long it took to rise.
</Callout>

#### Object Thrown Downward or Dropped

Dropped from height $H$ (taking downward as positive, $u = 0$):

$$v(t) = gt \qquad y(t) = \tfrac{1}{2}gt^2$$

Time to hit the ground: $t = \sqrt{2H/g}$

Speed just before impact: $v = \sqrt{2gH}$ (from $v^2 = 2gH$)

---

### 8.6 Multi-Stage Motion Problems

Many JEE problems involve an object that undergoes **different accelerations in different stages**. The key principle: **each stage is independent, connected only by the initial conditions inherited from the previous stage**.

**Framework:**
1. Process Stage 1: find position and velocity at end of Stage 1.
2. These become initial conditions for Stage 2.
3. Process Stage 2. Repeat for further stages.

**Example:** A car starts from rest and accelerates at $2$ m/s² for $10$ s, then travels at constant speed for $20$ s, then decelerates to rest at $4$ m/s². Find the total distance.

*Stage 1 (acceleration):*
$$v_1 = 0 + 2(10) = 20 \text{ m/s}, \qquad s_1 = 0 + \tfrac{1}{2}(2)(100) = 100 \text{ m}$$

*Stage 2 (constant speed):*
$$s_2 = 20 \times 20 = 400 \text{ m}, \qquad v_2 = 20 \text{ m/s (unchanged)}$$

*Stage 3 (deceleration):*
$$0 = 20^2 - 2(4)s_3 \implies s_3 = \frac{400}{8} = 50 \text{ m}$$

$$\text{Total distance} = 100 + 400 + 50 = 550 \text{ m}$$

---

### 8.7 Two-Body Problems: Catching and Collision

A classic JEE problem type: two objects moving along the same line with different initial velocities and/or accelerations.

#### Setup

Object A: initial position $x_A^0$, initial velocity $u_A$, acceleration $a_A$
Object B: initial position $x_B^0$, initial velocity $u_B$, acceleration $a_B$

$$x_A(t) = x_A^0 + u_A t + \tfrac{1}{2}a_A t^2$$
$$x_B(t) = x_B^0 + u_B t + \tfrac{1}{2}a_B t^2$$

**When do they meet?** Set $x_A = x_B$:
$$x_A^0 + u_A t + \tfrac{1}{2}a_A t^2 = x_B^0 + u_B t + \tfrac{1}{2}a_B t^2$$

This is a quadratic in $t$. Solve it.

**Relative displacement** $= x_A - x_B$:
$$\Delta x(t) = (x_A^0 - x_B^0) + (u_A - u_B)t + \tfrac{1}{2}(a_A - a_B)t^2$$

This is also governed by the equations of motion with $u_{rel} = u_A - u_B$ and $a_{rel} = a_A - a_B$.

<Callout type="tip">
**The Relative Motion Shortcut for Pursuit Problems**

Treat the problem in the reference frame of object B. In that frame, B is stationary, and A has:
- Relative initial velocity: $u_{rel} = u_A - u_B$
- Relative acceleration: $a_{rel} = a_A - a_B$
- Initial separation: $d = x_A^0 - x_B^0$

A "catches" B when relative displacement = initial separation. This reduces a two-body problem to a one-body problem.

**Maximum separation** occurs when relative velocity = 0:
$$t^* = -\frac{u_{rel}}{a_{rel}} = \frac{u_B - u_A}{a_A - a_B}$$

**Meeting time** (if they start at the same point): $t_{meet} = 2t^*$ — double the time of maximum separation.
</Callout>

---

### 8.8 When the Equations of Motion Fail

<Callout type="warning">
**The Constant-Acceleration Requirement**

The four equations $v = u+at$, $s = ut+\tfrac{1}{2}at^2$, $v^2 = u^2+2as$, $s = (u+v)t/2$ are valid **only when acceleration is constant throughout the entire time interval**.

They CANNOT be applied when:
- Acceleration varies with time: $a(t)$ (use integration)
- Acceleration varies with position: $a(x)$ (use $v\,dv/dx = a(x)$)
- Acceleration varies with velocity: $a(v)$ (use $dv/dt = a(v)$ or $v\,dv/dx = a(v)$)
- Motion involves direction changes in 2D (apply the equations separately to each component)

**Caught-out example:** A stone is thrown upward. "Time to rise = time to fall" is TRUE because $g$ is constant. If air resistance were included, $a$ would vary with velocity — the equations of motion would fail, and the descent would take longer than the ascent.
</Callout>

---

### 8.9 Worked Examples at JEE Level

**Example 1 (JEE Main pattern):** A body is thrown vertically upward. It rises to a height of 45 m and comes back. Taking $g = 10$ m/s²:
(a) initial velocity
(b) total time in air
(c) velocity when it has covered 20 m on the way down from the top

**(a):** At max height, $v = 0$: $0 = u^2 - 2(10)(45) \Rightarrow u = 30$ m/s.

**(b):** $T = 2u/g = 60/10 = 6$ s.

**(c):** It has fallen 20 m from the top. Using the top as origin (downward positive, $u_{top} = 0$):
$$v^2 = 0 + 2(10)(20) = 400 \Rightarrow v = 20 \text{ m/s (downward)}$$

---

**Example 2 (Two-body):** Car P starts from rest and accelerates at $4$ m/s². Car Q passes the same point 5 s later, also from rest, accelerating at $9$ m/s². When does Q overtake P?

Let $t$ = time measured from when P starts. Q starts at $t = 5$ s, so Q's motion starts at $t - 5$.

$$x_P = \tfrac{1}{2}(4)t^2 = 2t^2$$
$$x_Q = \tfrac{1}{2}(9)(t-5)^2 = 4.5(t-5)^2 \quad \text{(for } t \geq 5\text{)}$$

Set $x_P = x_Q$:
$$2t^2 = 4.5(t-5)^2 = 4.5t^2 - 45t + 112.5$$
$$0 = 2.5t^2 - 45t + 112.5$$
$$t^2 - 18t + 45 = 0$$
$$(t-3)(t-15) = 0 \Rightarrow t = 3 \text{ s or } t = 15 \text{ s}$$

$t = 3$ s is before Q starts, so discard. Q overtakes P at $t = 15$ s.

At $t = 15$: $x_P = 2(225) = 450$ m; $x_Q = 4.5(100) = 450$ m ✓

---

**Example 3 ($n$-th second):** A particle under constant acceleration covers 20 m in the 3rd second and 28 m in the 5th second. Find $u$ and $a$.

$$s_3 = u + \frac{a(5)}{2} = u + 2.5a = 20 \quad \cdots (1)$$
$$s_5 = u + \frac{a(9)}{2} = u + 4.5a = 28 \quad \cdots (2)$$

$(2) - (1)$: $2a = 8 \Rightarrow a = 4$ m/s². Substituting: $u = 20 - 10 = 10$ m/s.

**Check:** $s_4 = 10 + 4(7)/2 = 10 + 14 = 24$ m. Indeed 20, 24, 28 are in arithmetic progression with common difference 4 = $a$ ✓

---

**Example 4 (JEE Advanced style):** A particle starts from rest at $x = 0$ and moves with acceleration $a = 6t - 4$ m/s². Find:
(a) velocity at $t = 3$ s
(b) position at $t = 3$ s
(c) distance from $x = 0$ at $t = 3$ s (note: may differ from position due to reversal)

**(a):**
$$v(t) = \int_0^t (6t' - 4)\,dt' = 3t^2 - 4t$$
$$v(3) = 27 - 12 = 15 \text{ m/s}$$

**(b):**
$$x(t) = \int_0^t v\,dt' = \int_0^t(3t'^2 - 4t')\,dt' = t^3 - 2t^2$$
$$x(3) = 27 - 18 = 9 \text{ m}$$

**(c):** Check for reversal: $v = 0 \Rightarrow 3t^2 - 4t = 0 \Rightarrow t(3t-4) = 0 \Rightarrow t = 0$ or $t = 4/3$ s.

At $t = 4/3$: $x = (4/3)^3 - 2(4/3)^2 = 64/27 - 32/9 = 64/27 - 96/27 = -32/27 \approx -1.19$ m.

So at $t = 4/3$ s the particle is at $x = -32/27$ m (has gone left of the origin), then it turns back.

Distance = $|x(4/3) - x(0)| + |x(3) - x(4/3)|$
$= 32/27 + |9 - (-32/27)|$
$= 32/27 + (9 + 32/27)$
$= 32/27 + 243/27 + 32/27$
$= 307/27 \approx 11.4$ m

Position is 9 m from start; distance traveled is $\approx 11.4$ m — different because the particle reversed direction.

---

### 8.10 Key Identities and Cross-Relations

| Result | Formula | When useful |
|---|---|---|
| $s$ in first $n$ seconds from rest | $s = \tfrac{1}{2}an^2$ | Building a table of positions |
| $s$ in $n$-th second from rest | $s_n = \tfrac{a(2n-1)}{2}$ | Checking ratio 1:3:5:... |
| Ratio of consecutive $s_n$ from rest | $s_1:s_2:s_3:\cdots = 1:3:5:\cdots$ | Quick verification |
| Time to cover $s$ from rest | $t = \sqrt{2s/a}$ | Inverse problem |
| Speed after covering $s$ from rest | $v = \sqrt{2as}$ | Energy-like result |
| Average velocity for const. $a$ | $(u+v)/2$ | Always valid for const. $a$ |

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

Four equations of motion (constant $a$ only): $v = u+at$; $s = ut+\tfrac{1}{2}at^2$; $v^2 = u^2+2as$; $s=(u+v)t/2$. Each eliminates one of the five variables $\{v, u, a, s, t\}$. Match the given and unknown variables to choose the right equation.

Displacement in $n$-th second: $s_n = u + a(2n-1)/2$. From rest: ratio is $1:3:5:\cdots$

Free fall uses these equations with $a = g = 10$ m/s². Time-symmetry: rise time = fall time for an object that returns to its launch height.

Multi-stage: process each stage independently; carry forward position and velocity as initial conditions.

Two-body: use relative motion (subtract one body's kinematics from the other); maximum separation when relative velocity = 0; meeting time = $2 \times$ time of max separation (if starting from same point).

**Fatal error:** applying these equations when $a$ is not constant. Always check.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 8 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 9: Kinematics Graphs.*

---

## SECTION 9 — Kinematics Graphs

---

### 9.1 Why Graphs?

Equations encode kinematics algebraically. Graphs encode it geometrically. A skilled physicist reads the same information from both — and a well-drawn graph often reveals structure that algebraic manipulation obscures.

In JEE Main and Advanced, graph problems have appeared every single year. Students who can quickly extract velocity, acceleration, displacement, and qualitative motion type from a graph have a significant advantage. Students who rely on equations alone are lost whenever the graph is non-standard.

This section builds complete fluency with the three fundamental kinematics graphs and their mutual relationships.

---

### 9.2 The Master Framework

Three graphs describe all of 1D kinematics:

$$\underbrace{x\text{-}t}_{\text{position}} \xrightarrow{\text{slope}} \underbrace{v\text{-}t}_{\text{velocity}} \xrightarrow{\text{slope}} \underbrace{a\text{-}t}_{\text{acceleration}}$$

$$\underbrace{a\text{-}t}_{\text{acceleration}} \xrightarrow{\text{area}} \underbrace{v\text{-}t}_{\text{velocity}} \xrightarrow{\text{area}} \underbrace{x\text{-}t}_{\text{position}}$$

**Two master tools:**

| Graph | Slope gives | Area under curve gives |
|---|---|---|
| $x$-$t$ | Instantaneous velocity $v$ | — (no useful area) |
| $v$-$t$ | Instantaneous acceleration $a$ | Displacement $\Delta x$ |
| $a$-$t$ | Jerk $da/dt$ | Change in velocity $\Delta v$ |

These two tools — slope and area — are the complete toolkit. Every graph question in JEE reduces to applying them correctly.

---

### 9.3 The Position-Time ($x$-$t$) Graph

#### What the Shape Tells You

| Shape | Velocity | Acceleration |
|---|---|---|
| Horizontal line | $v = 0$ | $a = 0$; at rest |
| Straight line, positive slope | $v =$ const $> 0$ | $a = 0$; uniform motion |
| Straight line, negative slope | $v =$ const $< 0$ | $a = 0$; uniform motion (negative direction) |
| Curve concave upward ($\cup$) | $v$ increasing | $a > 0$ |
| Curve concave downward ($\cap$) | $v$ decreasing | $a < 0$ |
| Curve with inflection point | $v$ passes through local extremum | $a = 0$ at that instant |
| Turning point (local max/min) | $v = 0$ (object reverses) | $a \neq 0$ (usually) |

**Why concavity encodes acceleration:** The second derivative $d^2x/dt^2 = a$. By the standard calculus result, $d^2x/dt^2 > 0$ means concave up; $d^2x/dt^2 < 0$ means concave down. The curvature of the $x$-$t$ graph directly shows the sign of acceleration.

<Callout type="info">
**Reading Direction and Speed from an $x$-$t$ Graph**

- **Positive slope** = moving in the positive direction
- **Negative slope** = moving in the negative direction
- **Steep slope** = high speed
- **Gentle slope** = low speed
- **Horizontal** = zero velocity (momentarily or permanently at rest)
- **Slope getting steeper** = speeding up
- **Slope getting gentler** = slowing down
- **Slope changes sign** = direction of motion reverses (object bounces or turns around)
</Callout>

#### Common $x$-$t$ Shapes and Their Physical Meaning

**Parabola opening upward** ($x = ut + \tfrac{1}{2}at^2$ with $a > 0$, $u$ any sign):
Uniform acceleration in the positive direction.

**Parabola opening downward** ($a < 0$):
Uniform acceleration in the negative direction. This is a ball thrown upward — the $x$-$t$ graph is a downward parabola.

**Sine curve** ($x = A\sin(\omega t)$):
Simple harmonic motion — oscillating back and forth.

**Exponential approach** ($x = x_{max}(1 - e^{-t/\tau})$):
Object approaching a limit asymptotically (e.g., terminal velocity in a drag-retarded system).

#### Two Objects on the Same $x$-$t$ Graph

- **Intersection:** Both objects are at the **same position** at that instant. Not necessarily same velocity.
- **Parallel slopes at an instant:** Both objects have the **same velocity** at that instant.
- **Slope of one = slope of other at all times:** They always have the same velocity — moving at the same speed in the same direction (could be different positions).

<Callout type="warning">
**JEE Trap: Intersection ≠ same velocity**

Two $x$-$t$ curves crossing means the objects are at the same place simultaneously. But their velocities (tangent slopes) at that crossing point are generally different — one may be overtaking the other. The objects will have the same velocity only if their tangent slopes are equal at some point, which is a separate condition.
</Callout>

---

### 9.4 The Velocity-Time ($v$-$t$) Graph

The $v$-$t$ graph is the workhorse of JEE kinematics graphs. It directly shows velocity at every instant, acceleration from its slope, and displacement from its area.

#### Slope = Acceleration

$$a = \frac{dv}{dt} = \text{slope of } v\text{-}t \text{ graph}$$

- Steep positive slope: large positive acceleration
- Gentle positive slope: small positive acceleration
- Horizontal: zero acceleration (constant velocity)
- Negative slope: negative acceleration
- Kink (corner): discontinuous acceleration (abrupt change in force)

#### Area = Displacement (Signed)

$$\Delta x = \int_{t_1}^{t_2} v\,dt = \text{signed area under } v\text{-}t \text{ curve}$$

- Area **above** the time axis: positive displacement (in positive direction)
- Area **below** the time axis: negative displacement (in negative direction)
- **Total displacement** = sum of signed areas
- **Total distance** = sum of absolute areas (all areas treated as positive)

#### Common $v$-$t$ Shapes

| Shape | Motion type | Acceleration |
|---|---|---|
| Horizontal line | Constant velocity | $a = 0$ |
| Straight line, positive slope | Uniform acceleration (speeding up if $v > 0$) | Constant $a > 0$ |
| Straight line crossing t-axis | Direction reversal | $a$ = const, $v$ passes through 0 |
| Parabolic curve | Linearly varying acceleration | $a = a_0 + bt$ |
| Exponential decay | Drag force retardation | $a = -kv$ |

#### Computing Areas Geometrically

For piecewise-linear $v$-$t$ graphs (constant acceleration in each phase), compute areas as triangles, rectangles, and trapezoids:

- **Rectangle:** width $\Delta t$, height $v_0$ → area = $v_0 \Delta t$
- **Triangle** (velocity changes linearly from 0 to $v_{max}$): area = $\tfrac{1}{2}v_{max}\Delta t$
- **Trapezoid** (velocity changes from $v_1$ to $v_2$): area = $\tfrac{1}{2}(v_1+v_2)\Delta t$

<Callout type="tip">
**Signed vs Unsigned Area: The JEE Distinction**

If you are asked for **displacement**, sum the signed areas (subtract area below t-axis from area above).

If you are asked for **distance**, sum the unsigned areas (all positive, regardless of which side of the axis).

Never use unsigned area for displacement or signed area for distance — these are the two most common graph-reading errors.
</Callout>

---

### 9.5 The Acceleration-Time ($a$-$t$) Graph

Less common in JEE than the other two, but it appears — especially in JEE Advanced.

#### Area = Change in Velocity

$$\Delta v = \int_{t_1}^{t_2} a\,dt = \text{area under } a\text{-}t \text{ curve}$$

#### Slope = Jerk

$$j = \frac{da}{dt} = \text{slope of } a\text{-}t \text{ graph}$$

#### Deriving $v$-$t$ and $x$-$t$ from $a$-$t$

Given the $a$-$t$ graph:
1. The area from $0$ to $t$ gives $v(t) - v(0)$; add $v(0)$ to get $v(t)$.
2. The shape of the $v$-$t$ graph is determined by how $a$ varies:
   - $a$ = const $> 0$ → $v$ increases linearly ($v$-$t$ is a straight line with positive slope)
   - $a$ increases linearly → $v$ is a parabola opening upward
   - $a$ decreases to zero → $v$ increases but flattens out
   - $a$ oscillates → $v$ oscillates with a phase shift

---

### 9.6 Graph Transformations: The Complete Workflow

**From $x$-$t$ to $v$-$t$ and $a$-$t$:**

1. At each point of the $x$-$t$ curve, compute the tangent slope — this gives $v$ at that instant.
2. Plot $v$ vs $t$.
3. At each point of the $v$-$t$ curve, compute the tangent slope — this gives $a$ at that instant.
4. Plot $a$ vs $t$.

**From $a$-$t$ to $v$-$t$ and $x$-$t$:**

1. Start with $v_0$ (given initial condition).
2. Area under $a$-$t$ from 0 to $t$ gives $v(t) - v_0$; compute $v(t)$.
3. Plot the resulting $v$-$t$ curve.
4. Area under $v$-$t$ from 0 to $t$ gives $x(t) - x_0$; compute $x(t)$.
5. Plot the resulting $x$-$t$ curve.

**Key: the shape cascade:**

| $a$-$t$ shape | $v$-$t$ shape | $x$-$t$ shape |
|---|---|---|
| Horizontal line (const $a$) | Straight oblique line | Parabola |
| Straight oblique line (linear $a$) | Parabola | Cubic |
| Parabola (quadratic $a$) | Cubic | Quartic |
| Zero (horizontal at $a=0$) | Horizontal line | Straight oblique line |

Each graph is one "integration step" smoother than the one to its left. If $a$-$t$ has a corner (kink), $v$-$t$ has a slope change. If $a$-$t$ is discontinuous (jump), $v$-$t$ has a corner. $x$-$t$ is always one step smoother than $v$-$t$.

---

### 9.7 Multi-Phase $v$-$t$ Graphs: JEE Strategy

Many JEE problems give a piecewise $v$-$t$ graph and ask for various quantities. Here is the systematic approach:

**Step 1:** Label each phase (Phase 1, Phase 2, …) with its time interval.

**Step 2:** For each phase, read off: initial $v$, final $v$, time duration $\Delta t$.

**Step 3:** Compute the slope (= acceleration) in each phase: $a = (v_f - v_i)/\Delta t$.

**Step 4:** Compute the area (= displacement) in each phase using geometric formulas.

**Step 5:** Sum areas with signs for displacement; sum absolute values for distance.

**Step 6:** Identify direction reversals (where $v$ changes sign) — these mark where the object turns around.

---

### 9.8 Non-Standard Axes: The Advanced JEE Skill

JEE Advanced frequently presents graphs with non-standard axes — not $x$ vs $t$ or $v$ vs $t$, but rather $v^2$ vs $x$, $a$ vs $v$, $v$ vs $x$, or other combinations. The strategy: **linearise the kinematic equation** to match $y = mx + c$ form.

#### $v^2$ vs $x$ Graph (for constant $a$)

From $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$: plot $v^2$ ($y$-axis) vs $s = x - x_0$ ($x$-axis).

$$v^2 = \underbrace{2a}_{\text{slope}}\cdot x + \underbrace{u^2}_{\text{intercept}}$$

- Slope $= 2a$ → acceleration $= \tfrac{1}{2}\times\text{slope}$
- $y$-intercept $= u^2$ → initial speed $= \sqrt{\text{intercept}}$
- If line has positive slope: $a > 0$ (accelerating)
- If line has negative slope: $a < 0$ (decelerating)
- $x$-intercept: where $v = 0$, i.e., the stopping/turning point

<Callout type="warning">
**Factor of 2 trap:** The slope of the $v^2$-$x$ graph is $2a$, not $a$. Students routinely miss the factor of 2.
</Callout>

#### $a$ vs $v$ Graph

If $a = f(v)$ (velocity-dependent acceleration), plot $a$ ($y$-axis) vs $v$ ($x$-axis). The shape of this graph directly shows the force law:

- Linear: $a = c - kv$ → viscous drag plus constant force
- $a \propto v^2$: $a = -kv^2$ → quadratic drag
- $a = c$ (horizontal): constant acceleration (constant force)

#### $v$ vs $x$ Graph

From $v = dx/dt$: the slope of the $v$-$x$ graph is $dv/dx$. But $a = v\,dv/dx$, so:

$$a = v \times \text{slope of } v\text{-}x \text{ graph}$$

At any point on the $v$-$x$ graph with coordinates $(x_0, v_0)$ and slope $m = dv/dx$:
$$a = v_0 \cdot m$$

**Special case:** If the $v$-$x$ graph is a straight line through the origin: $v = kx$.
$$\frac{dv}{dx} = k \implies a = v\cdot k = kx\cdot k = k^2 x$$

This is SHM-like: acceleration proportional to position.

#### Area Under $a$-$s$ (acceleration vs displacement) Graph

$$\int_{x_1}^{x_2}a\,dx = \int_{x_1}^{x_2}v\,\frac{dv}{dx}\,dx = \int_{v_1}^{v_2}v\,dv = \frac{v_2^2 - v_1^2}{2}$$

**Area under the $a$-$s$ graph = change in $v^2/2$ = change in specific kinetic energy (per unit mass)**

---

### 9.9 The $x$-$t$ Graph and the Direction of Motion

A subtle but important analysis skill:

**Finding when the object is to the right/left of a reference point:** Just read the sign of $x(t)$.

**Finding when the object is moving right/left:** Read the sign of the slope of $x(t)$.

**Finding when the object is farthest right:** The maximum of $x(t)$, which occurs when $v = 0$ (slope = 0) and the object is about to move left.

**Finding when the object is at the origin:** Read where the $x$-$t$ curve crosses $x = 0$.

**Common confusion:** Students mix up "farthest from origin" with "farthest right." These are the same only if the object starts at the origin and moves rightward. In general: farthest from origin = maximum $|x(t)|$, not maximum $x(t)$.

---

### 9.10 Worked Examples

**Example 1:** A $v$-$t$ graph shows the following piecewise linear profile:
- $0 \leq t \leq 4$ s: $v$ increases from 0 to 8 m/s
- $4 \leq t \leq 8$ s: $v$ remains constant at 8 m/s
- $8 \leq t \leq 12$ s: $v$ decreases from 8 m/s to −4 m/s

Find: (a) acceleration in each phase, (b) displacement from $t=0$ to $t=12$ s, (c) distance from $t=0$ to $t=12$ s, (d) position at $t=12$ s if starts at $x=5$ m.

**(a):**
Phase 1: $a_1 = 8/4 = 2$ m/s²
Phase 2: $a_2 = 0$ m/s²
Phase 3: $a_3 = (-4-8)/4 = -3$ m/s²

**(b):** Areas (signed):
Phase 1 (triangle): $\tfrac{1}{2}(8)(4) = 16$ m
Phase 2 (rectangle): $8 \times 4 = 32$ m
Phase 3 (trapezoid, but $v$ crosses 0): Split at $t = t^*$ when $v = 0$.

$v = 8 - 3(t-8) = 0 \Rightarrow t^* - 8 = 8/3 \Rightarrow t^* = 8 + 8/3 = 32/3$ s.

Area from $t=8$ to $t=32/3$ (triangle, $v > 0$): $\tfrac{1}{2}\cdot(8/3)\cdot 8 = 32/3$ m
Area from $t=32/3$ to $t=12$ (triangle, $v < 0$): $-\tfrac{1}{2}\cdot(12-32/3)\cdot 4 = -\tfrac{1}{2}\cdot(4/3)\cdot 4 = -8/3$ m

Total displacement $= 16 + 32 + 32/3 - 8/3 = 48 + 8 = 56$ m

**(c):** Total distance $= 16 + 32 + 32/3 + 8/3 = 48 + 40/3 \approx 61.3$ m

**(d):** $x(12) = 5 + 56 = 61$ m.

---

**Example 2:** A $v^2$-$s$ graph is a straight line with slope $4$ m/s² per metre and $v^2$-intercept $= 36$ m²/s². Find: initial speed, acceleration, and the stopping distance if the object decelerates.

The slope of a $v^2$ vs $s$ graph is $2a$, so $a = 4/2 = 2$ m/s². Initial speed $= \sqrt{36} = 6$ m/s.

If instead this were a deceleration (slope negative $= -4$): $a = -2$ m/s². Stopping distance: $v^2 = 0 = 36 + (-4)s_{stop}$, so $s_{stop} = 9$ m.

---

**Example 3 (JEE Advanced graph reading):** An $x$-$t$ graph shows a particle at $x = 2$ m at $t = 0$, moving to a maximum of $x = 8$ m at $t = 3$ s, then returning to $x = 0$ m at $t = 7$ s. The motion is uniformly accelerated in each phase. Find acceleration in each phase and the speed at $t = 7$ s.

*Phase 1 ($0 \leq t \leq 3$ s):*
$s_1 = 8-2 = 6$ m, $u_1 = ?$, $v_{top} = 0$ (at turning point), $t_1 = 3$ s.
$v_{top} = u_1 + a_1(3) = 0 \Rightarrow u_1 = -3a_1$.
$s_1 = u_1(3) + \tfrac{1}{2}a_1(9) = -9a_1 + 4.5a_1 = -4.5a_1 = 6 \Rightarrow a_1 = -4/3$ m/s².
$u_1 = -3 \times (-4/3) = 4$ m/s.

*Phase 2 ($3 \leq t \leq 7$ s):*
Starts at $x = 8$ m with $v = 0$, ends at $x = 0$ m. Displacement $= -8$ m, time $= 4$ s.
$-8 = 0 + \tfrac{1}{2}a_2(16) \Rightarrow a_2 = -1$ m/s².
Speed at $t = 7$ s: $v = 0 + (-1)(4) = -4$ m/s → speed $= 4$ m/s.

---

### 9.11 The Conceptual Questions JEE Loves

**Q:** Can a $v$-$t$ graph have a vertical segment?

**A:** No — a vertical segment would mean infinite acceleration (infinite force), which is physically impossible.

**Q:** Can an $x$-$t$ graph loop back on itself?

**A:** No — at any given time, an object can only be at one position. The $x$-$t$ graph is a function $x(t)$; it passes the vertical line test. (An $x$-$y$ trajectory can loop; but $x$-$t$ cannot.)

**Q:** An object has a positive $x$ and negative $v$ on the $x$-$t$ graph. What does this mean?

**A:** The object is on the positive side of the origin (to the right of the reference point) but is moving in the negative direction (toward the origin or beyond).

**Q:** Two particles start at the same position. Particle A has a straight-line $v$-$t$ graph (positive, constant acceleration). Particle B has a concave-down $v$-$t$ graph (decreasing acceleration, always positive). After time $T$, which has the greater displacement?

**A:** B — for any given value of $v$ at time $T$, a concave-down curve has greater area underneath it than the straight line connecting the same endpoints. B reached higher speeds earlier, so its average velocity is higher, giving greater displacement.

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

- Three graphs: $x$-$t$, $v$-$t$, $a$-$t$ — related by: slope moves right (differentiation), area moves left (integration).
- On $x$-$t$: slope = velocity; concave up = positive acceleration; turning point = $v = 0$.
- On $v$-$t$: slope = acceleration; signed area = displacement; unsigned area = distance.
- On $a$-$t$: area = $\Delta v$.
- Shape cascade: each graph is one order smoother than the graph to its left.
- Non-standard axes: $v^2$-$s$ (slope = $2a$); $a$-$v$; $v$-$x$ (acceleration = $v \times$ slope); $a$-$s$ (area = $\Delta(v^2/2)$).
- Multi-phase piecewise graphs: label phases, compute slope and area in each, track direction reversals.
- $v$-$t$ graphs cannot have vertical segments; $x$-$t$ graphs cannot have loops.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 9 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 10: Motion in Two Dimensions.*

---

## SECTION 10 — Motion in Two Dimensions

---

### 10.1 The Big Step: From Lines to Planes

Everything in Sections 5–9 was restricted to one dimension — a single axis, one-component vectors, one equation of motion. The real world is three-dimensional, and even within a plane, motion involves two independent coordinates changing simultaneously.

Two-dimensional kinematics introduces exactly one new idea — and it is so powerful that it makes all 2D problems as easy as two independent 1D problems:

<Callout type="info">
**The Independence Principle (Galileo's Superposition Principle)**

The motion of an object in two dimensions can be decomposed into two completely independent one-dimensional motions — one along each coordinate axis — that share only the time variable $t$.

The x-motion does not know the y-motion exists, and vice versa.
</Callout>

This is not obvious. Before Galileo, people believed that throwing a ball harder horizontally would affect how quickly it fell. Galileo proved otherwise: horizontal and vertical motions are entirely independent — governed by separate kinematic equations, connected only by the shared clock.

---

### 10.2 Position, Velocity, and Acceleration as Vectors

In 2D, all kinematic quantities are 2-component vectors:

$$\vec{r}(t) = x(t)\,\hat{i} + y(t)\,\hat{j}$$

$$\vec{v}(t) = \frac{d\vec{r}}{dt} = \dot{x}\,\hat{i} + \dot{y}\,\hat{j} = v_x\,\hat{i} + v_y\,\hat{j}$$

$$\vec{a}(t) = \frac{d\vec{v}}{dt} = \ddot{x}\,\hat{i} + \ddot{y}\,\hat{j} = a_x\,\hat{i} + a_y\,\hat{j}$$

Each component obeys a completely separate 1D kinematics problem:

$$\text{x-axis:} \quad a_x = \frac{dv_x}{dt} \qquad v_x = \frac{dx}{dt}$$

$$\text{y-axis:} \quad a_y = \frac{dv_y}{dt} \qquad v_y = \frac{dy}{dt}$$

The bridge between x and y: **they use the same $t$**.

---

### 10.3 General 2D Motion with Constant Acceleration

For constant acceleration $\vec{a} = a_x\,\hat{i} + a_y\,\hat{j}$, the equations of motion apply to each component separately:

<Formula label="2D Equations of Motion (Constant Acceleration)">
$$v_x = u_x + a_x t \qquad\qquad v_y = u_y + a_y t$$

$$x = x_0 + u_x t + \tfrac{1}{2}a_x t^2 \qquad y = y_0 + u_y t + \tfrac{1}{2}a_y t^2$$

$$v_x^2 = u_x^2 + 2a_x(x-x_0) \qquad v_y^2 = u_y^2 + 2a_y(y-y_0)$$
</Formula>

The magnitudes and directions are recovered by:

$$v = |\vec{v}| = \sqrt{v_x^2 + v_y^2} \qquad \phi = \tan^{-1}\!\left(\frac{v_y}{v_x}\right)$$

$$r = |\vec{r}| = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \qquad \theta = \tan^{-1}\!\left(\frac{y}{x}\right)$$

---

### 10.4 The Trajectory Equation

The **trajectory** is the curve traced by the object in the $x$-$y$ plane, obtained by eliminating $t$ from the $x(t)$ and $y(t)$ equations.

**General procedure:**
1. From the x-equation, express $t$ in terms of $x$: $t = f(x)$.
2. Substitute into the y-equation to get $y = g(x)$.

The result $y = g(x)$ is the trajectory equation — a purely geometric relationship independent of time.

**Example — General constant acceleration:**

$$t = \frac{x - x_0 - u_x t}{u_x}$$

This becomes quadratic if $a_x \neq 0$, but for projectile motion where $a_x = 0$: $t = (x-x_0)/u_x$, which gives the clean parabola of Section 11.

---

### 10.5 Decomposing an Initial Velocity Vector

Given initial speed $v_0$ at angle $\theta$ above the positive x-axis:

$$u_x = v_0\cos\theta \qquad u_y = v_0\sin\theta$$

**For angles measured differently:**

| Description | $u_x$ | $u_y$ |
|---|---|---|
| $\theta$ above horizontal (standard) | $v_0\cos\theta$ | $v_0\sin\theta$ |
| $\theta$ below horizontal | $v_0\cos\theta$ | $-v_0\sin\theta$ |
| $\theta$ from vertical (elevation from top) | $v_0\sin\theta$ | $v_0\cos\theta$ |
| Due east (0°) | $v_0$ | $0$ |
| Due north (90°) | $0$ | $v_0$ |

Always draw a diagram and resolve carefully. Sign errors in the initial decomposition propagate through the entire problem.

---

### 10.6 The Velocity Vector in 2D: Direction and Magnitude

At any time $t$, the velocity vector $\vec{v}(t) = v_x\,\hat{i} + v_y\,\hat{j}$ has:

**Magnitude (instantaneous speed):**
$$v(t) = \sqrt{v_x(t)^2 + v_y(t)^2}$$

**Direction (angle with horizontal):**
$$\phi(t) = \tan^{-1}\!\left(\frac{v_y(t)}{v_x(t)}\right)$$

**Physical meaning of $\phi$:** The direction in which the object is moving at that instant — the tangent to the trajectory.

<Callout type="tip">
**Key insight:** The velocity direction changes continuously in 2D motion (whenever $a_y \neq 0$ or $a_x \neq 0$). The trajectory is not a straight line unless $\vec{a}$ is parallel to $\vec{v}$ at all times.
</Callout>

---

### 10.7 Acceleration Perpendicular to Velocity: Curved Paths

If at some instant $\vec{a}$ is perpendicular to $\vec{v}$:
$$\vec{a} \cdot \vec{v} = a_x v_x + a_y v_y = 0$$

Then $a_t = 0$ (no tangential acceleration) — the speed is momentarily constant. The acceleration is purely centripetal, curving the path without changing speed.

If $\vec{a}$ is parallel to $\vec{v}$: $a_c = 0$, speed changes but direction doesn't — the path is straight at that instant.

**Example:** At the peak of a projectile's trajectory, $v_y = 0$ so $\vec{v} = v_x\,\hat{i}$ (purely horizontal). The acceleration $\vec{a} = -g\,\hat{j}$ (purely vertical). They are perpendicular → no change in speed at the peak.

This confirms: the speed at the peak of a projectile is exactly $v_x = u\cos\theta$ — neither increasing nor decreasing at that instant.

---

### 10.8 Uniform vs Non-Uniform 2D Motion

| Condition | Motion type | Remarks |
|---|---|---|
| $\vec{a} = \vec{0}$ | Uniform (straight line at constant speed) | Both components constant |
| $\vec{a} \neq \vec{0}$, $\vec{a} \parallel \vec{v}$ | Non-uniform rectilinear | Speed changes, direction fixed |
| $\vec{a} \neq \vec{0}$, $\vec{a} \perp \vec{v}$, both constant magnitude | Uniform circular motion | Speed constant, direction changes |
| $\vec{a} \neq \vec{0}$, constant $\vec{a}$ not parallel to $\vec{v}$ | Parabolic (projectile) | Speed and direction both change |
| $\vec{a} \neq \vec{0}$, variable $\vec{a}$ | General curved motion | Anything goes |

---

### 10.9 Worked Example: General 2D Kinematics

**Problem:** A particle starts at $(1, 2)$ m with initial velocity $\vec{v}_0 = 3\,\hat{i} - 4\,\hat{j}$ m/s and constant acceleration $\vec{a} = 2\,\hat{i} + 2\,\hat{j}$ m/s².

(a) Find position and velocity at $t = 2$ s.
(b) Find the speed at $t = 2$ s.
(c) Find when $v_y = 0$.
(d) Find the trajectory equation $y(x)$.

**(a):**
$$x(2) = 1 + 3(2) + \tfrac{1}{2}(2)(4) = 1 + 6 + 4 = 11 \text{ m}$$
$$y(2) = 2 + (-4)(2) + \tfrac{1}{2}(2)(4) = 2 - 8 + 4 = -2 \text{ m}$$

$$v_x(2) = 3 + 2(2) = 7 \text{ m/s} \qquad v_y(2) = -4 + 2(2) = 0 \text{ m/s}$$

$$\vec{v}(2) = 7\,\hat{i} + 0\,\hat{j} = 7\,\hat{i} \text{ m/s (purely horizontal at } t = 2\text{ s)}$$

**(b):** Speed $= \sqrt{49+0} = 7$ m/s.

**(c):** $v_y = -4 + 2t = 0 \Rightarrow t = 2$ s (confirmed above).

**(d):**
$$x = 1 + 3t + t^2 \implies t^2 + 3t + (1-x) = 0 \implies t = \frac{-3 + \sqrt{9-4(1-x)}}{2} = \frac{-3+\sqrt{5+4x}}{2}$$

This is messy. Instead, from the parametric equations:

$x - 1 = 3t + t^2$ and $y - 2 = -4t + t^2$.

Subtract: $(x-1) - (y-2) = 3t + t^2 - (-4t + t^2) = 7t \Rightarrow t = \frac{x - y - 3}{7}$.

Sub back into $y - 2 = -4t + t^2$:

$$y - 2 = -4\cdot\frac{x-y-3}{7} + \left(\frac{x-y-3}{7}\right)^2$$

This gives an implicit quadratic relationship between $x$ and $y$ — the trajectory is a parabola in the $x$-$y$ plane.

---

### 10.10 The Constraint of Shared Time

The deepest idea in 2D kinematics: the x and y motions are independent in their accelerations and velocities, but **they must arrive at their respective positions at the same time**.

This is the key to solving all 2D problems:

1. Write $x(t)$ and $y(t)$ separately.
2. Use the condition(s) given to write equations in $t$.
3. Solve for $t$ from whichever equation gives it most easily.
4. Substitute into the other to find the remaining unknown.

**Example types that use this:**
- "When does the object reach height $H$?" → solve $y(t) = H$ for $t$, then find $x(t)$.
- "Where does the object land?" → solve $y(t) = 0$ for $t$, then find $x(t)$.
- "When do two objects meet?" → set $\vec{r}_A(t) = \vec{r}_B(t)$, which gives two equations (x and y) to solve simultaneously for $t$ and possibly other unknowns.

---

### 10.11 Relative Motion in 2D

If A moves with velocity $\vec{v}_A$ and B moves with velocity $\vec{v}_B$ (both in the ground frame):

$$\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B$$

The velocity of A relative to B is found by vector subtraction — in 2D this requires proper component subtraction, not just scalar subtraction.

<Callout type="tip">
**Vector subtraction in 2D:**

$$\vec{v}_{A/B} = (v_{Ax} - v_{Bx})\,\hat{i} + (v_{Ay} - v_{By})\,\hat{j}$$

Direction: $\tan^{-1}\!\left(\dfrac{v_{Ay}-v_{By}}{v_{Ax}-v_{Bx}}\right)$

Magnitude: $\sqrt{(v_{Ax}-v_{Bx})^2 + (v_{Ay}-v_{By})^2}$
</Callout>

**Worked example:** A car moves east at 60 km/h. Rain falls at 40 km/h vertically downward. Find the velocity of rain relative to the car, and the angle at which the driver must tilt their windshield wiper.

$$\vec{v}_{rain/car} = \vec{v}_{rain} - \vec{v}_{car} = (-40\,\hat{j}) - (60\,\hat{i}) = -60\,\hat{i} - 40\,\hat{j} \text{ km/h}$$

The rain appears to come from the front at an angle:
$$\tan\theta = \frac{40}{60} = \frac{2}{3} \implies \theta = \tan^{-1}(2/3) \approx 33.7° \text{ below the horizontal}$$

The driver tilts the wiper to make it perpendicular to this direction.

---

### 10.12 Two Objects Meeting in 2D

**Problem:** Object A starts at $(0, 0)$ with velocity $\vec{v}_A = 4\,\hat{i} + 3\,\hat{j}$ m/s. Object B starts at $(12, 0)$ with velocity $\vec{v}_B = -2\,\hat{i} + 5\,\hat{j}$ m/s. Do they meet? If so, when and where?

For meeting: $\vec{r}_A(t) = \vec{r}_B(t)$:

x: $4t = 12 - 2t \Rightarrow 6t = 12 \Rightarrow t = 2$ s

y: $3t = 5t \Rightarrow 2t = 0 \Rightarrow t = 0$ s

The x-equation gives $t = 2$ s, but the y-equation gives $t = 0$ s — there is no single time when both conditions are satisfied simultaneously. **The objects do not meet.**

But: the closest approach occurs when the rate of change of their separation is zero. Define $\vec{d} = \vec{r}_A - \vec{r}_B = (4t-12+2t)\,\hat{i} + (3t-5t)\,\hat{j} = (6t-12)\,\hat{i} - 2t\,\hat{j}$.

$$|\vec{d}|^2 = (6t-12)^2 + 4t^2 = 36t^2 - 144t + 144 + 4t^2 = 40t^2 - 144t + 144$$

Minimum when $\frac{d}{dt}|\vec{d}|^2 = 0$: $80t - 144 = 0 \Rightarrow t = 1.8$ s.

$$|\vec{d}|_{min} = \sqrt{40(3.24) - 144(1.8) + 144} = \sqrt{129.6 - 259.2 + 144} = \sqrt{14.4} \approx 3.79 \text{ m}$$

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

- 2D motion = two independent 1D motions sharing the same time $t$.
- All vector equations apply component-by-component; $t$ is the bridge between components.
- For constant $\vec{a}$: apply the four equations of motion separately to x and y components.
- Trajectory = curve in $x$-$y$ plane obtained by eliminating $t$ from $x(t)$ and $y(t)$.
- Speed $= \sqrt{v_x^2+v_y^2}$; direction $= \tan^{-1}(v_y/v_x)$.
- At peak of projectile: $\vec{v} \perp \vec{a}$ → speed momentarily not changing.
- Relative velocity in 2D: $\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B$ with component subtraction.
- Two objects meet when both x and y conditions are satisfied at the same $t$.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 10 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 11: Projectile Motion.*

---

## SECTION 11 — Projectile Motion

---

### 11.1 What Is a Projectile?

A **projectile** is any object that is given an initial velocity and then moves solely under the influence of gravity — no thrust, no air resistance, no other force. After launch, the only acceleration acting on it is $\vec{g}$ = vertically downward.

This is an idealisation — in reality, air resistance acts on every moving object. But for dense, slow-moving objects over short distances, the idealisation is excellent. A cricket ball, a cannonball, a shot-put: all are well-described by projectile motion.

The remarkable fact, established by Galileo and flowing directly from the Independence Principle:

> **Horizontal and vertical motions are completely decoupled. The horizontal motion proceeds at constant velocity; the vertical motion is free fall.**

---

### 11.2 Standard Setup

**Coordinate system:** Origin at the launch point. x-axis horizontal (in the direction of the horizontal component of launch velocity). y-axis vertically upward.

**Initial conditions:** Speed $u$, angle $\theta$ above the horizontal.

$$u_x = u\cos\theta \qquad u_y = u\sin\theta$$

**Accelerations:**

$$a_x = 0 \qquad a_y = -g$$

---

### 11.3 Equations of Motion

Applying Section 10's framework:

<Formula label="Projectile Position">
$$x(t) = u\cos\theta\cdot t \qquad y(t) = u\sin\theta\cdot t - \tfrac{1}{2}gt^2$$
</Formula>

<Formula label="Projectile Velocity">
$$v_x(t) = u\cos\theta \quad \text{(constant)} \qquad v_y(t) = u\sin\theta - gt$$
</Formula>

**Speed at any time:**

$$v(t) = \sqrt{v_x^2 + v_y^2} = \sqrt{u^2\cos^2\theta + (u\sin\theta - gt)^2}$$

**Direction of velocity at time $t$:**

$$\phi(t) = \tan^{-1}\!\left(\frac{u\sin\theta - gt}{u\cos\theta}\right)$$

---

### 11.4 The Four Key Quantities

#### Time to Reach Maximum Height

At the peak, $v_y = 0$:

$$u\sin\theta - gt_{top} = 0 \implies t_{top} = \frac{u\sin\theta}{g}$$

#### Maximum Height

$$H = y(t_{top}) = u\sin\theta\cdot\frac{u\sin\theta}{g} - \frac{1}{2}g\left(\frac{u\sin\theta}{g}\right)^2$$

<Formula label="Maximum Height">
$$\boxed{H = \frac{u^2\sin^2\theta}{2g}}$$
</Formula>

#### Time of Flight (same horizontal level)

Set $y(T) = 0$: $u\sin\theta\cdot T - \tfrac{1}{2}gT^2 = 0 \implies T(u\sin\theta - \tfrac{g}{2}T) = 0$.

Discard $T = 0$ (launch):

<Formula label="Time of Flight">
$$\boxed{T = \frac{2u\sin\theta}{g} = 2t_{top}}$$
</Formula>

The time of flight is exactly twice the time to reach the peak — a consequence of the time-symmetry of free fall.

#### Horizontal Range

$$R = x(T) = u\cos\theta\cdot T = u\cos\theta\cdot\frac{2u\sin\theta}{g}$$

<Formula label="Horizontal Range">
$$\boxed{R = \frac{u^2\sin 2\theta}{g}}$$
</Formula>

using $2\sin\theta\cos\theta = \sin 2\theta$.

---

### 11.5 Maximum Range

$R = \frac{u^2\sin 2\theta}{g}$ is maximised when $\sin 2\theta = 1$, i.e., $2\theta = 90°$, i.e., $\theta = 45°$:

<Formula label="Maximum Range">
$$\boxed{R_{max} = \frac{u^2}{g} \quad \text{at } \theta = 45°}$$
</Formula>

**At $\theta = 45°$:** $H = u^2/(4g) = R_{max}/4$. Maximum height is one-quarter of the maximum range.

---

### 11.6 Equation of the Trajectory

Eliminate $t$ using $t = x/(u\cos\theta)$:

$$y = u\sin\theta\cdot\frac{x}{u\cos\theta} - \frac{1}{2}g\cdot\frac{x^2}{u^2\cos^2\theta}$$

<Formula label="Trajectory Equation">
$$\boxed{y = x\tan\theta - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2\cos^2\theta}}$$
</Formula>

This has the form $y = Ax - Bx^2$ — a **downward-opening parabola** (since $B > 0$).

**Vertex** (maximum height) occurs at $x = A/(2B) = u^2\sin\theta\cos\theta/g = R/2$. The peak is directly above the horizontal midpoint of the range.

**Alternative form using $\sec^2\theta = 1 + \tan^2\theta$:**

$$y = x\tan\theta - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2}(1+\tan^2\theta)$$

If $\tan\theta$ is given directly (as it sometimes is in JEE problems), this form allows you to substitute without converting to $\theta$.

---

### 11.7 Symmetry Properties

#### Complementary Angles

For two launch angles $\theta$ and $(90° - \theta)$, the range is the same:

$$R(\theta) = \frac{u^2\sin 2\theta}{g} = \frac{u^2\sin(180°-2\theta)}{g} = R(90°-\theta)$$

But the other quantities differ:

| Quantity | $\theta$ | $90°-\theta$ |
|---|---|---|
| Time of flight | $T_1 = \frac{2u\sin\theta}{g}$ | $T_2 = \frac{2u\cos\theta}{g}$ |
| Max height | $H_1 = \frac{u^2\sin^2\theta}{2g}$ | $H_2 = \frac{u^2\cos^2\theta}{2g}$ |
| Product $T_1 T_2$ | $\dfrac{2R}{g}$ | $\dfrac{2R}{g}$ |
| Sum $H_1+H_2$ | $\dfrac{R_{\max}}{2}$ | $\dfrac{R_{\max}}{2}$ |

<Callout type="tip">
**JEE Standard Results for Complementary Angles**

Given same range for angles $\theta$ and $(90°-\theta)$:

$$T_1 T_2 = \frac{2R}{g} \qquad H_1 + H_2 = \frac{u^2}{2g} = \frac{R_{max}}{2}$$

$$\frac{H_1}{H_2} = \tan^2\theta \qquad \frac{T_1}{T_2} = \tan\theta$$

These are direct JEE results — memorise them and know which direction the inequality goes ($H_1 > H_2$ when $\theta > 45°$).
</Callout>

#### Velocity Symmetry

- At any height $h$ above the launch point, the speed is the same on the way up and on the way down: $v = \sqrt{u^2 - 2gh}$.
- On the way down, $v_y$ has the same magnitude but opposite sign as on the way up at the same height.
- The projectile lands with the same speed $u$ it was launched with (if landing at the same height).

---

### 11.8 Velocity at Any Point: Angle and Magnitude

**Speed at height $h$** (using vertical energy-like relation $v_y^2 = u_y^2 - 2gh$):

$$v = \sqrt{v_x^2 + v_y^2} = \sqrt{u^2\cos^2\theta + u^2\sin^2\theta - 2gh} = \sqrt{u^2 - 2gh}$$

Speed depends only on height, not on horizontal position.

**Angle of velocity with horizontal at height $h$:**

$$v_y = \pm\sqrt{u^2\sin^2\theta - 2gh} \quad (\text{+ on way up, – on way down})$$

$$\tan\phi = \frac{v_y}{v_x} = \frac{\pm\sqrt{u^2\sin^2\theta - 2gh}}{u\cos\theta}$$

---

### 11.9 Projectile Launched from a Height

A projectile is launched horizontally (or at angle $\theta$) from a height $H$ above the ground. The landing is now at $y = -H$ (taking launch point as origin).

**For horizontal launch** ($\theta = 0$, $u_y = 0$):

$$y = -\frac{1}{2}gt^2 = -H \implies t_{land} = \sqrt{\frac{2H}{g}}$$

$$R = u\cos\theta\cdot t_{land} = u\sqrt{\frac{2H}{g}}$$

$$v_{land} = \sqrt{u^2 + 2gH}$$

**Direction on landing:**

$$\tan\alpha = \frac{gt_{land}}{u} = \frac{g\sqrt{2H/g}}{u} = \frac{\sqrt{2gH}}{u}$$

---

### 11.10 Projectile on an Inclined Plane

A projectile is launched from the base of a slope inclined at angle $\beta$ to the horizontal. The initial velocity makes angle $\alpha$ with the slope (or angle $\alpha + \beta$ with the horizontal).

**Two approaches:**

#### Approach 1: Standard axes (horizontal and vertical)

Use the standard equations with $a_x = 0$, $a_y = -g$, and impose the landing condition: the projectile lands on the slope when $y/x = \tan\beta$.

#### Approach 2: Axes along and perpendicular to the slope

Set x-axis along the slope (positive upslope), y-axis perpendicular to the slope.

$$u_x = u\cos\alpha \qquad u_y = u\sin\alpha$$
$$a_x = -g\sin\beta \quad \text{(gravity component along slope, opposing upslope motion)}$$
$$a_y = -g\cos\beta \quad \text{(gravity component perpendicular to slope, into the slope)}$$

The projectile lands when $y = 0$ again:

$$0 = u\sin\alpha\cdot T - \tfrac{1}{2}g\cos\beta\cdot T^2 \implies T = \frac{2u\sin\alpha}{g\cos\beta}$$

Range along the slope:

$$R_{slope} = u\cos\alpha\cdot T - \tfrac{1}{2}g\sin\beta\cdot T^2$$

$$= \frac{2u^2\sin\alpha\cos\alpha}{g\cos\beta} - \frac{g\sin\beta}{2}\cdot\frac{4u^2\sin^2\alpha}{g^2\cos^2\beta}$$

$$= \frac{2u^2\sin\alpha}{g\cos^2\beta}(\cos\alpha\cos\beta - \sin\alpha\sin\beta) = \frac{2u^2\sin\alpha\cos(\alpha+\beta)}{g\cos^2\beta}$$

<Formula label="Range on Inclined Plane">
$$\boxed{R_{slope} = \frac{2u^2\sin\alpha\cos(\alpha+\beta)}{g\cos^2\beta}}$$
</Formula>

Using $2\sin A\cos B = \sin(A+B) + \sin(A-B)$:

$$R_{slope} = \frac{u^2}{g\cos^2\beta}[\sin(2\alpha+\beta) - \sin\beta]$$

**Maximum range on incline:** Maximise w.r.t. $\alpha$: $\sin(2\alpha+\beta) = 1 \Rightarrow 2\alpha+\beta = 90° \Rightarrow \alpha = 45° - \beta/2$.

<Formula label="Maximum Range on Inclined Plane">
$$\boxed{R_{slope,max} = \frac{u^2}{g(1+\sin\beta)}}$$
</Formula>

The optimal launch angle from the horizontal is $\theta = \alpha + \beta = 45° + \beta/2$ — always halfway between the incline and the vertical.

<Callout type="tip">
**Downward incline:** Replace $\beta$ with $-\beta$ throughout. The maximum range on a downward slope is $u^2/[g(1-\sin\beta)]$, which is greater than on flat ground. Throwing downhill (with the optimal angle) gives longer range than $45°$ on flat ground.
</Callout>

---

### 11.11 Projectile from a Moving Platform

A ball is launched from a vehicle moving at velocity $\vec{v}_0$ (horizontal). The ball is given additional velocity $u$ at angle $\theta$ relative to the vehicle.

**Initial velocity in ground frame:**

$$u_x = v_0 + u\cos\theta \qquad u_y = u\sin\theta$$

The equations of motion in the ground frame use these values. The trajectory in the ground frame is a different parabola than in the vehicle's frame.

**Key insight:** The trajectory depends on the frame of reference. In the vehicle's frame, the ball goes straight up and comes straight down (if $\theta = 90°$). In the ground frame, it traces a parabola. Both descriptions are correct.

**Time of flight** is the same in both frames (time is absolute in classical mechanics). Range as measured from the vehicle's launch point (in the ground frame) equals range in the vehicle's frame — but the absolute ground position of landing differs.

---

### 11.12 Radius of Curvature in Projectile Motion

From Section 7.11, the radius of curvature at any point is:

$$\rho = \frac{v^2}{a_\perp} = \frac{v^2}{g\cos\phi}$$

where $\phi$ is the angle the velocity makes with the horizontal, and $g\cos\phi$ is the component of $g$ perpendicular to the velocity.

<Formula label="Radius of Curvature at Angle φ">
$$\rho = \frac{v^2}{g\cos\phi} = \frac{(u^2-2gh)}{g\cos\phi}$$
</Formula>

**At the top** ($\phi = 0$, $v = u\cos\theta$):

$$\rho_{top} = \frac{(u\cos\theta)^2}{g} = \frac{u^2\cos^2\theta}{g}$$

**At launch** ($\phi = \theta$, $v = u$):

$$\rho_{launch} = \frac{u^2}{g\cos\theta}$$

Since $\cos\theta < 1$ for $\theta > 0$: $\rho_{launch} > \rho_{top}$. The trajectory is more tightly curved at the top (smaller $\rho$) than at launch.

**At $\theta = 45°$:** $\rho_{top} = u^2/(2g) = R_{max}/2$ and $\rho_{launch} = u^2\sqrt{2}/g$.

---

### 11.13 JEE-Level Worked Examples

**Example 1 (JEE Main standard):** A ball is thrown at $30°$ above horizontal with speed $20$ m/s. Find: (a) max height, (b) time of flight, (c) range, (d) speed and direction of velocity at $t = 1$ s. Take $g = 10$ m/s².

**(a):** $H = \dfrac{(20)^2\sin^2 30°}{2(10)} = \dfrac{400 \times 0.25}{20} = 5$ m

**(b):** $T = \dfrac{2 \times 20 \times \sin 30°}{10} = \dfrac{20}{10} = 2$ s

**(c):** $R = \dfrac{400\sin 60°}{10} = 40 \times \dfrac{\sqrt{3}}{2} = 20\sqrt{3} \approx 34.6$ m

**(d):**
$v_x = 20\cos 30° = 10\sqrt{3}$ m/s
$v_y = 20\sin 30° - 10(1) = 10 - 10 = 0$ m/s

Speed $= 10\sqrt{3}$ m/s, direction: horizontal. (At $t = 1$ s the ball is at the top of its arc.)

---

**Example 2 (Complementary angles):** Two balls are thrown with the same speed $u = 20$ m/s, one at $30°$ and one at $60°$. Show they have the same range, and find the ratio of their heights and times of flight.

Range: $R_{30°} = \dfrac{400\sin60°}{10} = 20\sqrt{3}$ m $= R_{60°}$ ✓

$H_{30°} = \dfrac{400\sin^230°}{20} = \dfrac{400/4}{20} = 5$ m

$H_{60°} = \dfrac{400\sin^260°}{20} = \dfrac{400\times3/4}{20} = 15$ m

$H_{30°}/H_{60°} = 1/3$ (equals $\tan^230°/\tan^260° = (1/\sqrt{3})^2/(\sqrt{3})^2 = 1/3$ ✓)

$T_{30°} = \dfrac{2\times20\times0.5}{10} = 2$ s; $T_{60°} = \dfrac{2\times20\times\sqrt{3}/2}{10} = 2\sqrt{3}$ s

$T_{30°}/T_{60°} = 1/\sqrt{3} = \tan30°$ ✓

---

**Example 3 (JEE Advanced: find $u$ and $\theta$ from two conditions):** A ball is projected such that its range is $80$ m and maximum height is $20$ m. Find $u$ and $\theta$.

From $R = u^2\sin2\theta/g$ and $H = u^2\sin^2\theta/(2g)$:

$$\frac{R}{4H} = \frac{u^2\sin2\theta/g}{4\cdot u^2\sin^2\theta/(2g)} = \frac{2\sin\theta\cos\theta}{\cdot 2\sin^2\theta} = \frac{\cos\theta}{\sin\theta} = \cot\theta$$

$$\frac{80}{4\times20} = \cot\theta \implies \cot\theta = 1 \implies \theta = 45°$$

From $H = u^2\sin^245°/(2g) = u^2/(4g) = 20$:
$$u^2 = 80g = 800 \implies u = 20\sqrt{2} \approx 28.3 \text{ m/s}$$

---

**Example 4 (Inclined plane, JEE Advanced):** A ball is projected from the bottom of a $30°$ incline at angle $30°$ above the incline. Speed $= 20$ m/s. Find the range along the incline.

$u = 20$ m/s, $\alpha = 30°$ (above incline), $\beta = 30°$ (incline angle), $g = 10$ m/s².

$$R_{slope} = \frac{2u^2\sin\alpha\cos(\alpha+\beta)}{g\cos^2\beta} = \frac{2(400)\sin30°\cos60°}{10\cos^230°}$$

$$= \frac{800\times0.5\times0.5}{10\times0.75} = \frac{200}{7.5} = \frac{80}{3} \approx 26.7 \text{ m}$$

---

**Example 5 (Radius of curvature, JEE Advanced):** For a projectile with $u = 20$ m/s, $\theta = 60°$, find the radius of curvature at the top of the trajectory.

$$\rho_{top} = \frac{(u\cos\theta)^2}{g} = \frac{(20\cos60°)^2}{10} = \frac{(10)^2}{10} = 10 \text{ m}$$

---

### 11.14 Common JEE Traps in Projectile Motion

<Callout type="warning">
**Trap 1:** Using $v^2 = u^2 - 2gH$ when the ball is launched at an angle. This gives speed at height $H$ correctly, but only if you remember $v$ here is the total speed (both components), not just the vertical.
</Callout>

<Callout type="warning">
**Trap 2:** Claiming the range is maximum at $45°$ when the launch and landing heights differ. The $45°$ result is only valid when the projectile lands at the same height as it was launched. For uphill or downhill situations, the optimal angle changes.
</Callout>

<Callout type="warning">
**Trap 3:** Forgetting that $T = 2u\sin\theta/g$ requires the projectile to return to the same height. If it lands on a different level, use $y(T) = -H_{drop}$ to find the actual time of flight.
</Callout>

<Callout type="warning">
**Trap 4:** In "two balls thrown with same speed at complementary angles" problems, the ranges are equal but everything else (heights, times, speeds at intermediate points) differs. Don't over-generalise.
</Callout>

<Callout type="warning">
**Trap 5:** The trajectory equation $y = x\tan\theta - gx^2/(2u^2\cos^2\theta)$ is derived from $x = u\cos\theta\cdot t$, which assumes $a_x = 0$. If there is horizontal acceleration (e.g., ball in a wind, or ball launched from accelerating vehicle), this derivation fails.
</Callout>

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

Standard setup: $u_x = u\cos\theta$, $u_y = u\sin\theta$, $a_x = 0$, $a_y = -g$.

Key results:
$$H = \frac{u^2\sin^2\theta}{2g} \qquad T = \frac{2u\sin\theta}{g} \qquad R = \frac{u^2\sin2\theta}{g} \qquad R_{max} = \frac{u^2}{g} \text{ at } 45°$$

Trajectory: $y = x\tan\theta - \dfrac{gx^2}{2u^2\cos^2\theta}$ — a downward parabola.

Complementary angles $\theta$ and $90°-\theta$: same range; $H_1/H_2 = \tan^2\theta$; $T_1/T_2 = \tan\theta$.

Speed at height $h$: $v = \sqrt{u^2 - 2gh}$ — depends only on height.

On inclined plane ($\beta$ = slope angle, $\alpha$ = angle above slope): $R_{slope} = 2u^2\sin\alpha\cos(\alpha+\beta)/(g\cos^2\beta)$; maximum at $\alpha = 45°-\beta/2$.

Radius of curvature at top: $\rho_{top} = u^2\cos^2\theta/g$ (minimum, tightest curve at peak).
</Callout>

---

*— Section 11 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 12: Relative Motion.*

---

## SECTION 12 — Relative Motion

---

### 12.1 The Core Idea

Every velocity you have measured so far has been relative to the ground. But the ground is just one observer among infinitely many possible ones. A passenger on a train, a pilot in a plane, a swimmer in a river — each sees the world differently.

Relative motion gives us the mathematical tools to convert between these descriptions and, more importantly, to solve problems that are far simpler to set up in one frame than another.

<Callout type="info">
**Relative Velocity**
