If A moves with velocity $\vec{v}_A$ and B moves with velocity $\vec{v}_B$ (both measured in the same inertial frame), then the **velocity of A as seen by B** is:

$$\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B$$

Read: "subtract the observer's velocity from the observed object's velocity."

**Corollaries:**
- $\vec{v}_{B/A} = -\vec{v}_{A/B}$
- $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ (velocity addition rule)
- In the frame of B, B is at rest and A moves with velocity $\vec{v}_{A/B}$.
</Callout>

---

### 12.2 Relative Acceleration

$$\vec{a}_{A/B} = \vec{a}_A - \vec{a}_B$$

If both A and B have the same acceleration (e.g., both in free fall), $\vec{a}_{A/B} = 0$ — in each other's frames, they are in uniform relative motion (no acceleration). This is why astronauts in orbit appear to float: everything in the space station falls with the same acceleration $g$, so their relative acceleration is zero.

---

### 12.3 The River-Boat Problem: The Canonical Relative Motion Setup

A boat can move at speed $v_b$ relative to the water (its own engine speed). The river flows at speed $v_r$ relative to the bank, in one direction (say, the positive x-direction).

The boat's velocity relative to the bank:

$$\vec{v}_{boat/bank} = \vec{v}_{boat/water} + \vec{v}_{water/bank}$$

If the boat aims at angle $\alpha$ from the direction perpendicular to the river (i.e., $\alpha$ upstream):

$$\vec{v}_{boat/bank} = (-v_b\sin\alpha)\,\hat{i} + (v_b\cos\alpha)\,\hat{j} + v_r\,\hat{i} = (v_r - v_b\sin\alpha)\,\hat{i} + (v_b\cos\alpha)\,\hat{j}$$

where $\hat{j}$ points across the river and $\hat{i}$ points downstream.

#### Case A: Shortest Time to Cross

To minimise crossing time, maximise the component of velocity perpendicular to the river ($v_b\cos\alpha$). This is maximised when $\alpha = 0$ — boat aims directly perpendicular to the bank.

<Formula label="Minimum Crossing Time">
$$t_{min} = \frac{d}{v_b}$$
</Formula>

where $d$ is the river width. The boat drifts downstream by:

$$x_{drift} = v_r \cdot t_{min} = \frac{v_r d}{v_b}$$

The boat does **not** land directly opposite — it lands downstream.

#### Case B: Shortest Path (Land Directly Opposite)

Aim the boat upstream at angle $\alpha$ such that the horizontal components cancel:

$$v_r - v_b\sin\alpha = 0 \implies \sin\alpha = \frac{v_r}{v_b}$$

This requires $v_b > v_r$ (boat faster than current). Then:

$$\vec{v}_{boat/bank} = v_b\cos\alpha\,\hat{j} = \sqrt{v_b^2 - v_r^2}\,\hat{j} \quad\text{(purely perpendicular)}$$

<Formula label="Crossing Time for Shortest Path">
$$t_{path} = \frac{d}{\sqrt{v_b^2 - v_r^2}}$$
</Formula>

The boat lands directly opposite. This takes longer than $t_{min}$ (since $\sqrt{v_b^2-v_r^2} < v_b$).

#### Case C: $v_b < v_r$ — Boat Cannot Go Straight Across

When the river is faster than the boat, the boat can never aim directly opposite — it will always be swept downstream. The best it can do is minimise downstream drift by choosing the angle that makes $\vec{v}_{boat/bank}$ as perpendicular to the bank as possible.

Downstream drift $= (v_r - v_b\sin\alpha)\cdot d/(v_b\cos\alpha)$. Minimise over $\alpha$:

$$\frac{d}{d\alpha}\left[\frac{v_r - v_b\sin\alpha}{v_b\cos\alpha}\right] = 0 \implies \sin\alpha = \frac{v_b}{v_r}$$

This gives the angle for minimum drift when $v_b < v_r$.

<Callout type="tip">
**Three-Case River Summary**

| Condition | Goal | Boat angle | Crossing time |
|---|---|---|---|
| Any $v_b, v_r$ | Minimum time | $\perp$ to bank ($\alpha = 0$) | $d/v_b$ |
| $v_b > v_r$ | Zero drift (go straight) | $\sin\alpha = v_r/v_b$ upstream | $d/\sqrt{v_b^2-v_r^2}$ |
| $v_b < v_r$ | Minimum drift | $\sin\alpha = v_b/v_r$ upstream | Longer than $d/v_b$ |

When $v_b = v_r$ and you aim for minimum drift: $\sin\alpha = 1 \Rightarrow \alpha = 90°$ (aim directly upstream). The boat makes no forward progress and drifts at 45° from the bank.
</Callout>

---

### 12.4 The Rain-Person Problem

Rain falls at speed $v_R$ in the vertical direction (downward). A person walks at speed $v_P$ horizontally.

**Velocity of rain relative to the person:**

$$\vec{v}_{rain/person} = \vec{v}_{rain} - \vec{v}_{person} = (-v_R\,\hat{j}) - (v_P\,\hat{i}) = -v_P\,\hat{i} - v_R\,\hat{j}$$

The rain appears to come from **in front and above** — from the direction of motion. This is why cyclists feel rain in the face even if it's falling vertically.

**Angle from vertical** (angle the apparent rain direction makes with vertical):

$$\tan\theta = \frac{v_P}{v_R}$$

The person must tilt the umbrella forward at angle $\theta$ from vertical to stay dry.

**Speed of apparent rain:**

$$v_{rain/person} = \sqrt{v_P^2 + v_R^2}$$

**If the person runs faster:** The apparent rain angle increases further forward, and the apparent rain speed increases. The rain hits the body harder.

<Callout type="warning">
**JEE Trap:** "A person walks faster — does the rain hit them more or less?"

More, in terms of rate of drops hitting the front. Less, in terms of total drops hitting top of the head per unit time (you spend less time in the rain). These are different questions — read carefully.
</Callout>

---

### 12.5 Aircraft in Wind: The Crosswind Problem

An aircraft has airspeed $v_a$ (speed relative to air). The wind blows at speed $v_w$ (speed relative to ground). The aircraft's velocity relative to the ground:

$$\vec{v}_{aircraft/ground} = \vec{v}_{aircraft/air} + \vec{v}_{air/ground}$$

**To fly straight to a destination** despite crosswind: aim the aircraft upwind at angle $\alpha$ from the desired direction, where $\sin\alpha = v_w/v_a$ (requires $v_a > v_w$).

The resulting ground speed is $\sqrt{v_a^2 - v_w^2}$.

This is mathematically identical to the river-boat problem with minimum drift: the aircraft is the "boat," the air is the "river."

---

### 12.6 Two Objects Moving Toward Each Other

If A and B move toward each other with speeds $v_A$ and $v_B$ (along the same line):

**Relative speed of approach** = $v_A + v_B$

Time to meet = initial separation / relative speed = $d/(v_A + v_B)$

**Moving in the same direction:**

**Relative speed** = $|v_A - v_B|$

If $v_A > v_B$: A catches up; time to catch = gap / (v_A - v_B)

---

### 12.7 Minimum Distance Between Two Moving Objects

Given two objects with initial positions and velocities, the minimum distance between them during their motion is one of the most useful relative motion results in JEE.

**Method:**

1. In the frame of object B, B is stationary. A moves with relative velocity $\vec{v}_{A/B}$.
2. The minimum distance is the **perpendicular distance** from B (at the origin in B's frame) to the straight-line path of A.

**Derivation:**

At $t = 0$: separation $\vec{d}_0 = \vec{r}_A(0) - \vec{r}_B(0)$. Relative velocity $\vec{v}_{rel} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B$.

Separation at time $t$: $\vec{d}(t) = \vec{d}_0 + \vec{v}_{rel}\cdot t$

$|\vec{d}(t)|^2 = |\vec{d}_0|^2 + 2(\vec{d}_0\cdot\vec{v}_{rel})t + |\vec{v}_{rel}|^2 t^2$

Minimum when $\dfrac{d}{dt}|\vec{d}|^2 = 0$:

$$t^* = -\frac{\vec{d}_0\cdot\vec{v}_{rel}}{|\vec{v}_{rel}|^2}$$

If $t^* < 0$: the objects are already moving apart; minimum distance is at $t = 0$.

If $t^* > 0$: minimum distance occurs at $t = t^*$.

<Formula label="Minimum Distance Between Two Moving Objects">
$$d_{min} = \frac{|\vec{d}_0 \times \vec{v}_{rel}|}{|\vec{v}_{rel}|}$$
</Formula>

This is the magnitude of the cross product of the initial separation with the relative velocity unit vector — geometrically, the perpendicular distance from the origin to the line of relative motion.

**In 2D:**

$$d_{min} = \frac{|d_{0x}v_{rel,y} - d_{0y}v_{rel,x}|}{|\vec{v}_{rel}|}$$

---

### 12.8 Worked Examples

**Example 1 (River-boat):** A river is 120 m wide. A boat has speed 5 m/s in still water. The river current is 3 m/s. Find: (a) minimum time to cross, (b) drift in (a), (c) angle to aim for zero drift.

**(a):** $t_{min} = d/v_b = 120/5 = 24$ s.

**(b):** Drift $= v_r \cdot t = 3 \times 24 = 72$ m downstream.

**(c):** $\sin\alpha = v_r/v_b = 3/5 \Rightarrow \alpha = 37°$ upstream from perpendicular (i.e., aim at $37°$ upstream of directly across).

Resultant speed $= \sqrt{25-9} = 4$ m/s. Crossing time $= 120/4 = 30$ s.

---

**Example 2 (Rain-man):** Rain falls vertically at 4 m/s. A man walks at 3 m/s. Find: (a) velocity of rain relative to man, (b) angle umbrella must make, (c) speed of apparent rain.

**(a):** $\vec{v}_{rain/man} = -3\,\hat{i} - 4\,\hat{j}$ m/s (forward and downward).

**(b):** $\tan\theta = 3/4 \Rightarrow \theta = 37°$ from vertical (forward).

**(c):** $v = \sqrt{9+16} = 5$ m/s.

---

**Example 3 (Aircraft):** A plane's airspeed is 200 km/h. Wind blows at 100 km/h from the east. The plane wants to fly due north. Find: (a) heading angle, (b) ground speed, (c) time to cover 300 km northward.

Plane aims at angle $\alpha$ west of north to compensate for eastward wind.

$\vec{v}_{plane/ground} = (-200\sin\alpha + (-100))\,\hat{i} + (200\cos\alpha)\,\hat{j}$

Note "wind from the east" means the wind blows westward: $\vec{v}_{wind} = -100\,\hat{i}$ km/h.

$\vec{v}_{plane/ground} = \vec{v}_{plane/air} + \vec{v}_{air/ground}$

For northward motion ($x$-component of ground velocity = 0):

$200\sin\alpha - 100 = 0 \Rightarrow \sin\alpha = 0.5 \Rightarrow \alpha = 30°$ east of north.

Ground speed (northward) $= 200\cos30° = 200\times\sqrt{3}/2 = 100\sqrt{3} \approx 173$ km/h.

Time $= 300/(100\sqrt{3}) = \sqrt{3} \approx 1.73$ h.

---

**Example 4 (Minimum distance, JEE Advanced):** Object A starts at $(0, 0)$ with velocity $4\,\hat{i}$ m/s. Object B starts at $(3, 4)$ m with velocity $-2\,\hat{j}$ m/s. Find the minimum distance between them.

$\vec{d}_0 = (3-0)\,\hat{i} + (4-0)\,\hat{j} = 3\,\hat{i} + 4\,\hat{j}$ m

$\vec{v}_{rel} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B = (4\,\hat{i}) - (-2\,\hat{j}) = 4\,\hat{i} + 2\,\hat{j}$ m/s

Using the cross-product formula:

$|\vec{d}_0 \times \vec{v}_{rel}| = |3\times2 - 4\times4| = |6-16| = 10$ m²/s

$|\vec{v}_{rel}| = \sqrt{16+4} = \sqrt{20} = 2\sqrt{5}$ m/s

$d_{min} = \dfrac{10}{2\sqrt{5}} = \dfrac{5}{\sqrt{5}} = \sqrt{5} \approx 2.24$ m

Check: time of minimum distance:

$t^* = -\dfrac{\vec{d}_0 \cdot \vec{v}_{rel}}{|\vec{v}_{rel}|^2} = -\dfrac{3(4)+4(2)}{20} = -\dfrac{20}{20} = -1$ s.

$t^* < 0$ — the minimum distance was in the past! So the closest distance during forward time ($t \geq 0$) is the distance at $t = 0$:

$|\vec{d}_0| = \sqrt{9+16} = 5$ m.

**Therefore** the objects are already separating, and the minimum distance for $t \geq 0$ is $5$ m (occurring at $t = 0$).

---

**Example 5 (Two cars, head-on):** Car A moves east at 60 km/h; car B moves west at 80 km/h. Initial separation: 700 km. When do they meet?

Relative speed of approach $= 60 + 80 = 140$ km/h.

Time to meet $= 700/140 = 5$ h.

Position of meeting: A travels $60 \times 5 = 300$ km east from A's start. B travels $80 \times 5 = 400$ km west from B's start. $300 + 400 = 700$ km ✓.

---

### 12.9 JEE Advanced: Relative Motion with Acceleration

When two objects have different accelerations:

$$\vec{a}_{rel} = \vec{a}_A - \vec{a}_B$$

The relative motion of A in B's frame obeys the equations of motion with:
- Initial relative velocity: $\vec{v}_{A/B}(0) = \vec{v}_A(0) - \vec{v}_B(0)$
- Relative acceleration: $\vec{a}_{A/B} = \vec{a}_A - \vec{a}_B$

**Example:** A ball is dropped inside a freely falling elevator. In the ground frame, the ball has acceleration $g$ downward. The elevator has acceleration $g$ downward. Relative acceleration $= g - g = 0$. In the elevator's frame, the ball floats — it has zero relative acceleration and moves in a straight line at constant relative velocity (zero if released from rest in the elevator). This is the weightlessness experienced in free fall.

**Monkey-gun problem (JEE classic):** A hunter aims directly at a monkey sitting on a tree branch. The monkey sees the gun fire and drops from the branch at the instant the bullet leaves the gun. Does the bullet hit the monkey?

*Analysis:* Let the horizontal distance be $L$, height of monkey be $H$.

Bullet's position: $x = v_0\cos\theta\cdot t$, $y = v_0\sin\theta\cdot t - \tfrac{1}{2}gt^2$.

Monkey's position: $x_m = L$ (doesn't move horizontally), $y_m = H - \tfrac{1}{2}gt^2$.

Bullet reaches $x = L$ when $t^* = L/(v_0\cos\theta)$. At that time:

Bullet height: $y_{bullet}(t^*) = v_0\sin\theta\cdot t^* - \tfrac{1}{2}g(t^*)^2$

Note that $v_0\sin\theta/v_0\cos\theta = \tan\theta = H/L$ (since the gun is aimed directly at the monkey). Therefore $v_0\sin\theta\cdot t^* = (H/L)\cdot L = H$.

$$y_{bullet}(t^*) = H - \tfrac{1}{2}g(t^*)^2 = y_m(t^*)$$

**The bullet always hits the monkey, regardless of $v_0$ (as long as the bullet reaches the tree before hitting the ground).**

The relative acceleration of the bullet with respect to the monkey is $\vec{a}_{bullet} - \vec{a}_{monkey} = (-g\,\hat{j}) - (-g\,\hat{j}) = \vec{0}$. In the monkey's freely-falling frame, the bullet travels in a straight line — and since the gun is aimed at the initial position of the monkey (which is also the monkey's position in that frame), the bullet hits.

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

- $\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B$: subtract the observer's velocity. $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ (chain rule).
- River-boat: minimum time → aim $\perp$ to bank ($t = d/v_b$); zero drift → $\sin\alpha = v_r/v_b$ ($t = d/\sqrt{v_b^2-v_r^2}$); requires $v_b > v_r$ for zero drift.
- Rain: apparent velocity = rain velocity − person velocity; umbrella tilt angle $\tan\theta = v_P/v_R$.
- Aircraft crosswind: same structure as river-boat; aim upwind by $\sin\alpha = v_{wind}/v_{air}$.
- Minimum distance: $d_{min} = |\vec{d}_0 \times \vec{v}_{rel}|/|\vec{v}_{rel}|$; valid only if $t^* > 0$.
- Monkey-gun: both fall with same $g$ → bullet always hits if it reaches the tree.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 12 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 13: Uniform Circular Motion.*

---

## SECTION 13 — Uniform Circular Motion

---

### 13.1 What Makes Circular Motion Special

Straight-line motion is the simplest: velocity changes only in magnitude. Circular motion is the next: velocity changes only in direction. Uniform circular motion (UCM) is the pure case of direction change with no speed change — and it turns out to require a very specific type of acceleration.

Understanding UCM geometrically is essential not just for the circular motion questions in JEE, but for all of rotational mechanics, gravitation (orbital motion), and electromagnetism (charged particle in a magnetic field).

---

### 13.2 Angular Quantities

#### Angular Displacement

When a particle moves along a circle of radius $r$, it sweeps out an **angle** at the centre. The angle $\Delta\theta$ (in radians) is defined as:

$$\Delta\theta = \frac{\Delta s}{r}$$

where $\Delta s$ is the arc length. This makes the radian a dimensionless unit: $1\,\text{rad} = 1\,\text{m/m}$.

**Conversion:** $180° = \pi\,\text{rad}$; $1° = \pi/180\,\text{rad}$; $360° = 2\pi\,\text{rad}$.

#### Angular Velocity

<Formula label="Angular Velocity">
$$\omega = \frac{d\theta}{dt}$$
</Formula>

- SI unit: rad/s
- Positive: counterclockwise (by convention)
- Negative: clockwise
- For UCM: $\omega = $ constant

**Relation to linear speed:**

$$v = r\omega$$

Since $\Delta s = r\Delta\theta$, dividing both sides by $\Delta t$ and taking the limit: $v = r\omega$. The linear speed is larger at larger radii — this is why the outer tracks of a CD spin faster than the inner ones for the same angular speed.

#### Period and Frequency

The **period** $T$ is the time for one complete revolution ($\Delta\theta = 2\pi$):

<Formula label="Period">
$$T = \frac{2\pi}{\omega} = \frac{2\pi r}{v}$$
</Formula>

The **frequency** $f$ is the number of revolutions per second:

<Formula label="Frequency">
$$f = \frac{1}{T} = \frac{\omega}{2\pi}$$
</Formula>

Units: Hz (hertz) = s⁻¹.

**Key relations:**

$$\omega = 2\pi f = \frac{2\pi}{T} \qquad v = r\omega = \frac{2\pi r}{T} = 2\pi r f$$

---

### 13.3 The Position Vector in UCM

For a particle moving counterclockwise in a circle of radius $R$ centred at the origin, starting at $(R, 0)$:

$$\vec{r}(t) = R\cos(\omega t)\,\hat{i} + R\sin(\omega t)\,\hat{j}$$

The position vector rotates at angular rate $\omega$, always maintaining magnitude $R$.

---

### 13.4 The Velocity Vector: Deriving $v = R\omega$, Direction Tangential

$$\vec{v}(t) = \frac{d\vec{r}}{dt} = -R\omega\sin(\omega t)\,\hat{i} + R\omega\cos(\omega t)\,\hat{j}$$

**Magnitude:** $|\vec{v}| = R\omega\sqrt{\sin^2\omega t + \cos^2\omega t} = R\omega$ ✓

**Direction:** $\vec{v} \cdot \vec{r} = R\cos\omega t\cdot(-R\omega\sin\omega t) + R\sin\omega t\cdot(R\omega\cos\omega t) = 0$

Velocity is always perpendicular to position — i.e., always **tangent** to the circle.

---

### 13.5 The Centripetal Acceleration: Derivation

$$\vec{a}(t) = \frac{d\vec{v}}{dt} = -R\omega^2\cos(\omega t)\,\hat{i} - R\omega^2\sin(\omega t)\,\hat{j} = -\omega^2\vec{r}$$

**Magnitude:**

<Formula label="Centripetal Acceleration">
$$a_c = R\omega^2 = \frac{v^2}{R} = v\omega$$
</Formula>

**Direction:** $\vec{a} = -\omega^2\vec{r}$ — always opposite to the position vector, i.e., always **pointing toward the centre** of the circle.

This is the **centripetal** ("centre-seeking") acceleration.

<Callout type="info">
**What centripetal acceleration does and does NOT do**

- It changes the **direction** of velocity.
- It does NOT change the **magnitude** (speed) of velocity — it is always perpendicular to $\vec{v}$.
- It is a real acceleration: $\vec{a} = d\vec{v}/dt \neq 0$.
- The force providing this acceleration is the **centripetal force** — it can be gravity, tension, friction, electric force, etc., depending on the physical scenario.
</Callout>

#### Geometric Derivation (Without Calculus)

Consider velocity vectors $\vec{v}_1$ and $\vec{v}_2$ at two nearby points on the circle, separated by angle $\Delta\theta$. Both have magnitude $v$.

The change in velocity: $|\Delta\vec{v}| = 2v\sin(\Delta\theta/2) \approx v\Delta\theta$ for small $\Delta\theta$.

The time to traverse the arc: $\Delta t = R\Delta\theta/v$.

$$a = \lim_{\Delta t\to 0}\frac{|\Delta\vec{v}|}{\Delta t} = \frac{v\Delta\theta}{R\Delta\theta/v} = \frac{v^2}{R}$$

The direction of $\Delta\vec{v}$ bisects the angle between $\vec{v}_1$ and $\vec{v}_2$ — as $\Delta\theta \to 0$, this direction becomes perpendicular to $\vec{v}$, pointing inward. ✓

---

### 13.6 Complete Kinematics of UCM

| Quantity | Formula |
|---|---|
| Position | $\vec{r} = R\cos\omega t\,\hat{i} + R\sin\omega t\,\hat{j}$ |
| Linear speed | $v = R\omega$ |
| Period | $T = 2\pi/\omega$ |
| Frequency | $f = \omega/(2\pi)$ |
| Centripetal acceleration magnitude | $a_c = v^2/R = R\omega^2 = v\omega$ |
| Centripetal acceleration direction | Toward centre (radially inward) |
| Tangential acceleration | $a_t = 0$ (for UCM) |
| Total acceleration | $a_c$ (radially inward) |

---

### 13.7 Centrifugal Force: The Pseudo-Force in a Rotating Frame

In the rotating frame (non-inertial), a particle at rest on the circle experiences a pseudo-force directed **radially outward** with magnitude $m\omega^2 R = mv^2/R$.

This is the **centrifugal force** — it exists only in the rotating frame. In the inertial ground frame, there is no such force; only the centripetal force (inward) acts.

<Callout type="warning">
**"Centrifugal force flings the passenger outward" — this is wrong in the ground frame.**

In the ground frame: the passenger's inertia tends to keep them moving in a straight line (tangentially). The centripetal force from the car seat pushes them inward to follow the circular path. When the car turns sharply and the seat can't provide enough centripetal force, the passenger slides tangentially — they do NOT get "flung outward" by any real force.

In the car's rotating frame: yes, a pseudo-centrifugal force pushes the passenger outward. But this frame is non-inertial.
</Callout>

---

### 13.8 Angular Acceleration and Non-Uniform Circular Motion

When an object moves in a circle but its speed is **not constant**, it undergoes non-uniform circular motion. The angular speed $\omega$ is no longer constant.

**Angular acceleration:**

$$\alpha = \frac{d\omega}{dt} = \frac{d^2\theta}{dt^2} \quad \text{(rad/s}^2\text{)}$$

**Relation to linear tangential acceleration:**

$$a_t = R\alpha$$

The total acceleration has two components:

<Formula label="Total Acceleration in Non-Uniform Circular Motion">
$$\vec{a} = a_t\,\hat{\theta} + a_c\,\hat{r}_{in}$$

$$a_t = R\alpha = \frac{dv}{dt} \qquad a_c = \frac{v^2}{R} = R\omega^2$$

$$|\vec{a}| = \sqrt{a_t^2 + a_c^2}$$
</Formula>

where $\hat{\theta}$ is the tangential unit vector (direction of motion) and $\hat{r}_{in}$ is the radially inward unit vector.

**Angle of total acceleration from the radius:**

$$\tan\phi = \frac{a_t}{a_c} = \frac{R\alpha}{R\omega^2} = \frac{\alpha}{\omega^2}$$

---

### 13.9 Equations of Circular Motion (Constant Angular Acceleration)

By analogy with linear kinematics (replacing $s\to\theta$, $v\to\omega$, $a\to\alpha$):

| Linear | Angular |
|---|---|
| $v = u + at$ | $\omega = \omega_0 + \alpha t$ |
| $s = ut + \frac{1}{2}at^2$ | $\theta = \omega_0 t + \frac{1}{2}\alpha t^2$ |
| $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$ | $\omega^2 = \omega_0^2 + 2\alpha\theta$ |
| $s = \frac{u+v}{2}t$ | $\theta = \frac{\omega_0+\omega}{2}t$ |

These apply only when $\alpha$ is constant, exactly as the linear equations require constant $a$.

---

### 13.10 The Angular Velocity Vector

In 3D, angular velocity is a **vector** $\vec{\omega}$ pointing along the rotation axis (right-hand rule: curl the fingers in the direction of rotation, thumb points in the direction of $\vec{\omega}$).

For a particle at position $\vec{r}$ from the axis:

$$\vec{v} = \vec{\omega} \times \vec{r}$$

**Magnitude check:** $|\vec{v}| = |\vec{\omega}||\vec{r}|\sin\phi$, where $\phi$ is the angle between $\vec{\omega}$ and $\vec{r}$. For a circle in the plane perpendicular to the axis, $\phi = 90°$, so $v = \omega r$ ✓.

For JEE Main problems, the scalar $\omega$ suffices. The vector form is used in JEE Advanced rotational problems.

---

### 13.11 Motion in a Vertical Circle

A particle moves in a vertical circle of radius $R$ attached to a string. The speed varies with height because of gravity — this is non-uniform circular motion.

**Energy conservation** (kinematics + work-energy):

$$v^2 = v_{bottom}^2 - 2gR(1-\cos\theta)$$

where $\theta$ is the angle from the bottom.

At angle $\theta$ from bottom:
$$v^2 = v_b^2 - 2gR + 2gR\cos\theta$$

At the top ($\theta = 180°$, height = $2R$):
$$v_t^2 = v_b^2 - 4gR$$

**Centripetal acceleration at angle $\theta$:**
$$a_c = \frac{v^2}{R} = \frac{v_b^2 - 2gR(1-\cos\theta)}{R}$$

This decreases from bottom to top (speed decreases as the particle rises).

**Condition for completing the loop:** The string must remain taut — tension $\geq 0$ at all points, especially the top. At the top: $T + mg = mv_t^2/R$. For $T \geq 0$: $v_t^2 \geq gR$, which gives $v_b \geq \sqrt{5gR}$.

This is a kinematics-plus-dynamics result, but the kinematics (how speed varies with position) is purely what we've discussed here.

---

### 13.12 Conical Pendulum and Banked Roads

Both of these involve circular motion in a horizontal plane — the object moves in a horizontal circle while gravity acts vertically.

#### Conical Pendulum

A mass on a string of length $L$ swings in a horizontal circle. String makes angle $\theta$ with the vertical. Radius of circle = $L\sin\theta$.

$$T\cos\theta = mg \quad \text{(vertical)} \qquad T\sin\theta = \frac{mv^2}{r} = m\omega^2 r \quad \text{(centripetal)}$$

Dividing: $\tan\theta = \omega^2 r / g = \omega^2 L\sin\theta/g$.

$$\omega^2 = \frac{g}{L\cos\theta} \implies T_{period} = 2\pi\sqrt{\frac{L\cos\theta}{g}}$$

#### Banked Road

A car rounds a banked curve (banking angle $\phi$) at speed $v$ on a road of radius $r$.

For no friction required:
$$\tan\phi = \frac{v^2}{rg} \implies v = \sqrt{rg\tan\phi}$$

If friction is present (maximum static friction $\mu$):
- Maximum safe speed: $v_{max} = \sqrt{rg\cdot\frac{\tan\phi+\mu}{1-\mu\tan\phi}}$
- Minimum safe speed (no sliding inward): $v_{min} = \sqrt{rg\cdot\frac{\tan\phi-\mu}{1+\mu\tan\phi}}$

---

### 13.13 Worked Examples

**Example 1:** A particle moves in a circle of radius 2 m with angular velocity $3$ rad/s. Find: (a) linear speed, (b) period, (c) centripetal acceleration, (d) centripetal acceleration in terms of $g$.

**(a):** $v = R\omega = 2\times3 = 6$ m/s

**(b):** $T = 2\pi/\omega = 2\pi/3 \approx 2.09$ s

**(c):** $a_c = v^2/R = 36/2 = 18$ m/s² (or $R\omega^2 = 2\times9 = 18$ m/s²) ✓

**(d):** $a_c/g = 18/10 = 1.8g$

---

**Example 2:** A wheel starts from rest and reaches $300$ rpm in $10$ s under constant angular acceleration. Find: (a) angular acceleration, (b) angle turned, (c) linear speed and centripetal acceleration of a point on the rim ($r = 0.3$ m) at $t = 10$ s.

**(a):** $\omega_f = 300\,\text{rpm} = 300\times2\pi/60 = 10\pi\,\text{rad/s}$

$\alpha = (\omega_f - \omega_0)/t = 10\pi/10 = \pi\,\text{rad/s}^2 \approx 3.14\,\text{rad/s}^2$

**(b):** $\theta = \omega_0 t + \tfrac{1}{2}\alpha t^2 = 0 + \tfrac{1}{2}(\pi)(100) = 50\pi\,\text{rad} \approx 157\,\text{rad}$

**(c):** $v = r\omega_f = 0.3\times10\pi = 3\pi \approx 9.42$ m/s

$a_c = v^2/r = (3\pi)^2/0.3 = 9\pi^2/0.3 = 30\pi^2 \approx 296$ m/s²

Also: $a_t = r\alpha = 0.3\pi \approx 0.94$ m/s²

Total: $|\vec{a}| = \sqrt{a_c^2+a_t^2} \approx \sqrt{296^2+0.94^2} \approx 296$ m/s² (centripetal dominates).

---

**Example 3 (JEE Advanced):** A particle moves in a circle of radius $R = 1$ m. At $t = 0$: speed $= 2$ m/s and rate of increase of speed $= 3$ m/s². Find the magnitude of the total acceleration vector and its angle with the radius at $t = 0$.

$a_c = v^2/R = 4/1 = 4$ m/s²

$a_t = dv/dt = 3$ m/s²

$|\vec{a}| = \sqrt{16+9} = 5$ m/s²

Angle from radius (inward direction): $\tan\phi = a_t/a_c = 3/4 \Rightarrow \phi = 37°$ from inward radius toward tangent.

---

**Example 4 (JEE Main: Earth satellite):** A satellite orbits Earth at height $h = 0$ (on the surface, approximately). Given $R_E = 6400$ km, $g = 10$ m/s², find the orbital speed and period.

For circular orbit at radius $R_E$: centripetal acceleration provided by gravity:

$$\frac{v^2}{R_E} = g \implies v = \sqrt{gR_E} = \sqrt{10\times6.4\times10^6} = \sqrt{6.4\times10^7} \approx 8000\text{ m/s} = 8\text{ km/s}$$

$$T = \frac{2\pi R_E}{v} = \frac{2\pi\times6.4\times10^6}{8000} \approx 5027\text{ s} \approx 84\text{ min}$$

---

**Example 5 (Non-uniform circular motion):** A particle moves in a circle of radius $5$ m. Its speed at $t = 0$ is $10$ m/s and increases uniformly at $2$ m/s². At $t = 3$ s find: (a) speed, (b) centripetal acceleration, (c) tangential acceleration, (d) total acceleration and angle with inward radius.

**(a):** $v(3) = 10 + 2\times3 = 16$ m/s

**(b):** $a_c = v^2/R = 256/5 = 51.2$ m/s²

**(c):** $a_t = 2$ m/s²

**(d):** $|\vec{a}| = \sqrt{51.2^2 + 4} = \sqrt{2621.44+4} = \sqrt{2625.44} \approx 51.24$ m/s²

Angle: $\tan\phi = 2/51.2 \approx 0.039 \Rightarrow \phi \approx 2.2°$ from inward radius.

---

### 13.14 Key Comparisons: UCM vs Other Motions

| Property | UCM | Free fall | Projectile | SHM |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Speed | Constant | Increases | Changes (component) | Changes |
| Velocity direction | Continuously changing | Fixed (down) | Changes | Changes |
| Acceleration direction | Radially inward (rotating) | Fixed (down) | Fixed (down) | Toward equilibrium |
| Acceleration magnitude | Constant $v^2/R$ | Constant $g$ | Constant $g$ | Variable $\omega^2 \vert x \vert $ |
| Trajectory | Circle | Straight line | Parabola | Straight line (oscillating) |

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

Uniform Circular Motion (UCM): speed constant, direction changes continuously.

Angular quantities: $\omega = d\theta/dt$ (rad/s); $v = R\omega$; $T = 2\pi/\omega$; $f = \omega/(2\pi)$.

Centripetal acceleration: $a_c = v^2/R = R\omega^2$ — always directed radially inward. Derived from $\vec{r}(t) = R\cos\omega t\,\hat{i} + R\sin\omega t\,\hat{j}$ by differentiating twice.

Centripetal force is the net inward force providing $a_c$; centrifugal force is a pseudo-force in the rotating frame only.

Non-uniform circular: $a_t = R\alpha$ (changes speed) + $a_c = v^2/R$ (changes direction); always perpendicular; $|\vec{a}| = \sqrt{a_t^2+a_c^2}$.

Angular equations of motion (constant $\alpha$): exactly analogous to linear equations with $s\to\theta$, $v\to\omega$, $a\to\alpha$.

Conical pendulum: $T_{period} = 2\pi\sqrt{L\cos\theta/g}$.

Vertical circle: minimum speed at top for completing loop: $v_{top,min} = \sqrt{gR}$; minimum speed at bottom: $v_{bottom,min} = \sqrt{5gR}$.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 13 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 14: Advanced JEE Insights.*

---

## SECTION 14 — Advanced JEE Insights

*Hidden assumptions · Limiting cases · Symmetry · Constraint analysis · Optimisation*

---

### 14.1 Why "Advanced Insights" Matter

You now have all the mathematical machinery of kinematics. But JEE Advanced does not simply test whether you can plug numbers into formulas — it tests whether you understand *why* those formulas work, what assumptions they rest on, and what happens when those assumptions break.

The students who score in the top 1% typically have two additional skills beyond the formulas:

1. **They check their answers** using limiting cases, dimensional analysis, and symmetry — and catch errors before finalising.
2. **They see structure** — constraint equations, conservation principles, symmetry arguments — that lets them bypass long calculations entirely.

This section teaches both.

---

### 14.2 Dimensional Analysis: The First Sanity Check

Every kinematic formula must be dimensionally consistent. If your answer has the wrong dimensions, it is wrong — no matter how correct the algebra seemed.

**Key dimensions:**
$$[x] = \text{L} \quad [v] = \text{LT}^{-1} \quad [a] = \text{LT}^{-2} \quad [t] = \text{T} \quad [\omega] = \text{T}^{-1}$$

**Practice:** Without memorising, derive the units of centripetal acceleration.

$[a_c] = [v^2/r] = (\text{LT}^{-1})^2/\text{L} = \text{L}^2\text{T}^{-2}/\text{L} = \text{LT}^{-2}$ ✓ (correct for acceleration)

**Deriving formulas from dimensions:**

The range of a projectile must depend on $u$ (initial speed, LT⁻¹) and $g$ (acceleration, LT⁻²). The only combination with dimensions of length is $u^2/g$. So $R \propto u^2/g$ — the rest (the $\sin2\theta$ factor) must come from the physics, but the dimensional structure is fixed.

<Callout type="tip">
**Dimensional analysis as a check at each step**

After each major algebraic step, verify the dimensions are correct. This catches factor errors, missing squares, and algebraic sign problems before they propagate.

In particular: $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$ — check: $[v^2] = [\text{LT}^{-1}]^2 = \text{L}^2\text{T}^{-2}$; $[2as] = [\text{LT}^{-2}][\text{L}] = \text{L}^2\text{T}^{-2}$ ✓.
</Callout>

---

### 14.3 Limiting Cases: Your Most Powerful Verification Tool

A **limiting case** is a special value of a parameter that makes the answer obviously known. If your formula doesn't reduce to the correct known result in the limit, it's wrong.

#### Projectile Limits

**$\theta \to 0$ (horizontal throw):**

$$H = \frac{u^2\sin^20}{2g} = 0 \checkmark \quad T = \frac{2u\sin0}{g} = 0 \checkmark \quad R = \frac{u^2\sin0}{g} = 0 \checkmark$$

A horizontal throw doesn't go up, has no flight time (on level ground), and has zero range — correct if we're measuring from launch height. (If launched from a cliff, the formula for $T$ changes; the limits tell you your formula assumes level ground.)

**$\theta \to 90°$ (straight up):**

$$H = \frac{u^2}{2g} \checkmark \quad T = \frac{2u}{g} \checkmark \quad R = \frac{u^2\sin180°}{g} = 0 \checkmark$$

Object goes straight up and comes back down — correct.

**$\theta = 45°$:**

$$R = \frac{u^2\sin90°}{g} = \frac{u^2}{g} = R_{max} \checkmark$$

#### Inclined Plane Limits

For range on incline $R = \frac{u^2}{g(1+\sin\beta)}$:

**$\beta \to 0$ (flat ground):** $R = u^2/g = R_{max}$ for $\theta = 45°$ ✓

**$\beta \to 90°$ (vertical wall):** $R = u^2/(2g)$ — half the flat-ground range. The ball hits the wall immediately since it can't go "up" the wall on a ballistic trajectory. The formula gives a small non-zero result because the "range" is along a near-vertical surface; physically it makes sense.

#### Circular Motion Limits

For centripetal acceleration $a_c = v^2/R$:

**$R \to \infty$ (infinite radius = straight line):** $a_c \to 0$ — no centripetal acceleration for straight-line motion ✓

**$R \to 0$ (infinitely tight turn):** $a_c \to \infty$ — need infinite centripetal force for a hairpin turn ✓

#### Relative Motion Limits

For river crossing time $t = d/\sqrt{v_b^2 - v_r^2}$:

**$v_r \to 0$ (no current):** $t = d/v_b$ — direct crossing in still water ✓

**$v_r \to v_b$ (current equals boat speed):** $t \to \infty$ — the boat can never make headway against an equal current ✓

---

### 14.4 Symmetry Arguments: Skipping Calculations

Symmetry is one of the most powerful tools in physics. If a problem has a symmetry, the answer must respect that symmetry — and this often determines the answer without calculation.

#### Time-Reversal Symmetry in Free Fall

Gravity is time-reversal symmetric: replace $t \to -t$ in the equations of motion and the physics is unchanged (for constant $g$, no dissipation).

**Consequence:** The upward and downward journeys under gravity are mirror images. An object thrown upward with speed $u$ returns to the launch height with speed $u$ after time $T = 2u/g$.

**JEE application:** A ball thrown upward at $20$ m/s from $h = 0$. Without any calculation:
- It lands with speed $20$ m/s (time-reversal says so).
- Rise time = fall time = $2$ s.
- Maximum height = $20$ m.

Students who "see" symmetry need only compute $H = u^2/2g = 20$ m — the rest follows.

#### Horizontal Symmetry in Projectile Motion

The peak of the trajectory is at exactly half the range horizontally. This follows from the parabolic shape and the horizontal-velocity being constant: the trajectory is symmetric about the vertical axis through the peak.

**Consequence:** If you know where a projectile is on the way up, you can immediately state where it will be at the mirror-image point on the way down (same height, same speed, velocity reflected through horizontal).

#### The $\theta$ and $90° - \theta$ Symmetry

Two launch angles giving the same range: this is a $\sin2\theta = \sin(180° - 2\theta)$ symmetry. The two trajectories have the same range but mirror-image heights and times.

**Application:** A ball is thrown at $60°$ with $R = 100$ m. What angle gives the same range?
By symmetry: $90° - 60° = 30°$. No calculation needed.

---

### 14.5 Hidden Assumptions: What JEE Problems Don't Say

Every JEE kinematics problem makes assumptions that are not stated. Missing them leads to wrong answers on problems that seem straightforward.

#### Hidden Assumption 1: "Starting from rest" means $u = 0$

This seems obvious, but note: "starts from rest at position $x_0$" means $u = 0$ AND $x = x_0$ at $t = 0$. If a problem says "a car starts from rest," it means $v_0 = 0$. But if it says "a car passes a point at rest," it still means $v = 0$ at that point — but the car may have been decelerating to that point and then started again.

#### Hidden Assumption 2: Equations of motion assume constant acceleration

When a problem says "uniformly accelerated" or gives a constant force, this is stated. But when it says "under gravity," it assumes $g$ is constant — valid near Earth's surface, not for satellite motion.

**Trap:** A ball thrown vertically upward from a height of $6400$ km above Earth's surface (equal to the Earth's radius). Here $g$ varies — you cannot use $v^2 = u^2 - 2gH$. Use energy conservation with $g = GM/r^2$.

#### Hidden Assumption 3: "Smooth" or "frictionless" means no tangential force

In circular motion problems on a track: "smooth track" means no friction, so no tangential force, meaning the only acceleration is centripetal. This is an assumption that dramatically simplifies the problem.

#### Hidden Assumption 4: Projectile equations assume flat Earth

$R = u^2\sin2\theta/g$ assumes the Earth is flat and $g$ is constant. For ranges $\gtrsim 10$ km, the Earth's curvature matters. In JEE, all projectile problems assume flat Earth.

#### Hidden Assumption 5: The ball is a particle

When a ball is thrown, the formula assumes point-mass motion — no rotation, no size effects, no aerodynamics. For spinning balls (cricket, tennis), the actual trajectory is not a parabola. In JEE, always assume the particle model.

#### Hidden Assumption 6: "At the same time" in collision problems

When two objects are launched simultaneously and you're asked when they meet, the implicit assumption is that "time starts at the same moment for both." If the problem says "a second ball is thrown 2 s after the first," you must account for this time offset.

---

### 14.6 Constraint Equations: Kinematics of Connected Objects

**Constraint equations** arise when objects are physically connected — by strings, rods, surfaces, or contact — such that their motions are geometrically related.

#### String Over a Pulley

A string over a frictionless pulley connects two masses. If mass A descends by $x$, mass B rises by $x$ (inextensible string). Differentiating twice:

$$a_A = a_B = a$$

If the pulley itself can accelerate (Atwood machine with massive pulley or compound pulley), the constraint becomes more complex:

For a compound Atwood machine where pulley P has masses hanging from it:

$$a_1 + a_2 = 2a_P$$

where $a_1$ and $a_2$ are the accelerations of the two masses and $a_P$ is the acceleration of the pulley. This is a purely kinematic result derived from the inextensibility of the string.

#### Object on an Incline

If a wedge of angle $\theta$ moves horizontally with acceleration $A$, and a ball rolls on the wedge, the constraint is:

The ball's velocity relative to the wedge is along the incline surface (no perpendicular component, assuming contact maintained). This gives a constraint relating the ball's acceleration in the ground frame to $A$ and $\theta$.

#### The General Constraint Method

1. Write the position of each object in terms of generalised coordinates.
2. Express the constraint (inextensible string = constant total length; contact = equal normal components of velocity).
3. Differentiate once for velocity constraint; twice for acceleration constraint.

---

### 14.7 Optimisation in Kinematics

Many JEE Advanced problems ask for the "maximum," "minimum," or "optimal" value of some kinematic quantity.

#### Standard Optimisation Problems

**Maximum range at fixed speed:** $\theta = 45°$ (Section 11).

**Maximum height for fixed range $R$:** Choose $\theta$ to maximise $H = R\tan\theta/4$ (from trajectory equation). As $\theta \to 90°$, $H \to \infty$ and $R \to 0$ — so there's no maximum height at fixed $R$. But for fixed $R$, $H$ is maximised at large $\theta$, bounded by the constraint that $R \leq u^2/g$.

**Time of flight for same range:** For range $R$ (same at $\theta$ and $90°-\theta$), time is longer at the larger angle. Students who want to make the ball "hang" in the air for longer should choose $\theta > 45°$.

**Minimum speed for a given range:** You want to cover range $R$ with minimum launch speed. $R = u^2\sin2\theta/g$ gives $u^2 = Rg/\sin2\theta$. This is minimised when $\sin2\theta = 1$, i.e., $\theta = 45°$. So:

$$u_{min} = \sqrt{Rg}$$

A target at range $R$ can be reached with minimum speed $\sqrt{Rg}$, only at $\theta = 45°$.

**Target on a wall:** A wall is at horizontal distance $d$. What speed $u$ is needed to hit height $h$ on the wall, at what angle?

From trajectory: $h = d\tan\theta - gd^2/(2u^2\cos^2\theta)$.

This is a quadratic in $\tan\theta$: $gd^2/(2u^2)\cdot\tan^2\theta - d\tan\theta + (h + gd^2/2u^2) = 0$.

For real solutions: discriminant $\geq 0$, giving a constraint on $u$.

**Minimum speed to clear a wall of height $h$ at distance $d$:** Set discriminant $= 0$:

$$u_{min}^2 = g(h + \sqrt{d^2+h^2}) = g(h + d/\cos\phi)$$

where $\phi = \tan^{-1}(d/h)$.

---

### 14.8 The Envelope of Projectile Trajectories

For fixed initial speed $u$, as $\theta$ varies, the set of all possible trajectories fills a region of space. The boundary of this region is the **envelope** — no projectile with speed $u$ can reach outside it.

**Equation of the envelope:**

Starting from $y = x\tan\theta - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2}(1+\tan^2\theta)$, eliminate $\theta$ by differentiating with respect to $\tan\theta$ and setting to zero:

$$x - \frac{gx^2\tan\theta}{u^2} = 0 \implies \tan\theta = \frac{u^2}{gx}$$

Substituting back:

$$y = \frac{u^2}{2g} - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2}$$

<Formula label="Envelope of Projectile Trajectories">
$$\boxed{y = \frac{u^2}{2g} - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2}}$$
</Formula>

This is itself a parabola — the "parabola of safety." Any target outside this parabola cannot be reached with speed $u$; any target inside can be hit with two different angles.

**Properties:**
- Maximum height: $u^2/(2g)$ (directly above the launch point)
- Maximum horizontal reach: $x = u^2/g$ (at $y = 0$, i.e., same level) ✓ equals $R_{max}$
- Shape: downward-opening parabola with vertex at $(0, u^2/2g)$

---

### 14.9 Non-Obvious Kinematic Results

#### The Equal-Angle Result for Projectile Velocity

At any point on a projectile's trajectory where the velocity makes angle $\phi$ with the horizontal, the horizontal distance from that point to the landing point equals:

$$x_{remain} = \frac{v^2\sin2\phi}{g}$$

This looks like a "mini-range" formula — the projectile from that instant, with speed $v$ at angle $\phi$, has the same remaining range as a fresh projectile from ground level with same speed and angle.

#### Speed-Curvature Tradeoff

In projectile motion, the radius of curvature $\rho = v^3/(v_x g) = v^3/(u\cos\theta\cdot g)$. The curvature (= $1/\rho$) is maximum when $v$ is minimum — at the peak. At the peak, the entire weight of the projectile is providing centripetal force:

$$mg = \frac{mv_{top}^2}{\rho_{top}} \implies \rho_{top} = \frac{v_{top}^2}{g} = \frac{u^2\cos^2\theta}{g}$$

This result is used in JEE problems asking for the radius of curvature at the apex.

#### Galilean Velocity Addition is Not Relativistic

In classical mechanics, $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ (velocities simply add). This breaks down at speeds approaching $c$ (special relativity). For JEE, always use the classical formula.

#### The Average Velocity Theorem

For motion with **constant acceleration**:

$$\bar{v} = \frac{s}{\Delta t} = v_{midtime} = \frac{u+v}{2}$$

The average velocity equals the instantaneous velocity at the **midpoint in time** — not the midpoint in position. The position midpoint velocity is $\sqrt{(u^2+v^2)/2}$ (RMS), which is always greater than the time midpoint velocity.

---

### 14.10 Constraint Kinematics: Advanced Examples

**Rope over a peg — unequal arms:**

A rope of length $L$ is draped over a smooth peg at height $H$ above the floor. At time $t$, the left arm has length $l$ and the right arm has length $L-l$. If the left side descends with acceleration $a$:

- Left side velocity: $v$ downward
- Right side velocity: $v$ upward (inextensible rope)
- Relative velocity between the two ends: $2v$ always

**Car turning a corner:**

A car of length $d$ turns a corner of radius $R$ (measured to the inside wheels). The inside and outside wheels trace circles of different radii: $R_{inside} = R$ and $R_{outside} = \sqrt{R^2 + d^2}$ (approximately $R + d^2/(2R)$ for small $d$).

The outside wheels must rotate faster — which is why cars have differential gears.

**Ball on a rotating turntable:**

A ball sits on a rotating turntable at radius $r$ from the centre. If the turntable has angular velocity $\omega$ and angular acceleration $\alpha$:

- Centripetal acceleration of ball: $a_c = r\omega^2$ (inward)
- Tangential acceleration: $a_t = r\alpha$ (tangential)

These are the accelerations the ball must have to move with the turntable. The friction force provides these accelerations. If $a_{total} = \sqrt{(r\omega^2)^2 + (r\alpha)^2}$ exceeds $\mu g$, the ball slips.

---

### 14.11 Dimensional Consistency of JEE Advanced Answers

JEE Advanced often gives answers in a specific form (e.g., "$v = k\sqrt{gR}$" or "$T = c\sqrt{L/g}$") and asks you to find the numerical constant $k$ or $c$.

**Strategy:**
1. Write down the physical equation.
2. Identify which combinations of given quantities have the right dimensions.
3. Find the numerical coefficient by careful algebra.

**Example:** What is the speed of a satellite in a circular orbit at height $h$ above Earth?

$v^2/r = g_{orbit}$ where $r = R_E + h$ and $g_{orbit} = GM/r^2 = gR_E^2/r^2$.

$v = \sqrt{gR_E^2/r} = R_E\sqrt{g/r} = R_E\sqrt{g/(R_E+h)}$.

Dimensional check: $[R_E\sqrt{g/r}] = \text{L}\sqrt{\text{LT}^{-2}/\text{L}} = \text{L}\sqrt{\text{T}^{-2}} = \text{LT}^{-1}$ ✓.

---

### 14.12 The "Smart" Approach vs the "Brute Force" Approach

Here is the most important meta-skill this section teaches:

**Brute force:** Set up all equations, solve for every unknown, substitute back. Correct but slow and error-prone.

**Smart approach:** Identify a symmetry, constraint, or limiting case that reduces the problem to a one-step calculation.

| Problem | Brute force | Smart approach |
|---|---|---|
| Velocity at top of projectile | Compute $v_x$ and $v_y$ at $t = T/2$, combine | $v_y = 0$ at top, so $v = v_x = u\cos\theta$ directly |
| Same range for two angles | Solve $R(\theta_1) = R(\theta_2)$ | $\sin2\theta = \sin(180°-2\theta)$ gives $\theta_1 + \theta_2 = 90°$ |
| Speed at height $h$ in projectile | Use energy separately or kinematics | $v^2 = u^2 - 2gh$ directly (height-only dependence) |
| River crossing time for zero drift | Set up vectors, find components, solve quadratic | $\sin\alpha = v_r/v_b$, then $v_{net} = \sqrt{v_b^2-v_r^2}$ |
| Monkey-gun | Set up bullet and monkey equations separately | Relative acceleration = 0; bullet hits in monkey's frame |

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

- **Dimensional analysis:** every formula must be dimensionally correct; use it to verify and to guess formula structure.
- **Limiting cases:** substitute $\theta = 0°$, $90°$; $R \to 0$ or $\to\infty$; same/opposite velocities. If your formula doesn't reproduce the known limiting result, it's wrong.
- **Symmetry:** time-reversal in free fall (rise = fall, same speed); parabolic symmetry (peak at $x = R/2$); complementary angle symmetry ($\theta + 90°-\theta = $ same range).
- **Hidden assumptions:** constant $g$ (valid near Earth's surface), flat Earth (valid for short ranges), particle model, simultaneous launch.
- **Constraint equations:** derived from geometry alone; differentiate position constraints to get velocity and acceleration constraints.
- **Optimisation:** minimum launch speed for range $R$ is $\sqrt{Rg}$ at $\theta = 45°$; envelope of trajectories is the parabola of safety $y = u^2/2g - gx^2/2u^2$.
- **Smart approach beats brute force:** identify the insight that collapses the problem.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 14 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 15: Complete Error Analysis.*

---

## SECTION 15 — Complete Error Analysis

*The top mistakes that cost marks in JEE Main and Advanced — categorised, explained, and fixed.*

---

### 15.1 Why an Error Catalogue?

Physics errors are not random. They cluster around the same conceptual gaps, the same algebraic shortcuts, and the same misreadings of problem statements — year after year, student after student. The errors listed here are drawn from the most common patterns in JEE solutions.

Reading this section once is not enough. Return to it after every mock test. When you lose marks, locate the error category here and fix the underlying concept — not just the specific problem.

---

### CATEGORY A — Fundamental Concepts (Errors 1–15)

---

**Error 1 — Treating motion as absolute**

*What students do:* Write "the velocity of the ball is 20 m/s" without specifying a frame.

*Why it's wrong:* Velocity is frame-dependent. In JEE problems involving moving platforms, rivers, or wind, forgetting the frame gives wrong answers.

*Fix:* Always state your reference frame. "20 m/s relative to the ground" is complete; "20 m/s" alone is not.

---

**Error 2 — Confusing distance and displacement**

*What students do:* Use $s = ut + \tfrac{1}{2}at^2$ to compute "distance" when direction reversal has occurred.

*Why it's wrong:* $s$ in the equation is displacement (a vector in 1D, can be negative). Distance is always positive and requires splitting at reversal points.

*Fix:* Find the time $t^*$ when $v = 0$ (turning point). Distance $= |s(0 \to t^*)| + |s(t^* \to T)|$.

---

**Error 3 — Claiming "zero velocity means zero acceleration"**

*What students do:* When a ball reaches the top of its trajectory, state $a = 0$ because $v = 0$.

*Why it's wrong:* $a = dv/dt$ — it describes how velocity is changing, not the value of velocity itself. At the top, $v = 0$ but $a = g$ downward.

*Fix:* These are independent quantities. $v = 0$ only tells you the object is momentarily at rest. Check $a$ separately.

---

**Error 4 — "Negative acceleration means deceleration"**

*What students do:* Assume $a < 0$ always means the object is slowing down.

*Why it's wrong:* The sign of acceleration only tells you its direction. The object decelerates when $v$ and $a$ have opposite signs.

*Fix:* Object speeds up if $v$ and $a$ have the same sign; slows down if opposite. Always check both signs, not just $a$ alone.

---

**Error 5 — Using average velocity as $(v_1 + v_2)/2$ universally**

*What students do:* Average velocity $= (v_1+v_2)/2$ whenever two velocities are given.

*Why it's wrong:* This formula is valid only for constant acceleration. For variable acceleration, $\bar{v} = \Delta x/\Delta t$.

*Fix:* Arithmetic mean of velocities = average only for constant $a$. Default to definition.

---

**Error 6 — Average speed = arithmetic mean of speeds for equal-distance segments**

*What students do:* A→B at 40 km/h, B→A at 60 km/h. Average speed $= (40+60)/2 = 50$ km/h.

*Why it's wrong:* Equal distances → harmonic mean. $\bar{v} = 2(40)(60)/(40+60) = 48$ km/h.

*Fix:* Equal distances → harmonic mean; equal times → arithmetic mean.

---

**Error 7 — Forgetting to check for direction reversal when using $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$**

*What students do:* Apply $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$ and obtain $v^2 < 0$, then claim "the object never reaches that point."

*Why it's wrong:* $v^2 < 0$ means the object turned around before reaching displacement $s$. You should find the maximum $s$ where $v = 0$.

*Fix:* Before using the formula, check whether the object actually travels displacement $s$ without reversing.

---

**Error 8 — Applying equations of motion to variable acceleration**

*What students do:* A force $F = kx$ is given. Student uses $s = ut + \tfrac{1}{2}at^2$ with $a = F/m$.

*Why it's wrong:* $F = kx$ gives an acceleration that changes with position — not constant. The kinematic equations require constant $a$.

*Fix:* Use $v\,dv/dx = a(x)$ and integrate.

---

**Error 9 — Confusing $|\Delta\vec{r}|$ with $\Delta r = r_2 - r_1$**

*What students do:* When a particle moves from $(3,0)$ to $(0,3)$, claim the displacement is zero because both points are distance 3 from the origin.

*Why it's wrong:* $|\Delta\vec{r}| = |(0-3)\hat{i} + (3-0)\hat{j}| = 3\sqrt{2} \neq 0$. The change in distance from origin is zero, but the displacement is not.

*Fix:* Displacement = $\vec{r}_f - \vec{r}_i$ (vector subtraction). Change in distance from origin = $r_f - r_i$ (scalar subtraction). These are different.

---

**Error 10 — Forgetting that the independence principle only applies to independent accelerations**

*What students do:* When a ball is thrown from a moving train, add the train's acceleration to the ball's horizontal component as if they're independent.

*Why it's wrong:* Once the ball leaves the thrower's hand, only gravity acts. The train's acceleration is irrelevant to the ball's subsequent motion.

*Fix:* Identify the instant of separation. After that, only the initial velocity (in the ground frame at separation) and gravity determine the ball's trajectory.

---

**Error 11 — Wrong sign convention for $g$**

*What students do:* Choose upward as positive, but use $a = +g$ in the equations, getting wrong signs throughout.

*Why it's wrong:* If upward is positive and gravity acts downward, then $a = -g$.

*Fix:* State your sign convention explicitly at the start. "Upward = positive, so $a = -10$ m/s²." Then be consistent.

---

**Error 12 — Claiming average speed equals average velocity magnitude**

*What students do:* Assume $\bar{v} = |\bar{\vec{v}}|$ always.

*Why it's wrong:* $\bar{v} = d/t$ (distance/time); $|\bar{\vec{v}}| = |\Delta\vec{r}|/t$ (displacement/time). They are equal only for straight-line, no-reversal motion.

*Fix:* These are always different unless the path is straight and there is no reversal.

---

**Error 13 — Misidentifying the "at rest" condition**

*What students do:* When the problem says "find when the object returns to rest," find when $v = 0$ and assume this is the only such time.

*Why it's wrong:* An object can pass through $v = 0$ multiple times if acceleration keeps reversing.

*Fix:* Solve $v(t) = 0$ for ALL positive values of $t$. Check each solution to confirm the context.

---

**Error 14 — Treating position and velocity as the same sign**

*What students do:* Assume an object with positive position must be moving in the positive direction.

*Why it's wrong:* An object at $x = 5$ m might have $v = -3$ m/s (moving toward origin or beyond). Position and velocity signs are completely independent.

*Fix:* On an $x$-$t$ graph: positive $x$ means above the axis; positive slope means positive velocity. These are separate features of the graph.

---

**Error 15 — Applying formulas beyond their domain**

*What students do:* Use $R = u^2\sin2\theta/g$ when the projectile is launched from an elevated position.

*Why it's wrong:* That formula assumes the projectile lands at the same height as it was launched. If landing height differs, set $y = -H$ and solve from scratch.

*Fix:* Before applying any formula, verify all its assumptions match the problem.

---

### CATEGORY B — Graphs (Errors 16–28)

---

**Error 16 — Slope of $v$-$t$ graph = velocity, not acceleration**

*What students do:* Read the height of a point on the $v$-$t$ graph as the acceleration.

*Why it's wrong:* The height is the velocity. The slope is the acceleration.

*Fix:* $a = \Delta v / \Delta t = $ slope. Read carefully which axis is which.

---

**Error 17 — Using signed area of $v$-$t$ graph for distance**

*What students do:* Add areas above and below the time axis with signs, and call it "distance."

*Why it's wrong:* Signed area = displacement. Distance = unsigned area (all positive).

*Fix:* For distance: integrate $|v|$, or split at sign changes and add magnitudes.

---

**Error 18 — Saying intersection of $x$-$t$ graphs means same velocity**

*What students do:* Two curves cross at $t = 3$ s; student says "they have the same velocity at $t = 3$ s."

*Why it's wrong:* Intersection means same position. Same velocity requires equal slopes (tangents) at the crossing point — a separate condition.

*Fix:* Intersection → same position. Equal slopes → same velocity.

---

**Error 19 — Confusing shape of graph with sign of the quantity**

*What students do:* An upward-curving $x$-$t$ graph is said to have "positive velocity and positive acceleration" when the curve is in the $x < 0$ region.

*Why it's wrong:* The sign of $x$ tells you the object's position relative to origin. The sign of $v$ is the slope of the $x$-$t$ curve. The sign of $a$ is the concavity. These are independent.

*Fix:* Position sign $\neq$ velocity sign $\neq$ acceleration sign.

---

**Error 20 — $v^2$-$s$ slope is $a$, not $2a$**

*What students do:* From $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$, read the slope of the $v^2$-$s$ graph as $a$.

*Why it's wrong:* Slope = $2a$. So $a = \text{slope}/2$.

*Fix:* The equation is $v^2 = u^2 + (2a)s$: the coefficient of $s$ in the linear equation is $2a$, not $a$.

---

**Error 21 — Confusing $a$-$t$ area with displacement**

*What students do:* Area under $a$-$t$ graph = displacement.

*Why it's wrong:* Area under $a$-$t$ graph = change in velocity $\Delta v$. Area under $v$-$t$ graph = displacement.

*Fix:* Each integration gives the quantity one "level down": $a \to \Delta v$; $v \to \Delta x$.

---

**Error 22 — Slope of $x$-$t$ graph at a turning point is nonzero**

*What students do:* When an object reverses direction, draw the $x$-$t$ graph with a corner at the turning point.

*Why it's wrong:* At the turning point $v = 0$, so the slope of the $x$-$t$ graph must be zero (horizontal tangent). A corner would imply an instantaneous jump in velocity — infinite acceleration.

*Fix:* The $x$-$t$ graph has a horizontal tangent (smooth maximum or minimum) at every turning point.

---

**Error 23 — Misinterpreting a curve that returns to the $t$-axis as "same position"**

*What students do:* On a $v$-$t$ graph, the curve touches the $t$-axis at $t = 5$ s. Student says "object is back at its starting position."

*Why it's wrong:* On a $v$-$t$ graph, crossing the $t$-axis means $v = 0$ (momentarily at rest). Position is given by area under the curve — not by where the curve is vertically.

*Fix:* $v$-$t$ graph: curve at axis → $v = 0$. Accumulated area → displacement.

---

**Error 24 — Missing the phase difference between $x$-$t$, $v$-$t$, $a$-$t$ for oscillatory motion**

*What students do:* For $x = A\sin\omega t$, draw $v$-$t$ as also $\propto \sin\omega t$ (same phase).

*Why it's wrong:* $v = A\omega\cos\omega t$ — a $90°$ phase shift from $x$. $a = -A\omega^2\sin\omega t$ — $180°$ shifted from $x$.

*Fix:* Differentiation shifts phase by $+90°$ each time (for sinusoidal functions).

---

**Error 25 — Treating straight-line $x$-$t$ graph in a curved region as constant velocity**

*What students do:* Part of an $x$-$t$ graph is an oblique line; student writes $a = 0$ throughout.

*Why it's wrong:* If the graph is truly straight, then yes $a = 0$. But if it only appears straight at the given scale, it could have small $a$.

*Fix:* Only write $a = 0$ if the graph is explicitly stated to be piecewise linear in that region, or if the problem says "uniform motion."

---

**Error 26 — Integrating $v$ instead of $|v|$ for distance on a $v$-$t$ graph**

*(Duplicate emphasis — the most common graph error)*

**Error 27 — Forgetting to add the initial position when finding final position from $v$-$t$ area**

*What students do:* Compute area under $v$-$t$ curve from 0 to $T$ and call it the final position.

*Why it's wrong:* Area = displacement = $x(T) - x(0)$. Final position $= x(0) + $ area.

*Fix:* Final position $= x_0 + \int_0^T v\,dt$.

---

**Error 28 — Wrong area formula for non-triangular regions**

*What students do:* For a $v$-$t$ graph showing a parabolic curve, calculate the area as if it were a triangle or trapezoid.

*Why it's wrong:* Only straight-line $v$-$t$ segments give triangles/trapezoids. Curved segments require integration.

*Fix:* If the graph is curved, use $\int v\,dt$. Geometric area formulas only for straight-line segments.

---

### CATEGORY C — Projectile Motion (Errors 29–45)

---

**Error 29 — Using $T = 2u\sin\theta/g$ when landing height $\neq$ launch height**

Already covered in Section 11 traps. The most common projectile error.

---

**Error 30 — Claiming $\theta = 45°$ gives maximum range for all situations**

*Fix:* $45°$ is optimal only for launch and landing at the same height. For elevated launch or inclined surfaces, the optimal angle changes.

---

**Error 31 — Forgetting horizontal velocity is constant**

*What students do:* In projectile problems, compute the final velocity by finding only $v_y$ and ignoring $v_x$.

*Why it's wrong:* The full velocity is $\vec{v} = v_x\hat{i} + v_y\hat{j}$. The horizontal component $v_x = u\cos\theta$ is constant throughout.

*Fix:* Always assemble both components. Final speed $= \sqrt{v_x^2 + v_y^2}$.

---

**Error 32 — Confusing time to peak with total time of flight**

*Fix:* Time to peak = $u\sin\theta/g$. Total time = $2u\sin\theta/g$ (twice the peak time) — only for same-height landing.

---

**Error 33 — Trajectory formula used with horizontal acceleration**

*Fix:* $y = x\tan\theta - gx^2/(2u^2\cos^2\theta)$ requires $a_x = 0$. If horizontal force acts, derive trajectory from scratch.

---

**Error 34 — Computing range on incline using flat-ground formula**

*Fix:* On inclined plane, use $R_{slope} = 2u^2\sin\alpha\cos(\alpha+\beta)/(g\cos^2\beta)$, not $u^2\sin2\theta/g$.

---

**Error 35 — Forgetting that complementary angles give same range but different everything else**

*Fix:* Heights and times are different for $\theta$ and $90°-\theta$. Only range is the same.

---

**Error 36 — Speed at a point depends on horizontal position as well as height**

*What students do:* At the same height, claim the speed is the same at two different horizontal positions.

*Why it's wrong:* Speed depends only on height: $v = \sqrt{u^2 - 2gh}$. This is correct — speed at the same height IS the same. But students sometimes mistakenly think position affects it.

*Fix:* Speed at height $h$: $v = \sqrt{u^2-2gh}$, independent of horizontal position — but only valid for the same projectile (same $u$).

---

**Error 37 — Radius of curvature at peak is $u^2/g$, not $u^2/(2g)$**

*Fix:* $\rho_{top} = v_{top}^2/g = (u\cos\theta)^2/g = u^2\cos^2\theta/g$. At $\theta = 45°$: $\rho = u^2/(2g)$, not $u^2/g$.

---

**Error 38 — Using initial speed instead of horizontal speed in $\rho_{top}$ formula**

*Fix:* At the top, only horizontal speed $v_x = u\cos\theta$ remains. Use that, not $u$.

---

**Error 39 — Neglecting the launch angle when thrown from moving platform**

*Fix:* In the ground frame: $u_{x,total} = v_{platform} + u\cos\theta$; $u_y = u\sin\theta$. Use these initial conditions.

---

**Error 40 — Two balls reach the same point simultaneously: forgetting two equations**

*What students do:* Equate x-positions but forget to also equate y-positions (or times).

*Fix:* Two objects meet when BOTH $x_A(t) = x_B(t)$ AND $y_A(t) = y_B(t)$ at the SAME $t$. This gives two equations.

---

**Error 41 — "At the same height" means same $y$, not same absolute elevation if origins differ**

*Fix:* If object A starts at $(0,0)$ and object B at $(0, H)$, "same height" means $y_A(t) = H + y_B(t)$ if B's origin is at height $H$.

---

**Error 42 — Applying the envelope formula to a specific trajectory**

*Fix:* The parabola of safety $y = u^2/2g - gx^2/2u^2$ is the envelope (boundary of reachable region), not a specific trajectory.

---

**Error 43 — Claiming the ball takes longer to fall than to rise when $g$ is constant**

*Fix:* With constant $g$, rise time = fall time (time-reversal symmetry). Unequal times arise only with air resistance or variable $g$.

---

**Error 44 — Getting the angle wrong: mixing "angle from horizontal" and "angle from vertical"**

*Fix:* Standard: $\theta$ is measured from horizontal. $u_x = u\cos\theta$, $u_y = u\sin\theta$. If given "from vertical," interchange $\sin$ and $\cos$.

---

**Error 45 — Forgetting to convert $rpm$ to $rad/s$ before using $v = r\omega$**

*Fix:* $\omega\,[\text{rad/s}] = 2\pi \times f\,[\text{Hz}] = 2\pi \times N/60$ where $N$ is in rpm.

---

### CATEGORY D — Relative Motion (Errors 46–58)

---

**Error 46 — Subtracting in the wrong order**

*Fix:* $\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B$. "Velocity of A relative to B" — subtract B's velocity from A's. Not the other way around.

---

**Error 47 — River-boat: using $v_b + v_r$ as the crossing speed**

*Fix:* The boat aims perpendicular to the bank. The crossing speed (perpendicular component) is $v_b$ alone. The river current ($v_r$) is parallel to the bank and causes drift — it does NOT speed up or slow down the crossing.

---

**Error 48 — Using crossing time formula when the boat aims for zero drift**

*Fix:* For zero drift, the boat aims upstream at $\sin\alpha = v_r/v_b$. The crossing speed (perpendicular) is $v_b\cos\alpha = \sqrt{v_b^2-v_r^2}$. Time = $d/\sqrt{v_b^2-v_r^2}$, NOT $d/v_b$.

---

**Error 49 — Rain problem: tilting umbrella in the wrong direction**

*Fix:* The rain appears to come from the direction you are moving toward. Tilt the umbrella toward your direction of motion — forward, not backward.

---

**Error 50 — Minimum distance formula: forgetting to check $t^* > 0$**

*Fix:* $t^* = -(\vec{d}_0\cdot\vec{v}_{rel})/|\vec{v}_{rel}|^2$. If $t^* < 0$, the closest approach was in the past. The minimum future distance is $|\vec{d}_0|$ (the current separation).

---

**Error 51 — Relative acceleration: forgetting both objects fall under gravity**

*Fix:* If both A and B are in free fall, $\vec{a}_{A/B} = \vec{a}_A - \vec{a}_B = (-g\hat{j}) - (-g\hat{j}) = 0$. They have zero relative acceleration.

---

**Error 52 — Head-on collision: using $v_r = v_1 - v_2$ instead of $v_1 + v_2$**

*Fix:* Objects moving toward each other: relative speed = $v_1 + v_2$. Objects moving in the same direction: relative speed = $|v_1 - v_2|$.

---

**Error 53 — Confusing aircraft heading with aircraft track**

*Fix:* Heading = direction the aircraft nose points (direction of $\vec{v}_{aircraft/air}$). Track = actual direction of movement over the ground (direction of $\vec{v}_{aircraft/ground}$). These differ when wind blows.

---

**Error 54 — Not accounting for wind when adding aircraft velocities**

*Fix:* $\vec{v}_{aircraft/ground} = \vec{v}_{aircraft/air} + \vec{v}_{air/ground}$. Always add the wind velocity to the airspeed.

---

**Error 55 — In monkey-gun: forgetting monkey is in free fall**

*Fix:* Monkey drops at the instant the gun fires — it undergoes free fall ($a = -g$). The bullet also has $a = -g$. Relative acceleration = 0 → bullet always hits.

---

**Error 56 — Relative velocity in 2D: taking magnitudes of components separately**

*What students do:* $v_{A/B} = |v_{Ax} - v_{Bx}| + |v_{Ay} - v_{By}|$.

*Fix:* $|\vec{v}_{A/B}| = \sqrt{(v_{Ax}-v_{Bx})^2 + (v_{Ay}-v_{By})^2}$.

---

**Error 57 — Velocity of rain relative to man = velocity of man relative to rain**

*Fix:* $\vec{v}_{rain/man} = \vec{v}_{rain} - \vec{v}_{man}$. $\vec{v}_{man/rain} = \vec{v}_{man} - \vec{v}_{rain} = -\vec{v}_{rain/man}$. Opposite sign.

---

**Error 58 — "Same speed relative to each other" means same absolute speed**

*Fix:* If $|\vec{v}_{A/B}| = 5$ m/s, it means A moves at 5 m/s relative to B. Their absolute speeds could be anything. Example: $v_A = 3$ m/s right, $v_B = 2$ m/s right → $v_{A/B} = 1$ m/s right; but $v_A = 100$, $v_B = 95$ → also $v_{A/B} = 5$ m/s.

---

### CATEGORY E — Circular Motion (Errors 59–72)

---

**Error 59 — Centrifugal force in the inertial frame**

*Fix:* Centrifugal force does not exist in the inertial (ground) frame. It is a pseudo-force in the rotating frame only. In the ground frame, only the centripetal force (real, inward) acts.

---

**Error 60 — Centripetal acceleration has a component that changes speed**

*Fix:* Centripetal acceleration is always perpendicular to velocity. It cannot change speed — only direction.

---

**Error 61 — $v = r\omega$ in degrees instead of radians**

*Fix:* $\omega$ must be in rad/s for $v = r\omega$. Convert: $\omega\,[\text{rad/s}] = 2\pi\times f$ or $2\pi\times N/60$ for rpm.

---

**Error 62 — Confusing period and frequency**

*Fix:* $T$ = time for one revolution (seconds). $f$ = revolutions per second (Hz). $T = 1/f$, $\omega = 2\pi f = 2\pi/T$.

---

**Error 63 — Centripetal acceleration direction on a graph**

*Fix:* On a circle, centripetal acceleration always points toward the centre. At the rightmost point, it points left. At the top, it points down. At the bottom, it points up.

---

**Error 64 — Using the equation of motion for angular quantities with $\omega$ in rpm**

*Fix:* $\omega = \omega_0 + \alpha t$ requires $\omega$ and $\alpha$ in rad/s and rad/s² respectively. Convert before substituting.

---

**Error 65 — Confusing centripetal force with centrifugal force**

*Fix:* Centripetal force is the real inward force that maintains circular motion (tension, friction, gravity, etc.). Centrifugal force is a pseudo-force in the rotating frame, directed outward. Only one is "real" in the inertial frame.

---

**Error 66 — Minimum speed at top of vertical circle: using $v_{min} = 0$**

*Fix:* Minimum speed at top for maintaining contact (string taut or track contact): $v_{min} = \sqrt{gR}$, not 0. (At $v = 0$, the required centripetal force is 0, but the string goes slack before that since string can only pull, not push.)

---

**Error 67 — Minimum speed at bottom of vertical circle: using $v_{min} = \sqrt{2gR}$**

*Fix:* Minimum speed at bottom for completing the loop is $\sqrt{5gR}$, derived from energy conservation between bottom and top.

---

**Error 68 — For non-uniform circular motion: $a = v^2/R$ gives total acceleration**

*Fix:* In non-uniform circular motion, $a_c = v^2/R$ gives only the centripetal (radial) component. The tangential component $a_t = dv/dt$ is additional. Total: $|\vec{a}| = \sqrt{a_c^2 + a_t^2}$.

---

**Error 69 — Period of conical pendulum equals $2\pi\sqrt{L/g}$ (like simple pendulum)**

*Fix:* Conical pendulum: $T = 2\pi\sqrt{L\cos\theta/g}$. Simple pendulum: $T = 2\pi\sqrt{L/g}$ (small angle). The $\cos\theta$ factor is crucial.

---

**Error 70 — Banking angle: using $\tan\phi = v^2/(Rg)$ with $v$ in km/h**

*Fix:* Use SI units. Convert $v$ to m/s before substituting into $\tan\phi = v^2/(Rg)$.

---

**Error 71 — The acceleration of a particle in UCM is zero because speed is constant**

*Fix:* Speed (magnitude of velocity) is constant, but velocity (direction) changes continuously. Therefore $\vec{a} = d\vec{v}/dt \neq 0$. UCM is not uniform motion.

---

**Error 72 — Treating angular displacement in degrees in $\omega^2 = \omega_0^2 + 2\alpha\theta$**

*Fix:* $\theta$ must be in radians. Convert before using.

---

### CATEGORY F — Problem-Solving Strategy (Errors 73–85)

---

**Error 73 — Not drawing a diagram**

*Why it matters:* Most vector direction errors disappear when you draw a clear diagram with labelled axes, initial velocity components, and acceleration arrows.

*Fix:* Draw a diagram for every problem. Label everything. This takes 30 seconds and saves 5 minutes.

---

**Error 74 — Not stating the sign convention**

*Fix:* Write "Upward positive, downward negative" or "rightward positive" at the start of every problem. Stick to it.

---

**Error 75 — Setting up the problem in the wrong frame**

*Fix:* Choose the inertial (ground) frame by default. If the problem is simpler in another frame, explicitly state the transformation and verify consistency.

---

**Error 76 — Not checking units/dimensions of intermediate steps**

*Fix:* After every major calculation, check that the units match what's expected. If you're computing a time and get metres, there's an error somewhere.

---

**Error 77 — Applying a formula that eliminated time when time is needed**

*Fix:* Match the given information to the right equation. If time is required, don't use $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$.

---

**Error 78 — Not checking the answer with a limiting case**

*Fix:* After getting an answer, check at least one limiting case (e.g., $\theta = 0$ or $\theta = 90°$). If the limit gives a wrong known result, there's an error.

---

**Error 79 — Over-relying on memorised formulas without understanding derivation**

*Why it matters:* JEE Advanced creates variants where the standard formula doesn't apply directly. Knowing the derivation lets you re-derive for the modified case.

*Fix:* For every formula you use, know: what assumptions it rests on, how it's derived, and what the limiting cases are.

---

**Error 80 — Solving two-body problems without explicitly writing both position functions**

*Fix:* Write $x_A(t)$ and $x_B(t)$ explicitly. Set them equal. Never try to do this "in your head."

---

**Error 81 — Treating "meets" and "overtakes" as the same event**

*Fix:* "Meets" = same position at some instant. "Overtakes" = the trailing object passes the leading object (goes from behind to ahead). Overtaking happens at the second intersection of position functions (the first is where the leading object laps the trailing one).

---

**Error 82 — Forgetting the initial position when computing meeting time**

*Fix:* If object A starts at $x_0 \neq 0$, use $x_A(t) = x_0 + u_A t + \tfrac{1}{2}a_A t^2$. Don't set $x_0 = 0$ unless the problem places the origin at A's starting position.

---

**Error 83 — Not splitting a multi-stage problem properly**

*Fix:* Identify phase boundaries (where acceleration changes). Solve each phase in sequence. Carry forward the position and velocity from each phase as initial conditions for the next.

---

**Error 84 — Squaring both sides and introducing an extraneous solution**

*Fix:* After solving $v = u + at$ type equations or quadratics, check each solution in the original equation. Extraneous solutions arise when squaring introduces phantom roots.

---

**Error 85 — Premature rounding of intermediate values**

*Fix:* Keep exact (fraction or surd) values throughout the calculation. Only convert to decimal in the final answer. Rounding $\sqrt{3} = 1.732$ too early compounds errors.

---

### CATEGORY G — JEE Advanced Conceptual Traps (Errors 86–100)

---

**Error 86 — "The ball is weightless at the top of the arc"**

*Fix:* The ball is NOT weightless — gravity acts on it throughout. At the top, $v_y = 0$ but $a = g$ downward. Weightlessness would mean zero net gravitational force, which doesn't happen here.

---

**Error 87 — Forgetting that velocity is tangent to trajectory at every point**

*Fix:* At any instant, the velocity vector is tangent to the path. It is not along the position vector, not toward the target, and not in any fixed direction — it follows the curve.

---

**Error 88 — Missing the constraint in Atwood machine kinematics**

*Fix:* For a simple Atwood machine: $|a_1| = |a_2|$. For compound (double) Atwood: $a_1 + a_2 = 2a_P$. Set up the string-length constraint explicitly.

---

**Error 89 — Claiming that "uniform motion" and "uniform speed" are the same**

*Fix:* Uniform motion = constant velocity (constant speed AND direction) → straight-line path. Uniform speed = constant speed, direction may change → e.g., UCM.

---

**Error 90 — Radius of curvature formula: using total acceleration, not perpendicular component**

*Fix:* $\rho = v^2/a_\perp$ where $a_\perp$ is the component of acceleration perpendicular to velocity. NOT $v^2/|\vec{a}|$ unless $\vec{a}$ is entirely perpendicular to $\vec{v}$.

---

**Error 91 — In SHM: claiming max acceleration occurs at max velocity**

*Fix:* In SHM, $v$ is maximum at the equilibrium (zero displacement) and $a$ is maximum at the extremes (maximum displacement). They are $90°$ out of phase.

---

**Error 92 — Forgetting $\Delta r$ in the limit: "average acceleration at a point"**

*Fix:* Instantaneous acceleration is the limit of $\Delta\vec{v}/\Delta t$ as $\Delta t \to 0$. Average acceleration over an interval is $\Delta\vec{v}/\Delta t$. At a point, only instantaneous makes sense.

---

**Error 93 — Position-time graph: thinking a smooth maximum means $v = 0$ AND $a = 0$**

*Fix:* At a local maximum/minimum of $x(t)$: $v = dx/dt = 0$ ✓. But $a = d^2x/dt^2$ can be nonzero (and usually is — otherwise it wouldn't be a turning point). At a smooth maximum: $a < 0$ (concave down).

---

**Error 94 — For two objects thrown at each other: forgetting to use relative velocity**

*Fix:* When two balls are thrown directly at each other (same line, opposite directions), use relative speed = $v_1 + v_2$ for time-to-meet problems.

---

**Error 95 — The parabolic trajectory equation is valid for negative $x$**

*Fix:* The trajectory $y = x\tan\theta - gx^2/(2u^2\cos^2\theta)$ is physically valid only for $0 \leq x \leq R$ (from launch to landing). Extending it to $x < 0$ or $x > R$ has no physical meaning.

---

**Error 96 — Confusing "component of velocity along a direction" with "speed in that direction"**

*Fix:* These are the same thing: the component of $\vec{v}$ along a unit vector $\hat{n}$ is $\vec{v}\cdot\hat{n}$. This is a signed scalar — it can be negative (moving opposite to $\hat{n}$).

---

**Error 97 — Ignoring the constraint that the projectile must not hit the inclined plane on the way up**

*Fix:* On an inclined plane, the projectile must land on the surface (positive range). Verify that the flight path doesn't intersect the surface before the intended landing point.

---

**Error 98 — Time of flight on inclined plane: using $T = 2u\sin\theta/g$ instead of the incline formula**

*Fix:* On inclined plane with slope $\beta$, time of flight is $T = 2u\sin\alpha/(g\cos\beta)$, NOT $2u\sin\theta/g$.

---

**Error 99 — Centripetal acceleration of the Earth around the Sun is $v^2/R$, not $v^2/(R^2)$**

*Fix:* Centripetal acceleration = $v^2/R$ where $R$ is the orbital radius. Newton's gravity provides $GM_s/R^2$. Setting equal: $v^2/R = GM_s/R^2$ gives $v = \sqrt{GM_s/R}$. The formula $v^2/R$ is correct.

---

**Error 100 — "Faster is always farther in projectile motion"**

*Fix:* FALSE. Range depends on $\sin2\theta$ as well as $u$. A ball thrown at $\theta = 89°$ with large $u$ has a tiny range (nearly straight up). A ball thrown at $\theta = 45°$ with smaller $u$ may have a larger range.

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section Summary**

100 errors in 7 categories:

- **Fundamentals (1–15):** sign conventions, $v \neq 0$ when $a = 0$, distance vs displacement, variable-$a$ misuse
- **Graphs (16–28):** slope/area confusion, $v^2$-$s$ factor-of-2, intersection vs equal velocity, distance vs displacement from $v$-$t$ graph
- **Projectile (29–45):** landing-height assumption, constant $v_x$, trajectory formula limits, incline formula
- **Relative motion (46–58):** subtraction order, river-boat components, minimum-distance $t^*$ check
- **Circular motion (59–72):** centrifugal pseudo-force, non-uniform acceleration decomposition, radian conversion
- **Strategy (73–85):** diagram, sign convention, frame choice, multi-stage processing, formula-matching
- **JEE Advanced traps (86–100):** weightlessness at arc top, velocity tangency, Atwood constraint, parabola of safety scope

For each error lost on a mock test: locate its category here, re-read the relevant theory section, and solve 5 similar problems before moving on.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 15 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 16: Visual Learning Module (SVG Diagrams).*

## Section 16: Visual Learning Module

> **How to use this section:** Each diagram below captures a core kinematics concept in a single image. Study the diagram first, try to reconstruct the physics from it, then read the caption and interpretation notes. This trains the visual-to-equation translation skill that JEE Advanced questions heavily test.

---

### 16.1 Distance vs Displacement — At a Glance

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/displacement-distance.svg"
  alt="Curved path from A to B showing distance (path length) vs displacement (straight line)"
  caption="Distance is the total path length (red curve). Displacement is the straight-line vector from start to finish (blue dashed arrow). They are equal only when the path is straight with no reversals."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
