- The red curve can be arbitrarily long; the blue arrow is always the shortest route.
- $|\vec{\Delta r}| \leq d$ — equality holds for straight-line, one-direction motion only.
- If the object returns to start: displacement $= 0$, distance $> 0$.
- **Exam trigger:** "Find distance and displacement" — always draw the path, not just the start/end points.

---

### 16.2 Position Vector

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/position-vector.svg"
  alt="2D coordinate axes with position vector r from origin O to point P"
  caption="Position vector r⃗ from origin O to point P(x, y). The x and y components are shown as dashed projection lines. The magnitude |r⃗| = √(x² + y²)."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- The position vector always originates from the coordinate **origin**, not from a previous position.
- The displacement vector $\vec{\Delta r} = \vec{r}_2 - \vec{r}_1$ originates from $P_1$, not from $O$.
- In 3D: $|\vec{r}| = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2 + z^2}$ — just add the $z$-component under the square root.

---

### 16.3 x-t Graphs — Four Motion Types

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/xt-graph.svg"
  alt="Four x-t graph curves: at rest (horizontal), uniform motion (straight line), uniformly accelerating (upward curve), and decelerating (downward curve)"
  caption="A: At rest — flat line. B: Uniform velocity — straight slope. C: Accelerating — concave up (slope increasing). D: Decelerating — concave down (slope decreasing). Slope = instantaneous velocity."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- **Slope of x-t = velocity.** Steeper slope → higher speed; negative slope → moving in $-x$ direction.
- **Concavity = sign of acceleration.** Concave up ($\cup$) → $a > 0$; concave down ($\cap$) → $a < 0$.
- A **horizontal** x-t line means the object is stationary (slope = 0 = velocity).
- An **inflection point** on x-t means acceleration changes sign at that instant.
- The x-t graph **cannot** have a vertical segment (that would mean infinite velocity).

**Practice reading:** At any point on an x-t graph, draw the tangent line. Its slope gives the instantaneous velocity at that moment — positive if tilted right-upward, negative if tilted right-downward.

---

### 16.4 v-t Graphs — Area and Slope

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/vt-graph.svg"
  alt="Piecewise v-t graph with four phases: acceleration, constant velocity, deceleration, reverse motion. Shaded areas represent displacement."
  caption="Slope of v-t = acceleration. Shaded area under v-t = displacement (signed). Area above t-axis is positive displacement; area below is negative. Total distance = sum of absolute areas."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- **Slope of v-t = acceleration.** Steeper positive slope → greater acceleration; negative slope → deceleration (or reverse acceleration).
- **Area under v-t = displacement** (signed). Area above the time axis: positive displacement. Area below: negative displacement.
- **Area under |v-t| = distance** (unsigned). When the graph dips below the t-axis, take the absolute value of that area.
- The velocity is zero where the v-t graph crosses the t-axis — the object momentarily stops.
- A **horizontal** v-t line means constant velocity ($a = 0$).
- A **straight sloped** v-t line means uniform acceleration (the standard SUVAT regime).

**JEE trap:** "Find displacement from $t = 0$ to $t = T$" when the graph crosses the axis. Many students forget to flip the sign of the below-axis area. Always note the sign!

---

### 16.5 Projectile Motion — The Full Picture

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/projectile-motion.svg"
  alt="Parabolic projectile trajectory with launch angle θ, maximum height H, range R, and velocity vectors at launch, peak, and landing"
  caption="Horizontal velocity vₓ = u cos θ is constant throughout. Vertical velocity vᵧ = u sin θ − gt decreases to 0 at peak, then increases downward. The trajectory is a parabola."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- At the **peak**: $v_y = 0$, but $v_x = u\cos\theta \neq 0$. Speed is minimum at peak, not zero.
- The trajectory is **symmetric** about the vertical through the peak (if landing height = launch height).
- **Range formula** $R = u^2\sin2\theta/g$ is maximised at $\theta = 45°$.
- Complementary angles ($\theta$ and $90° - \theta$) give the **same range**.
- Velocity vectors at landing: $v_x$ is unchanged; $v_y$ has same magnitude as at launch but points downward.
- The **parabolic path**: $y = x\tan\theta - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2\cos^2\theta}$ — memorise the form; the exam may ask you to identify it.

**Diagram reading drill:** At any point on the parabola, decompose the velocity vector into horizontal (rightward, constant) and vertical (downward, increasing) components. This decomposition is always valid.

---

### 16.6 River-Boat Problem

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/river-boat.svg"
  alt="River with two crossing cases: minimum time (aim perpendicular) and zero drift (aim upstream at angle)"
  caption="Left case: boat aims perpendicular to current → minimum crossing time t = d/v_boat, but drifts downstream. Right case: boat aims upstream → zero lateral drift, longer crossing time."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- **Case 1 — Minimum time:** Aim the boat perpendicular to the banks. Crossing time $t_{min} = d/v_b$ (only the boat's speed matters). Drift $= v_r \cdot t_{min}$ downstream.
- **Case 2 — Zero drift:** Aim upstream at angle $\theta = \sin^{-1}(v_r/v_b)$ so the river current exactly cancels lateral velocity. Only possible if $v_b > v_r$.
- The resultant velocity is always the **vector sum** of boat velocity (relative to water) and river current (relative to ground).
- When $v_b < v_r$: zero-drift crossing is impossible; the minimum drift angle is found by calculus.
- **Exam diagram tip:** Always draw the velocity triangle with: (1) boat vector relative to water, (2) water velocity, (3) resultant relative to ground.

---

### 16.7 Rain and Moving Person

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/rain-person.svg"
  alt="Stick figure with tilted umbrella, vertical rain drops, and relative rain velocity vector"
  caption="Rain falls vertically at speed v_R. Person walks forward at v_P. Relative velocity of rain w.r.t. person tilts forward. Tilt angle: tan θ = v_P/v_R. Umbrella tilts at angle θ forward."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- $\vec{v}_{rain/person} = \vec{v}_{rain} - \vec{v}_{person}$. Rain is vertical ($-v_R\hat{j}$); person is horizontal ($+v_P\hat{i}$).
- Result: $\vec{v}_{rain/person} = -v_P\hat{i} - v_R\hat{j}$ — rain appears to come from the **front**.
- Tilt the umbrella forward by $\theta = \tan^{-1}(v_P/v_R)$ measured from vertical.
- If the person turns and walks backward, rain appears to come from **behind** — tilt umbrella backward.
- **Exam variation:** Find how hard rain seems to hit — this is $|\vec{v}_{rain/person}| = \sqrt{v_P^2 + v_R^2}$.

---

### 16.8 Uniform Circular Motion — Velocity and Acceleration

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/circular-motion.svg"
  alt="Circle with particle showing tangential velocity vector (green) and centripetal acceleration vector (red) pointing inward"
  caption="Velocity is always tangent to the circle (green). Centripetal acceleration always points toward the centre (red). They are perpendicular at every instant."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- Velocity is **tangential** — it is always $90°$ to the radius at the point of contact.
- Centripetal acceleration is **radial inward** — it changes direction, not speed.
- The velocity vector rotates at the same rate as the position vector: angular velocity $\omega$ is the same for both.
- Speed is constant in uniform circular motion; velocity is not (direction changes continuously).
- **Magnitude:** $a_c = v^2/R = \omega^2 R$; $v = \omega R$.

**Exam drill:** Draw a particle on a circle. Draw the velocity tangent to the circle. Draw the centripetal acceleration toward the center. These two vectors are always perpendicular — if your diagram has them at any other angle, it is wrong.

---

### 16.9 Non-Uniform Circular Motion — Net Acceleration

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/non-uniform-circular.svg"
  alt="Circle with particle showing tangential acceleration, centripetal acceleration, and net acceleration vectors"
  caption="When speed changes, a tangential component aₜ = d|v|/dt appears. Net acceleration has both centripetal (inward, red) and tangential (along velocity, green) components. |a_net| = √(aₜ² + aᶜ²)."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- Centripetal acceleration $a_c = v^2/R$: always inward, even when speed changes.
- Tangential acceleration $a_t = d|v|/dt$: along the velocity direction (speeding up) or opposite (slowing down).
- Net acceleration points **between** the radius and the tangent — neither purely inward nor purely tangential.
- The angle of net acceleration from the radius: $\tan\phi = a_t/a_c$.
- In uniform circular motion: $a_t = 0$, so net $a$ = centripetal only.

---

### 16.10 Free Fall — Time Symmetry

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/free-fall.svg"
  alt="Ball positions going up (blue) and coming down (red) showing equal time intervals and height symmetry"
  caption="Blue: ball going up with decreasing spacing (decelerating). Red: ball coming down with increasing spacing (accelerating). Time to reach peak = time to fall from peak. Speed at any height is the same going up and coming down."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- **Equal time up and down** (for same launch and landing height): $T_{up} = T_{down} = u/g$.
- **Equal speed** at any given height: the speed going up equals the speed coming down at the same height.
- The spacing between successive equal-time positions **decreases** going up (decelerating), **increases** coming down (accelerating).
- This symmetry breaks if air resistance is present — then the object decelerates faster going up than it accelerates coming down.
- **Exam implication:** If a ball is thrown up and caught at the same height, its speed when caught equals its launch speed. Total time in air $= 2u/g$.

---

### 16.11 Relative Velocity — Vector Subtraction

<Diagram
  src="/diagrams/kinematics/relative-velocity.svg"
  alt="Two objects A and B moving in the same direction, with relative velocity shown as vector difference"
  caption="Relative velocity of A with respect to B is v_AB = v_A - v_B (vector subtraction). Same direction: |v_AB| = |v_A - v_B|. Opposite directions: |v_AB| = v_A + v_B."
/>

**Key visual lessons:**
- Always subtract: $\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B$ (not the other way around).
- **Same direction:** relative speed = difference of speeds (they seem slow relative to each other).
- **Opposite directions:** relative speed = sum of speeds (they close in fast).
- In 2D: resolve into components first, subtract, then find magnitude and direction.
- **Frame shift trick:** In the reference frame of B, object A moves with $\vec{v}_{A/B}$. This simplifies many collision and pursuit problems.

---

### 16.12 Bringing It All Together — The Visual Checklist

Before solving any kinematics problem, run through this visual checklist:

<Callout type="tip">
**The 5-Second Diagram Protocol**

1. **Draw the situation** — trajectory, initial position, final position, key points.
2. **Mark vectors** — velocity at each key point, acceleration (constant? changing direction?).
3. **Choose axes** — align $x$ with initial velocity for projectile; align with the bank for river-boat.
4. **Identify the independent motions** — what happens in x? What happens in y?
5. **Find the shared constraint** — usually time $t$ links both axes.

A diagram drawn in 5 seconds eliminates 80% of sign errors and missing-component mistakes.
</Callout>

**Pattern recognition from diagrams:**

| If you see in the diagram... | The physics says... |
|---|---|
| Parabolic trajectory | Projectile motion, $a_x = 0$, $a_y = -g$ |
| Equal-time positions getting farther apart | Accelerating (uniform $a$) |
| Equal-time positions equally spaced | Constant velocity |
| Circle with tangent arrow | Circular motion; $v$ is tangent |
| Two arrows, one subtracted from other | Relative velocity problem |
| River + crossing boat + drift | Vector triangle for velocity |
| Ball thrown up, same landing height | Use time symmetry: $T = 2u\sin\theta/g$ |
| $v$-$t$ graph area | Displacement (signed) = $\int v\,dt$ |

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section 16 Summary — Visual Learning**

Eleven SVG diagrams created for this module:

1. **Distance vs Displacement** — path vs straight-line, $|\Delta r| \leq d$
2. **Position Vector** — components, magnitude, distinction from displacement
3. **x-t Graphs** — four motion archetypes, slope = velocity, concavity = acceleration sign
4. **v-t Graphs** — slope = acceleration, area = displacement (signed), absolute area = distance
5. **Projectile Motion** — constant $v_x$, parabolic path, peak properties, complementary angles
6. **River-Boat** — minimum time vs zero-drift cases, velocity triangle
7. **Rain-Person** — relative velocity as vector subtraction, umbrella tilt angle
8. **Uniform Circular Motion** — tangential velocity, centripetal acceleration, perpendicularity
9. **Non-Uniform Circular** — tangential + centripetal components, net acceleration
10. **Free Fall** — time symmetry, equal speed at equal heights
11. **Relative Velocity** — vector subtraction, same vs opposite directions

**Study strategy:** Close this page. Sketch each diagram from memory. If any detail is fuzzy, return and study that specific diagram again before moving to Section 17.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 16 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 17: Solved Examples (50 JEE Main + 30 JEE Advanced).*

## Section 17: Solved Examples

> **50 JEE Main + 30 JEE Advanced fully solved examples.** Each solution shows every step — the working you must reproduce under exam conditions. Problems are ordered from foundational to complex within each category.

---

## Part A: JEE Main Solved Examples (1–50)

### Category 1: Distance, Displacement, Speed, Velocity (Examples 1–10)

---

**Example 1.** A particle moves along a semicircular arc of radius $R$ from point A to point B (diametrically opposite). Find (a) the distance travelled and (b) the magnitude of displacement.

**Solution:**

(a) The path is a semicircle, so:
$$d = \pi R$$

(b) The displacement is the straight line from A to B, which is the diameter:
$$|\vec{\Delta r}| = 2R$$

**Note:** $|\vec{\Delta r}|/d = 2/\pi \approx 0.637$ — displacement is about 64% of distance for a semicircular path.

**Answer:** Distance $= \pi R$; Displacement $= 2R$.

---

**Example 2.** A person walks 40 m due north, then 30 m due east. Find (a) total distance travelled and (b) magnitude and direction of displacement.

**Solution:**

(a) Distance $= 40 + 30 = 70$ m (total path length).

(b) The two legs are perpendicular, so by Pythagoras:
$$|\vec{\Delta r}| = \sqrt{40^2 + 30^2} = \sqrt{1600 + 900} = \sqrt{2500} = 50 \text{ m}$$

Direction from north toward east:
$$\theta = \tan^{-1}\!\left(\frac{30}{40}\right) = \tan^{-1}(0.75) \approx 37° \text{ east of north}$$

**Answer:** Distance $= 70$ m; Displacement $= 50$ m at $37°$ east of north.

---

**Example 3.** A car travels from A to B (100 km) at 50 km/h, then returns from B to A at 100 km/h. Find (a) average speed and (b) average velocity for the entire journey.

**Solution:**

(a) Time A→B: $t_1 = 100/50 = 2$ h. Time B→A: $t_2 = 100/100 = 1$ h.
Total distance $= 200$ km, total time $= 3$ h.
$$\bar{v} = \frac{200}{3} \approx 66.7 \text{ km/h}$$

(b) Total displacement = 0 (returns to start), so:
$$\bar{v}_{vector} = 0$$

**Common error:** Students take $(50 + 100)/2 = 75$ km/h. Wrong — that only works if equal times are spent at each speed, not equal distances.

**Answer:** Average speed $\approx 66.7$ km/h; Average velocity $= 0$.

---

**Example 4.** The position of a particle is given by $x(t) = 3t^2 - 2t + 1$ (SI units). Find (a) instantaneous velocity at $t = 2$ s, (b) average velocity between $t = 0$ and $t = 2$ s.

**Solution:**

(a) $v(t) = \frac{dx}{dt} = 6t - 2$

At $t = 2$ s: $v = 6(2) - 2 = \boxed{10}$ m/s

(b) $x(0) = 1$ m; $x(2) = 3(4) - 2(2) + 1 = 12 - 4 + 1 = 9$ m

$$\bar{v} = \frac{x(2) - x(0)}{2 - 0} = \frac{9 - 1}{2} = 4 \text{ m/s}$$

**Answer:** Instantaneous velocity at $t = 2$ s is 10 m/s; average velocity $= 4$ m/s.

---

**Example 5.** A particle moves 8 m east, then 6 m north, then 8 m west. Find (a) total distance, (b) displacement magnitude.

**Solution:**

(a) Distance $= 8 + 6 + 8 = 22$ m.

(b) Net east-west displacement $= 8 - 8 = 0$. Net north displacement $= 6$ m.
$$|\vec{\Delta r}| = \sqrt{0^2 + 6^2} = 6 \text{ m due north}$$

**Answer:** Distance $= 22$ m; Displacement $= 6$ m due north.

---

**Example 6.** Velocity of a particle: $v(t) = 4 - 2t$ m/s. Find (a) when the particle stops, (b) distance travelled in $t = 0$ to $t = 4$ s.

**Solution:**

(a) $v = 0$: $4 - 2t = 0 \Rightarrow t = 2$ s.

(b) From $t = 0$ to $t = 2$ s (moving in $+x$):
$$x_1 = \int_0^2 (4 - 2t)\,dt = \left[4t - t^2\right]_0^2 = 8 - 4 = 4 \text{ m}$$

From $t = 2$ to $t = 4$ s (moving in $-x$, speed $= |4 - 2t|$):
$$x_2 = \left|\int_2^4 (4-2t)\,dt\right| = \left|\left[4t - t^2\right]_2^4\right| = |(16-16) - (8-4)| = |-4| = 4 \text{ m}$$

Total distance $= 4 + 4 = 8$ m. (Displacement $= 4 - 4 = 0$ m.)

**Answer:** Stops at $t = 2$ s; distance $= 8$ m; displacement $= 0$.

---

**Example 7.** A particle starts from rest and moves with acceleration $a = 3t$ m/s². Find its velocity and position at $t = 4$ s.

**Solution:**

$$v(t) = \int_0^t 3t'\,dt' = \frac{3t^2}{2}$$

At $t = 4$ s: $v = \frac{3 \times 16}{2} = 24$ m/s

$$x(t) = \int_0^t \frac{3t'^2}{2}\,dt' = \frac{t^3}{2}$$

At $t = 4$ s: $x = \frac{64}{2} = 32$ m

**Answer:** $v = 24$ m/s; $x = 32$ m.

---

**Example 8.** Two particles A and B start from the same point. A moves with constant velocity 4 m/s. B starts from rest with acceleration 2 m/s². When does B overtake A?

**Solution:**

Positions: $x_A = 4t$; $x_B = \frac{1}{2}(2)t^2 = t^2$.

At overtaking: $x_A = x_B \Rightarrow 4t = t^2 \Rightarrow t(t-4) = 0 \Rightarrow t = 4$ s (reject $t = 0$).

At $t = 4$ s: $x = 16$ m.

Note: At $t = 2$ s, B's velocity $= 2(2) = 4$ m/s $=$ A's velocity — this is when B momentarily has the same speed as A, not when it overtakes. Overtaking happens later at $t = 4$ s when B has built up more displacement.

**Answer:** B overtakes A at $t = 4$ s at position $x = 16$ m from start.

---

**Example 9.** Distance traveled in the $n$th second: $s_n = u + \frac{a}{2}(2n - 1)$. A particle has $u = 10$ m/s, $a = 2$ m/s². Find the distance in (a) the 3rd second, (b) the 6th second.

**Solution:**

(a) $s_3 = 10 + \frac{2}{2}(2 \times 3 - 1) = 10 + 1 \times 5 = 15$ m

(b) $s_6 = 10 + \frac{2}{2}(2 \times 6 - 1) = 10 + 11 = 21$ m

**Verification for (a):** $x(3) - x(2) = \left(10(3) + \frac{1}{2}(2)(9)\right) - \left(10(2) + \frac{1}{2}(2)(4)\right) = 39 - 24 = 15$ m. ✓

**Answer:** $s_3 = 15$ m; $s_6 = 21$ m.

---

**Example 10.** Two stones are dropped from the same point at $t = 0$ and $t = 1$ s respectively. Find the separation between them at $t = 3$ s (after the first stone was dropped). Take $g = 10$ m/s².

**Solution:**

Stone 1 (dropped at $t = 0$): falls for 3 s.
$$y_1 = \frac{1}{2}(10)(3^2) = 45 \text{ m}$$

Stone 2 (dropped at $t = 1$ s): falls for 2 s.
$$y_2 = \frac{1}{2}(10)(2^2) = 20 \text{ m}$$

Separation $= 45 - 20 = 25$ m.

**Key insight:** The separation at time $t$ after the first drop is $\frac{1}{2}g[(t)^2 - (t-1)^2] = \frac{1}{2}g(2t-1)$. As $t$ increases, the separation grows linearly.

**Answer:** Separation $= 25$ m.

---

### Category 2: One-Dimensional Motion (Examples 11–20)

---

**Example 11.** A ball is thrown vertically upward with speed $u = 30$ m/s from ground. Find (a) maximum height, (b) time to reach max height, (c) velocity at $t = 4$ s, (d) position at $t = 4$ s. Take $g = 10$ m/s².

**Solution:**

(a) $H = \frac{u^2}{2g} = \frac{900}{20} = 45$ m

(b) $t_{peak} = \frac{u}{g} = \frac{30}{10} = 3$ s

(c) At $t = 4$ s (1 s after peak, ball is falling):
$v = u - gt = 30 - 10(4) = -10$ m/s (downward, $-$ sign means downward)

(d) $y(4) = 30(4) - \frac{1}{2}(10)(16) = 120 - 80 = 40$ m above ground.

**Answer:** $H = 45$ m; $t_{peak} = 3$ s; $v(4) = -10$ m/s; $y(4) = 40$ m.

---

**Example 12.** A stone is dropped from a height of 125 m. At the same instant, another stone is thrown upward from the ground at 25 m/s. Where and when do they meet?

**Solution:**

Let meeting time $= t$. Let upward be positive, ground is origin.

Stone 1 (dropped): $y_1 = 125 - \frac{1}{2}(10)t^2 = 125 - 5t^2$

Stone 2 (thrown up): $y_2 = 25t - 5t^2$

At meeting: $y_1 = y_2$:
$$125 - 5t^2 = 25t - 5t^2$$
$$125 = 25t \Rightarrow t = 5 \text{ s}$$
$$y = 25(5) - 5(25) = 125 - 125 = 0 \text{ m}$$

Both stones reach the ground at exactly $t = 5$ s (stone 1: $125 = 5t^2 \Rightarrow t = 5$ s; stone 2: time of flight $= 2(25)/10 = 5$ s), so they meet right at ground level.

**Answer:** They meet at ground level at $t = 5$ s.

---

**Example 13.** A car is moving at 72 km/h when the brakes are applied, giving a deceleration of $5$ m/s². Find (a) stopping distance, (b) time to stop.

**Solution:**

$u = 72 \times \frac{1000}{3600} = 20$ m/s; $v = 0$; $a = -5$ m/s²

(a) $v^2 = u^2 + 2as$: $0 = 400 - 10s \Rightarrow s = 40$ m

(b) $v = u + at$: $0 = 20 - 5t \Rightarrow t = 4$ s

**Answer:** Stopping distance $= 40$ m; time to stop $= 4$ s.

---

**Example 14.** The velocity of a particle is related to displacement by $v^2 = 4 + 2x$. Find the acceleration as a function of $x$.

**Solution:**

Use $a = v\frac{dv}{dx}$, or differentiate $v^2 = 4 + 2x$ with respect to time:

$$2v\frac{dv}{dt} = 2\frac{dx}{dt} = 2v$$
$$2va = 2v \Rightarrow a = 1 \text{ m/s}^2 \text{ (constant!)}$$

Alternatively: $a = \frac{v\,dv}{dx} = \frac{d(v^2/2)}{dx} = \frac{d(2 + x)}{dx} = 1$ m/s².

**Answer:** $a = 1$ m/s² (constant, independent of $x$).

---

**Example 15.** A body starts with velocity 2 m/s and moves with acceleration $a = 6 - 2t$ m/s². Find (a) when acceleration is zero, (b) maximum velocity, (c) velocity at $t = 5$ s.

**Solution:**

(a) $a = 0$: $6 - 2t = 0 \Rightarrow t = 3$ s

(b) Velocity is maximum when $a = 0$ (i.e., at $t = 3$ s):
$$v(t) = 2 + \int_0^t (6 - 2t')\,dt' = 2 + 6t - t^2$$
$v(3) = 2 + 18 - 9 = 11$ m/s

(c) $v(5) = 2 + 30 - 25 = 7$ m/s

**Answer:** $a = 0$ at $t = 3$ s; max velocity $= 11$ m/s; $v(5) = 7$ m/s.

---

**Example 16.** Two trains approach each other on the same track. Train A moves at 54 km/h and Train B at 72 km/h. When they are 630 m apart, both drivers apply brakes simultaneously, with decelerations 1 m/s² and 2 m/s² respectively. Do they collide?

**Solution:**

Convert: $v_A = 15$ m/s, $v_B = 20$ m/s.

Stopping distances:
$$s_A = \frac{v_A^2}{2a_A} = \frac{225}{2} = 112.5 \text{ m}$$
$$s_B = \frac{v_B^2}{2a_B} = \frac{400}{4} = 100 \text{ m}$$

Total stopping distance $= 112.5 + 100 = 212.5$ m $< 630$ m.

**They do not collide.** Gap remaining $= 630 - 212.5 = 417.5$ m.

**Answer:** No collision; final gap $= 417.5$ m.

---

**Example 17.** A particle moves along x-axis with $x(t) = 2t^3 - 9t^2 + 12t - 4$. Find (a) velocity at $t = 0$, (b) when velocity = 0, (c) acceleration at $t = 2$ s.

**Solution:**

$v(t) = 6t^2 - 18t + 12 = 6(t^2 - 3t + 2) = 6(t-1)(t-2)$
$a(t) = 12t - 18$

(a) $v(0) = 12$ m/s

(b) $v = 0$: $t = 1$ s or $t = 2$ s

(c) $a(2) = 24 - 18 = 6$ m/s²

**Answer:** $v(0) = 12$ m/s; $v = 0$ at $t = 1$ s and $t = 2$ s; $a(2) = 6$ m/s².

---

**Example 18.** A ball is dropped from rest from height $H$. It bounces elastically (no energy loss). Find the total distance traveled after $n$ bounces and the total time.

**Solution:**

After dropping from $H$: speed at ground $= \sqrt{2gH}$, time $= \sqrt{2H/g}$.

The ball bounces back to height $H$ (elastic), taking time $2\sqrt{2H/g}$ for each full bounce.

**Distance:** Down $H$, up $H$, down $H$, up $H$, ... After $n$ complete bounces: $H + 2nH = (2n+1)H$.

**Time:** First drop: $t_0 = \sqrt{2H/g}$. Each bounce (up + down): $2t_0$. After $n$ bounces: $T = t_0 + 2nt_0 = (2n+1)\sqrt{2H/g}$.

**Answer:** Distance after $n$ bounces $= (2n+1)H$; total time $= (2n+1)\sqrt{2H/g}$.

---

**Example 19.** Find the distance a freely falling body covers during the last second of its fall if it falls from rest from height $H$. ($g = 10$ m/s²)

**Solution:**

Total fall time: $T = \sqrt{2H/g}$.

Distance in last second:
$$s_{last} = H - \frac{1}{2}g(T-1)^2 = \frac{1}{2}gT^2 - \frac{1}{2}g(T-1)^2 = \frac{g}{2}(2T-1)$$

$$s_{last} = \frac{g}{2}\!\left(2\sqrt{\frac{2H}{g}} - 1\right) = g\sqrt{\frac{H}{2g}} - \frac{g}{2} = \sqrt{\frac{gH}{2}} - \frac{g}{2}$$

**Numerical check:** $H = 80$ m, $T = 4$ s. $s_{last} = 80 - \frac{1}{2}(10)(9) = 80 - 45 = 35$ m. Formula: $\sqrt{(10)(80)/2} - 5 = \sqrt{400} - 5 = 20 - 5 = 15$... 

Recalculate: $\frac{g}{2}(2T-1) = 5(8-1) = 35$ m. ✓

**Answer:** $s_{last} = \frac{g}{2}(2T-1)$ where $T = \sqrt{2H/g}$.

---

**Example 20.** A particle starts from rest and has uniform acceleration. In the first 2 s it covers $x_1$, in the next 2 s it covers $x_2$. Show that $x_2 = 3x_1$.

**Solution:**

$x_1 = \frac{1}{2}a(2)^2 = 2a$

$x_1 + x_2 = \frac{1}{2}a(4)^2 = 8a$

$x_2 = 8a - 2a = 6a = 3(2a) = 3x_1$ ✓

**Generalisation:** In equal time intervals $t$, the distances covered from rest are in ratio $1 : 3 : 5 : 7 : \ldots$ (odd numbers). This is Galileo's odd-number law.

**Answer:** Proved: $x_2 = 3x_1$.

---

### Category 3: Graphs in Kinematics (Examples 21–28)

---

**Example 21.** A v-t graph shows velocity increasing linearly from 0 to 20 m/s in 4 s, then constant at 20 m/s for 2 s, then decreasing linearly to 0 in 2 s. Find (a) acceleration in each phase, (b) total displacement, (c) total distance.

**Solution:**

(a) Phase 1 (0–4 s): $a_1 = \frac{20-0}{4} = 5$ m/s²
Phase 2 (4–6 s): $a_2 = 0$
Phase 3 (6–8 s): $a_3 = \frac{0-20}{2} = -10$ m/s²

(b) Displacement = area under v-t graph (velocity stays positive throughout):
$$s = \frac{1}{2}(4)(20) + (2)(20) + \frac{1}{2}(2)(20) = 40 + 40 + 20 = 100 \text{ m}$$

(c) Since velocity never goes negative, distance $=$ displacement $= 100$ m.

**Answer:** Accelerations: 5, 0, $-10$ m/s²; displacement = distance $= 100$ m.

---

**Example 22.** An x-t graph for a particle is a straight line passing through $(0, 2)$ and $(4, 10)$. Find (a) initial position, (b) velocity, (c) position at $t = 7$ s.

**Solution:**

(a) At $t = 0$: $x = 2$ m.

(b) Slope (velocity) $= \frac{10 - 2}{4 - 0} = \frac{8}{4} = 2$ m/s.

(c) $x(7) = 2 + 2(7) = 16$ m.

**Answer:** $x_0 = 2$ m; $v = 2$ m/s; $x(7) = 16$ m.

---

**Example 23.** A v-t graph crosses the time axis at $t = 3$ s, going from $+12$ m/s (at $t = 0$) to $-6$ m/s (at $t = 6$ s), linearly. Find (a) acceleration, (b) displacement from $t = 0$ to $t = 6$ s, (c) total distance.

**Solution:**

(a) $a = \frac{-6 - 12}{6} = \frac{-18}{6} = -3$ m/s²

(b) Displacement = algebraic area:
$$s = \frac{1}{2}(3)(12) + \frac{1}{2}(3)(-6) = 18 - 9 = 9 \text{ m}$$

(c) Distance = sum of absolute areas:
$$d = \frac{1}{2}(3)(12) + \frac{1}{2}(3)(6) = 18 + 9 = 27 \text{ m}$$

**Answer:** $a = -3$ m/s²; displacement $= 9$ m; distance $= 27$ m.

---

**Example 24.** A $v^2$–$s$ graph is a straight line passing through the origin with slope 4 s$^{-2}$ (i.e., $v^2 = 4s$). Find the acceleration.

**Solution:**

$a = v\frac{dv}{ds} = \frac{d(v^2/2)}{ds} = \frac{1}{2}\frac{d(v^2)}{ds} = \frac{1}{2} \times 4 = 2$ m/s²

**Or:** Differentiating $v^2 = 4s$ with respect to time: $2v\dot{v} = 4\dot{s} = 4v$, so $a = 2$ m/s² (constant).

**Answer:** $a = 2$ m/s².

---

**Example 25.** From a v-t graph: two particles A and B start from the same point. A has $v_A = 2 + t$; B has $v_B = 4$ m/s (constant). At what time does A catch up to B?

**Solution:**

At $t = 0$: $v_A = 2 < v_B = 4$, so B is initially faster.

A overtakes when equal displacement: $\int_0^T v_A\,dt = \int_0^T v_B\,dt$

$$\int_0^T (2+t)\,dt = \int_0^T 4\,dt$$
$$2T + \frac{T^2}{2} = 4T$$
$$\frac{T^2}{2} = 2T \Rightarrow T = 4 \text{ s}$$

At $T = 4$ s: both have covered $\int_0^4 4\,dt = 16$ m. ✓

**Answer:** A catches up to B at $t = 4$ s.

---

**Example 26.** A particle's position follows $x = 5t - t^2$ (SI). Sketch (a) x-t graph and find when velocity reverses, (b) v-t graph, (c) the total distance in $t = 0$ to $t = 7$ s.

**Solution:**

$v = 5 - 2t$; $v = 0$ at $t = 2.5$ s (velocity reverses direction here).

Maximum position: $x(2.5) = 5(2.5) - (2.5)^2 = 12.5 - 6.25 = 6.25$ m.

At $t = 7$: $x(7) = 35 - 49 = -14$ m.

Distance: from 0 to 2.5 s: moves to $x = 6.25$ m (travels 6.25 m).
From 2.5 s to 7 s: moves from 6.25 m to $-14$ m (travels $6.25 + 14 = 20.25$ m).

Total distance $= 6.25 + 20.25 = 26.5$ m.

Displacement $= x(7) - x(0) = -14 - 0 = -14$ m.

**Answer:** Velocity reverses at $t = 2.5$ s; total distance $= 26.5$ m; displacement $= -14$ m.

---

**Example 27.** A v-t graph shows a horizontal line at $v = 5$ m/s from $t = 0$ to $t = 3$ s. Find (a) displacement, (b) acceleration, (c) distance traveled.

**Solution:**

(a) Displacement $= 5 \times 3 = 15$ m.

(b) $a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t} = 0$ (constant velocity).

(c) Velocity is always positive, so distance $=$ displacement $= 15$ m.

---

**Example 28.** The x-t graph of two particles P and Q intersect at $t = 2$ s at position $x = 10$ m. What does the intersection mean physically?

**Solution:**

The intersection means both particles are at the **same position** ($x = 10$ m) at the **same time** ($t = 2$ s).

**This does not mean their velocities are equal.** The slopes (velocities) of the two curves at the intersection point may be different. Velocities are equal when the tangents to the two curves have equal slopes.

**Common error:** Students confuse "intersection of x-t graphs" (same position at same time) with "same velocity at same time."

**Answer:** Both particles are at $x = 10$ m at $t = 2$ s — they meet at that point. Their velocities (slopes of x-t curves) are generally not equal at this moment.


---

### Category 4: Projectile Motion (Examples 29–38)

---

**Example 29.** A ball is launched at $u = 40$ m/s, $\theta = 30°$ above horizontal. Find (a) time of flight, (b) maximum height, (c) horizontal range, (d) velocity at the highest point. Take $g = 10$ m/s².

**Solution:**

Resolve: $u_x = 40\cos30° = 40 \times \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} = 20\sqrt{3}$ m/s; $u_y = 40\sin30° = 20$ m/s.

(a) $T = \frac{2u_y}{g} = \frac{2 \times 20}{10} = 4$ s

(b) $H = \frac{u_y^2}{2g} = \frac{400}{20} = 20$ m

(c) $R = u_x \times T = 20\sqrt{3} \times 4 = 80\sqrt{3} \approx 138.6$ m

Or: $R = \frac{u^2\sin2\theta}{g} = \frac{1600 \times \sin60°}{10} = \frac{1600\sqrt{3}/2}{10} = 80\sqrt{3}$ m ✓

(d) At highest point, $v_y = 0$; $v_x = u_x = 20\sqrt{3}$ m/s (unchanged throughout).

**Answer:** $T = 4$ s; $H = 20$ m; $R = 80\sqrt{3}$ m; speed at peak $= 20\sqrt{3}$ m/s (horizontal).

---

**Example 30.** A ball is thrown horizontally at 15 m/s from the edge of a cliff 80 m high. Find (a) time to hit the ground, (b) horizontal range, (c) speed just before impact.

**Solution:**

Horizontal: $u_x = 15$ m/s, $a_x = 0$.
Vertical: $u_y = 0$, $a_y = g = 10$ m/s².

(a) Vertical: $80 = \frac{1}{2}(10)t^2 \Rightarrow t^2 = 16 \Rightarrow t = 4$ s

(b) $R = u_x \times t = 15 \times 4 = 60$ m

(c) $v_x = 15$ m/s; $v_y = gt = 40$ m/s
$v = \sqrt{15^2 + 40^2} = \sqrt{225 + 1600} = \sqrt{1825} = 5\sqrt{73} \approx 42.7$ m/s

**Answer:** $t = 4$ s; $R = 60$ m; $v_{impact} \approx 42.7$ m/s.

---

**Example 31.** Two projectiles are fired from the same point with the same speed 20 m/s, at angles 30° and 60°. Compare their (a) ranges, (b) maximum heights, (c) times of flight.

**Solution:**

Note: $\sin(2 \times 30°) = \sin60° = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ and $\sin(2 \times 60°) = \sin120° = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$.

(a) $R_{30} = R_{60} = \frac{u^2\sin2\theta}{g} = \frac{400 \times \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}}{10} = 20\sqrt{3}$ m. **Ranges are equal** (complementary angles).

(b) $H_{30} = \frac{u^2\sin^2 30°}{2g} = \frac{400 \times 0.25}{20} = 5$ m
$H_{60} = \frac{u^2\sin^2 60°}{2g} = \frac{400 \times 0.75}{20} = 15$ m

Ratio $H_{30} : H_{60} = 1 : 3$.

(c) $T_{30} = \frac{2 \times 20\sin30°}{10} = 2$ s; $T_{60} = \frac{2 \times 20\sin60°}{10} = 2\sqrt{3}$ s.

Ratio $T_{30} : T_{60} = 1 : \sqrt{3}$.

**Answer:** Same range; $H_{60} = 3H_{30}$; $T_{60} = \sqrt{3}\,T_{30}$.

---

**Example 32.** A projectile is fired at $\theta = 45°$, $u = 20\sqrt{2}$ m/s. Find the velocity vector at $t = 2$ s.

**Solution:**

$u_x = 20\sqrt{2}\cos45° = 20$ m/s; $u_y = 20\sqrt{2}\sin45° = 20$ m/s.

At $t = 2$ s:
$v_x = 20$ m/s (unchanged)
$v_y = 20 - 10(2) = 0$ m/s

So at $t = 2$ s, the particle is at the peak (vertical velocity = 0).

$\vec{v} = 20\hat{i}$ m/s; speed $= 20$ m/s; direction: horizontal.

**Answer:** $\vec{v} = 20\hat{i}$ m/s at $t = 2$ s (particle is at the peak).

---

**Example 33.** The trajectory of a projectile is given by $y = x\tan30° - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2\cos^2 30°}$. If $g = 10$ m/s² and $u = 20$ m/s, find the range.

**Solution:**

The range is where $y = 0$ (other than $x = 0$):
$$0 = x\tan30° - \frac{gx^2}{2u^2\cos^2 30°}$$
$$\tan30° = \frac{gx}{2u^2\cos^2 30°}$$
$$x = \frac{2u^2\cos^2 30° \cdot \tan30°}{g} = \frac{2u^2\sin30°\cos30°}{g} = \frac{u^2\sin60°}{g}$$
$$R = \frac{400 \times \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}}{10} = 20\sqrt{3} \approx 34.6 \text{ m}$$

**Answer:** $R = 20\sqrt{3}$ m.

---

**Example 34.** A ball is kicked at $u = 25$ m/s, $\theta = 53°$ above horizontal. A wall of height 5 m stands at horizontal distance 30 m. Does the ball clear the wall?

**Solution:**

$u_x = 25\cos53° = 25 \times 0.6 = 15$ m/s; $u_y = 25\sin53° = 25 \times 0.8 = 20$ m/s.

Time to reach $x = 30$ m: $t = 30/15 = 2$ s.

Height at $x = 30$ m: $y(2) = 20(2) - \frac{1}{2}(10)(4) = 40 - 20 = 20$ m.

Since $y = 20$ m $> 5$ m (wall height), the ball clears the wall with 15 m to spare.

**Answer:** Yes, the ball clears the wall. Height at wall $= 20$ m.

---

**Example 35.** At what angles should a ball be projected with speed $u = 20$ m/s so that the horizontal range is 30 m? (Take $g = 10$ m/s².)

**Solution:**

$$R = \frac{u^2\sin2\theta}{g} \Rightarrow \sin2\theta = \frac{Rg}{u^2} = \frac{30 \times 10}{400} = 0.75$$

$$2\theta = \sin^{-1}(0.75) \approx 48.6° \text{ or } 131.4°$$

$$\theta \approx 24.3° \text{ or } 65.7°$$

These are complementary angles (sum $\approx 90°$, confirming the rule). Both give the same range.

**Answer:** $\theta \approx 24.3°$ or $65.7°$.

---

**Example 36.** A projectile is launched from height $h = 20$ m above the ground at $u = 30$ m/s horizontally. A target is on the ground at horizontal distance $d = 60$ m. Does the projectile hit the target?

**Solution:**

Time to fall $h = 20$ m: $20 = \frac{1}{2}(10)t^2 \Rightarrow t = 2$ s.

Horizontal distance in $t = 2$ s: $x = 30 \times 2 = 60$ m = $d$. ✓

**The projectile hits the target exactly.**

**Answer:** Yes — horizontal range $= 60$ m matches target distance.

---

**Example 37.** A projectile has maximum range $R_{max}$ at $\theta = 45°$. At angle $\theta$, its range is $R = R_{max}\sin2\theta$. If $R = \frac{R_{max}\sqrt{3}}{2}$, find $\theta$.

**Solution:**

$$\frac{R_{max}\sqrt{3}}{2} = R_{max}\sin2\theta \Rightarrow \sin2\theta = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \Rightarrow 2\theta = 60° \text{ or } 120°$$

$$\theta = 30° \text{ or } 60°$$

**Answer:** $\theta = 30°$ or $60°$ (both give the same range by complementary angle rule).

---

**Example 38.** A particle is thrown at angle $\theta$ from horizontal with speed $u$. Show that the time to reach maximum height equals half the total time of flight.

**Solution:**

Time to reach peak: $t_{peak} = \frac{u\sin\theta}{g}$.

Total time of flight (symmetric trajectory): $T = \frac{2u\sin\theta}{g}$.

$$t_{peak} = \frac{T}{2} \quad \checkmark$$

**Physical interpretation:** By time symmetry, the time ascending equals the time descending (when launch and landing heights are equal). At the peak, vertical velocity is zero — the trajectory is symmetric about this point.

---

### Category 5: Relative Motion (Examples 39–44)

---

**Example 39.** A river flows at $v_r = 3$ m/s eastward. A boat can move at $v_b = 5$ m/s relative to water. The river is 200 m wide. Find (a) minimum crossing time and drift, (b) heading for zero drift (if possible).

**Solution:**

**(a) Minimum time:** Aim the boat perpendicular to the banks (northward).

$t_{min} = \frac{d}{v_b} = \frac{200}{5} = 40$ s

Drift $= v_r \times t_{min} = 3 \times 40 = 120$ m eastward.

**(b) Zero drift:** Aim upstream at angle $\alpha$ to the perpendicular where $\sin\alpha = \frac{v_r}{v_b} = \frac{3}{5}$, so $\alpha = 37°$ (upstream from perpendicular).

This is possible since $v_b > v_r$. Net northward speed $= v_b\cos\alpha = 5 \times 0.8 = 4$ m/s.

Crossing time $= 200/4 = 50$ s.

**Answer:** (a) $t_{min} = 40$ s, drift $= 120$ m; (b) upstream angle $= 37°$, time $= 50$ s.

---

**Example 40.** Rain falls vertically at 20 m/s. A cyclist moves at 10 m/s eastward. At what angle to vertical should the cyclist hold the umbrella?

**Solution:**

$\vec{v}_{rain} = -20\hat{j}$ m/s; $\vec{v}_{cyclist} = 10\hat{i}$ m/s.

$\vec{v}_{rain/cyclist} = \vec{v}_{rain} - \vec{v}_{cyclist} = -10\hat{i} - 20\hat{j}$ m/s.

The rain appears to come from the **front** (eastward-upward direction relative to cyclist). The umbrella must tilt forward (eastward) at:

$$\tan\theta = \frac{v_{horizontal}}{v_{vertical}} = \frac{10}{20} = 0.5 \Rightarrow \theta = \tan^{-1}(0.5) \approx 26.6° \text{ from vertical}$$

**Answer:** Tilt umbrella $\approx 26.6°$ forward (eastward) from vertical.

---

**Example 41.** Two cars A and B move along parallel roads. A moves north at 40 m/s, B moves north at 30 m/s. Find the velocity of A relative to B.

**Solution:**

$\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_A - \vec{v}_B = 40\hat{j} - 30\hat{j} = 10\hat{j}$ m/s (northward).

From B's perspective, A appears to move north at 10 m/s.

**If B moves south:** $\vec{v}_{A/B} = 40\hat{j} - (-30\hat{j}) = 70\hat{j}$ m/s — they approach each other at 70 m/s.

**Answer:** Relative velocity $= 10$ m/s northward (same direction); if opposite, $= 70$ m/s.

---

**Example 42.** A bird flies between two trains approaching each other. Train A: 54 km/h; Train B: 72 km/h. Initial separation: 270 km. The bird starts from Train A, flies toward B at 120 km/h, turns back when it reaches B, and so on. Find total distance flown by the bird before the trains meet.

**Solution:**

Relative speed of trains $= 54 + 72 = 126$ km/h.

Time to meet $= \frac{270}{126} = \frac{15}{7}$ h.

Bird flies continuously at 120 km/h for $\frac{15}{7}$ h:

$$d_{bird} = 120 \times \frac{15}{7} = \frac{1800}{7} \approx 257.1 \text{ km}$$

**Key insight:** Don't calculate each individual leg — just multiply bird's speed by total time.

**Answer:** Bird flies $\approx 257$ km.

---

**Example 43.** A man can row at 5 m/s in still water. A river flows at 3 m/s. He wants to cross a 150 m wide river. Find the angle upstream he should row to reach the point directly opposite, and the time taken.

**Solution:**

For zero drift, the upstream component of rowing cancels river current:
$$v_b\sin\alpha = v_r \Rightarrow \sin\alpha = \frac{3}{5} \Rightarrow \alpha = 37° \text{ upstream}$$

Net perpendicular speed $= v_b\cos\alpha = 5 \times \frac{4}{5} = 4$ m/s.

Time $= \frac{150}{4} = 37.5$ s.

**Answer:** Row at 37° upstream; time $= 37.5$ s.

---

**Example 44.** Two objects A and B move with velocities $\vec{v}_A = 3\hat{i} + 4\hat{j}$ m/s and $\vec{v}_B = 5\hat{i}$ m/s. Find the magnitude of the relative velocity of A with respect to B.

**Solution:**

$$\vec{v}_{A/B} = (3-5)\hat{i} + (4-0)\hat{j} = -2\hat{i} + 4\hat{j} \text{ m/s}$$

$$|\vec{v}_{A/B}| = \sqrt{(-2)^2 + 4^2} = \sqrt{4 + 16} = \sqrt{20} = 2\sqrt{5} \approx 4.47 \text{ m/s}$$

**Answer:** $|\vec{v}_{A/B}| = 2\sqrt{5}$ m/s.

---

### Category 6: Circular Motion (Examples 45–50)

---

**Example 45.** A particle moves in a circle of radius 2 m with a constant speed of 4 m/s. Find (a) angular velocity, (b) centripetal acceleration, (c) period.

**Solution:**

(a) $\omega = \frac{v}{r} = \frac{4}{2} = 2$ rad/s

(b) $a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{16}{2} = 8$ m/s² (directed toward centre)

(c) $T = \frac{2\pi}{\omega} = \frac{2\pi}{2} = \pi \approx 3.14$ s

**Answer:** $\omega = 2$ rad/s; $a_c = 8$ m/s²; $T = \pi$ s.

---

**Example 46.** A fan blade rotates at 300 rpm. Find (a) angular velocity in rad/s, (b) speed of the tip if blade length is 0.4 m, (c) centripetal acceleration of the tip.

**Solution:**

(a) $\omega = 300 \times \frac{2\pi}{60} = 10\pi \approx 31.4$ rad/s

(b) $v = \omega r = 10\pi \times 0.4 = 4\pi \approx 12.6$ m/s

(c) $a_c = \omega^2 r = (10\pi)^2 \times 0.4 = 100\pi^2 \times 0.4 = 40\pi^2 \approx 395$ m/s²

**Answer:** $\omega = 10\pi$ rad/s; $v = 4\pi$ m/s; $a_c = 40\pi^2$ m/s².

---

**Example 47.** An athlete runs around a circular track of circumference 400 m in 50 s. Find (a) speed, (b) radius, (c) centripetal acceleration.

**Solution:**

(a) $v = \frac{400}{50} = 8$ m/s

(b) $C = 2\pi r \Rightarrow r = \frac{400}{2\pi} = \frac{200}{\pi} \approx 63.7$ m

(c) $a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{64}{200/\pi} = \frac{64\pi}{200} = \frac{16\pi}{50} \approx 1.0$ m/s²

**Answer:** $v = 8$ m/s; $r \approx 63.7$ m; $a_c \approx 1.0$ m/s².

---

**Example 48.** A particle moves in a circle of radius $R$ with non-uniform speed. At a certain instant, its speed is $v$ and increasing at rate $\alpha$ m/s². Find the net acceleration and its angle with the radius.

**Solution:**

Centripetal acceleration: $a_c = \frac{v^2}{R}$ (inward along radius)

Tangential acceleration: $a_t = \alpha$ (along velocity, tangent to circle)

Net acceleration: $|a_{net}| = \sqrt{a_c^2 + a_t^2} = \sqrt{\frac{v^4}{R^2} + \alpha^2}$

Angle with radius: $\tan\phi = \frac{a_t}{a_c} = \frac{\alpha R}{v^2}$

**Answer:** $|a| = \sqrt{v^4/R^2 + \alpha^2}$; angle from radius $\phi = \tan^{-1}(\alpha R/v^2)$.

---

**Example 49.** Earth moves around the Sun in an approximately circular orbit of radius $1.5 \times 10^{11}$ m with period 365 days. Find (a) orbital speed, (b) centripetal acceleration.

**Solution:**

$T = 365 \times 24 \times 3600 = 3.156 \times 10^7$ s; $r = 1.5 \times 10^{11}$ m.

(a) $v = \frac{2\pi r}{T} = \frac{2\pi \times 1.5 \times 10^{11}}{3.156 \times 10^7} \approx \frac{9.42 \times 10^{11}}{3.156 \times 10^7} \approx 2.98 \times 10^4$ m/s $\approx$ 30 km/s

(b) $a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{(2.98 \times 10^4)^2}{1.5 \times 10^{11}} = \frac{8.88 \times 10^8}{1.5 \times 10^{11}} \approx 5.92 \times 10^{-3}$ m/s²

**Answer:** Earth's orbital speed $\approx 30$ km/s; centripetal acceleration $\approx 5.9 \times 10^{-3}$ m/s².

---

**Example 50.** A particle starts from rest and undergoes uniform circular motion. Its angular velocity increases uniformly to $\omega_f = 20$ rad/s in $T = 4$ s. Find (a) angular acceleration, (b) number of revolutions in this time.

**Solution:**

(a) $\alpha = \frac{\omega_f - \omega_0}{T} = \frac{20 - 0}{4} = 5$ rad/s²

(b) Angular displacement: $\theta = \omega_0 t + \frac{1}{2}\alpha t^2 = 0 + \frac{1}{2}(5)(16) = 40$ rad

Number of revolutions $= \frac{\theta}{2\pi} = \frac{40}{2\pi} = \frac{20}{\pi} \approx 6.37$ revolutions.

**Answer:** $\alpha = 5$ rad/s²; number of revolutions $\approx 6.37$.

---


---

### JEE Advanced — Category 3: Relative Motion (Adv. 15–20)

---

**Adv. Example 15.** A river of width $d = 100$ m flows at $v_r = 4$ m/s. A boat moves at $v_b = 3$ m/s relative to water. Show that minimum drift is not achieved by aiming perpendicularly. Find the minimum drift and the required heading.

**Solution:**

Since $v_b < v_r$, zero drift is impossible. Let the boat aim at angle $\phi$ upstream from perpendicular.

Crossing time: $t = \frac{d}{v_b\cos\phi} = \frac{100}{3\cos\phi}$

Net downstream drift speed: $v_r - v_b\sin\phi = 4 - 3\sin\phi$

Total drift: $D(\phi) = (4 - 3\sin\phi) \cdot \frac{100}{3\cos\phi} = \frac{100(4 - 3\sin\phi)}{3\cos\phi}$

For minimum, $\frac{dD}{d\phi} = 0$:

$$\frac{d}{d\phi}\left(\frac{4-3\sin\phi}{\cos\phi}\right) = \frac{-3\cos^2\phi - (4-3\sin\phi)(-\sin\phi)}{\cos^2\phi} = 0$$

$$-3\cos^2\phi + 4\sin\phi - 3\sin^2\phi = 0 \Rightarrow -3 + 4\sin\phi = 0 \Rightarrow \sin\phi = \frac{3}{4}$$

$$\phi = \sin^{-1}(3/4) \approx 48.6°$$

At this $\phi$: $\cos\phi = \sqrt{1 - 9/16} = \sqrt{7}/4$

$$D_{min} = \frac{100(4 - 3 \times 3/4)}{3 \times \sqrt{7}/4} = \frac{100(4 - 9/4)}{3\sqrt{7}/4} = \frac{100 \times 7/4}{3\sqrt{7}/4} = \frac{100 \times 7}{3\sqrt{7}} = \frac{100\sqrt{7}}{3} \approx 88.2 \text{ m}$$

**Answer:** Aim $\phi \approx 48.6°$ upstream; minimum drift $\approx 88.2$ m.

---

**Adv. Example 16.** Two particles A and B start simultaneously. A: from origin with $\vec{v}_A = 3\hat{i} + 4\hat{j}$ m/s. B: from $(10, 0)$ with $\vec{v}_B = -3\hat{i} + 4\hat{j}$ m/s. Find when they are closest and the minimum distance.

**Solution:**

$$\vec{r}_A(t) = 3t\hat{i} + 4t\hat{j}; \quad \vec{r}_B(t) = (10-3t)\hat{i} + 4t\hat{j}$$

Separation: $\vec{d}(t) = \vec{r}_A - \vec{r}_B = (6t - 10)\hat{i}$

The $y$-separation is always zero. Minimum $|\vec{d}|$ when $|6t - 10| = 0 \Rightarrow t = 5/3$ s — **they meet** (minimum distance $= 0$).

**Answer:** They meet at $t = 5/3$ s at position $(5,\; 20/3)$ m.

---

**Adv. Example 17.** Rain falls at angle $\alpha$ to vertical (blown by wind eastward). A man runs eastward at speed $v_m$. Find the speed for rain to appear vertical.

**Solution:**

$\vec{v}_{rain} = v_R\tan\alpha\,\hat{i} - v_R\hat{j}$ (east component $= v_R\tan\alpha$, downward $= v_R$).

Relative velocity: $\vec{v}_{rel} = (v_R\tan\alpha - v_m)\hat{i} - v_R\hat{j}$

For vertical appearance: $v_R\tan\alpha - v_m = 0 \Rightarrow v_m = v_R\tan\alpha$.

**Answer:** $v_m = v_R\tan\alpha$ (equals the horizontal wind component of rain).

---

**Adv. Example 18.** Particle A is at origin moving with $\vec{v}_A = 2\hat{i}$ m/s. Particle B is at $(0, 10)$ m moving with $\vec{v}_B = 2\hat{j}$ m/s. Find minimum distance between them and time it occurs.

**Solution:**

$$\vec{d}(t) = \vec{r}_A - \vec{r}_B = 2t\hat{i} - (10 + 2t)\hat{j}$$
$$|\vec{d}|^2 = 4t^2 + (10+2t)^2 = 8t^2 + 40t + 100$$

Minimize: $16t + 40 = 0 \Rightarrow t = -2.5$ s (before $t = 0$).

For $t \geq 0$, the distance is increasing. Minimum for $t \geq 0$ is at $t = 0$: $|\vec{d}| = 10$ m.

**Answer:** Minimum distance $= 10$ m at $t = 0$.

---

**Adv. Example 19.** A man swims at $v_s = 5$ m/s (still water), runs at $v_r = 10$ m/s. River width $= 100$ m (no current). Point B is 200 m downstream on far bank. Find optimal upstream swim angle for minimum total time.

**Solution:**

Swim at angle $\phi$ upstream. Crossing time: $t_1 = \frac{100}{5\cos\phi} = \frac{20}{\cos\phi}$

Landing point: $100\tan\phi$ upstream of the opposite bank's landing. Distance left to run: $200 - 100\tan\phi$.

Total time: $T = \frac{20}{\cos\phi} + \frac{200 - 100\tan\phi}{10} = \frac{20}{\cos\phi} + 20 - 10\tan\phi$

$\frac{dT}{d\phi} = \frac{20\sin\phi}{\cos^2\phi} - \frac{10}{\cos^2\phi} = \frac{20\sin\phi - 10}{\cos^2\phi} = 0 \Rightarrow \sin\phi = \frac{1}{2} \Rightarrow \phi = 30°$

$T_{min} = \frac{20}{\cos30°} + 20 - 10\tan30° = \frac{40}{\sqrt{3}} + 20 - \frac{10}{\sqrt{3}} = \frac{30}{\sqrt{3}} + 20 = 10\sqrt{3} + 20 \approx 37.3$ s.

**Answer:** Optimal angle $= 30°$ upstream; $T_{min} = (10\sqrt{3} + 20)$ s $\approx 37.3$ s.

---

**Adv. Example 20.** Two cars approach an intersection. Car A moves east at 15 m/s; Car B moves north at 20 m/s. At $t = 0$, A is 90 m west of the intersection and B is 80 m south. Find the time of minimum separation and its value.

**Solution:**

Position at time $t$: $\vec{r}_A = (15t - 90)\hat{i}$; $\vec{r}_B = (20t - 80)\hat{j}$

Separation: $\vec{d}(t) = \vec{r}_A - \vec{r}_B = (15t-90)\hat{i} - (20t-80)\hat{j}$

$$|\vec{d}|^2 = (15t-90)^2 + (20t-80)^2 = 225t^2 - 2700t + 8100 + 400t^2 - 3200t + 6400$$
$$= 625t^2 - 5900t + 14500$$

Minimize: $\frac{d}{dt}|\vec{d}|^2 = 1250t - 5900 = 0 \Rightarrow t = \frac{5900}{1250} = 4.72$ s

$$|\vec{d}|^2_{min} = 625(4.72)^2 - 5900(4.72) + 14500 = 13924 - 27848 + 14500 = 576$$
$$|\vec{d}|_{min} = 24 \text{ m}$$

**Answer:** Minimum separation $= 24$ m at $t = 4.72$ s.

---

### JEE Advanced — Category 4: Circular Motion (Adv. 21–25)

---

**Adv. Example 21.** A particle moves in a circle of radius $R = 2$ m, starting from rest with $\omega = kt$ ($k = 2$ rad/s²). Find magnitude of net acceleration at $t = 2$ s.

**Solution:**

$\omega(2) = 4$ rad/s; $v = \omega R = 8$ m/s.

$a_c = \omega^2 R = 32$ m/s²; $a_t = \frac{d\omega}{dt} \cdot R = 2 \times 2 = 4$ m/s²

$|a| = \sqrt{32^2 + 4^2} = \sqrt{1040} = 4\sqrt{65} \approx 32.2$ m/s²

**Answer:** $|a| = 4\sqrt{65} \approx 32.2$ m/s².

---

**Adv. Example 22.** A bead on a circular wire (radius $R$) has speed $v = k\sqrt{s}$ ($s$ = arc length). Find $a_t$ and $a_c$.

**Solution:**

$a_t = \frac{dv}{dt} = \frac{dv}{ds}\cdot v = \frac{k}{2\sqrt{s}} \cdot k\sqrt{s} = \frac{k^2}{2}$ (constant)

$a_c = \frac{v^2}{R} = \frac{k^2 s}{R}$ (increases with $s$)

**Answer:** $a_t = k^2/2$ (constant); $a_c = k^2 s/R$.

---

**Adv. Example 23.** On a circle of radius 10 m, speed $= 5$ m/s and $dv/dt = 2\sqrt{3}$ m/s². Find angle between net acceleration and radius.

**Solution:**

$a_c = v^2/R = 2.5$ m/s²; $a_t = 2\sqrt{3}$ m/s²

$\tan\phi = a_t/a_c = 2\sqrt{3}/2.5 = 4\sqrt{3}/5$

$\phi = \tan^{-1}(4\sqrt{3}/5) \approx 54.2°$ from radius.

**Answer:** $\phi \approx 54.2°$.

---

**Adv. Example 24.** $\theta(t) = 2t^3 - 3t^2 + t$ rad. Find (a) when $\omega = 0$, (b) average angular acceleration in $0$ to $2$ s.

**Solution:**

$\omega = 6t^2 - 6t + 1 = 0 \Rightarrow t = \frac{3 \pm \sqrt{3}}{6}$; so $t \approx 0.211$ s and $0.789$ s.

Average $\alpha = \frac{\omega(2) - \omega(0)}{2} = \frac{13 - 1}{2} = 6$ rad/s².

**Answer:** $\omega = 0$ at $t \approx 0.211$ s and $0.789$ s; $\bar{\alpha} = 6$ rad/s².

---

**Adv. Example 25.** For $x = R\cos\omega t$, $y = R\sin\omega t$: prove $|\vec{v}| = R\omega$, $|\vec{a}| = R\omega^2$, and $\vec{a} = -\omega^2\vec{r}$.

**Solution:**

$\vec{v} = -R\omega\sin\omega t\,\hat{i} + R\omega\cos\omega t\,\hat{j}$; $|\vec{v}| = R\omega$ ✓

$\vec{a} = -R\omega^2\cos\omega t\,\hat{i} - R\omega^2\sin\omega t\,\hat{j} = -\omega^2\vec{r}$; $|\vec{a}| = R\omega^2$ ✓

Velocity $\perp$ position: $\vec{v}\cdot\vec{r} = 0$ ✓. All proved.

---

### JEE Advanced — Category 5: Multi-Concept (Adv. 26–30)

---

**Adv. Example 26.** Find angles for projectile with $u = 20$ m/s to hit target at $(10, 5)$ m.

**Solution:**

Using trajectory equation with $T = \tan\theta$:

$5 = 10T - \frac{g(100)}{2u^2}(1+T^2) = 10T - \frac{5}{4}(1+T^2)$

$T^2 - 8T + 5 = 0 \Rightarrow T = 4 \pm \sqrt{11}$

$\theta_1 = \tan^{-1}(4+\sqrt{11}) \approx 82.2°$; $\theta_2 = \tan^{-1}(4-\sqrt{11}) \approx 34.1°$

**Answer:** $\theta \approx 34.1°$ or $82.2°$.

---

**Adv. Example 27.** Ball released from rim of frictionless bowl, radius $R$. Speed and acceleration at bottom.

**Solution:**

$v = \sqrt{2gR}$ (energy conservation).

At bottom: $a_c = v^2/R = 2g$ upward; $a_t = 0$ (no tangential force).

Net acceleration $= 2g$ upward; normal force $= 3mg$.

**Answer:** $v = \sqrt{2gR}$; $|a| = 2g$ upward.

---

**Adv. Example 28.** Projectile from base of slope angle $\beta$. Find optimal angle $\theta$ (from horizontal) for max slope range and the max range formula.

**Solution:**

Using oblique projectile analysis:

$R_{slope} = \frac{2u^2\sin(\theta-\beta)\cos\theta}{g\cos^2\beta}$

Maximize $\sin(\theta-\beta)\cos\theta = \frac{1}{2}[\sin(2\theta-\beta) - \sin\beta]$.

Max when $2\theta - \beta = 90°$ so $\theta_{opt} = 45° + \beta/2$.

$R_{max} = \frac{u^2}{g(1+\sin\beta)}$

**Answer:** $\theta_{opt} = 45° + \beta/2$; $R_{max} = \frac{u^2}{g(1+\sin\beta)}$.

---

**Adv. Example 29.** A: from origin at $u = 10$ m/s, $60°$. B: from $(0, h)$ horizontally at $u_B$. Launched simultaneously; they collide. Find $u_B$ and $h$ if collision occurs at $t = 1$ s.

**Solution:**

$x_A = 5t$; $x_B = u_B t$. Equal $x$: $u_B = 5$ m/s.

$y_A = 5\sqrt{3}\,t - 5t^2$; $y_B = h - 5t^2$. Equal $y$: $h = 5\sqrt{3}\,t$.

At $t = 1$ s: $h = 5\sqrt{3} \approx 8.66$ m; $u_B = 5$ m/s.

**Answer:** $u_B = 5$ m/s; $h = 5\sqrt{3}$ m.

---

**Adv. Example 30.** Particle starts from origin with $\vec{u} = u\hat{i}$, constant acceleration $\vec{a} = a\hat{j}$. Prove path is parabola $y = \frac{a}{2u^2}x^2$ and find radius of curvature at origin.

**Solution:**

$x = ut$, $y = \frac{1}{2}at^2$. Eliminate $t$: $y = \frac{a}{2u^2}x^2$ — parabola. ✓

At origin: speed $= u$ (horizontal), all acceleration is centripetal:
$\rho = \frac{u^2}{a}$

**Answer:** Path: $y = \frac{a}{2u^2}x^2$; $\rho_0 = u^2/a$.

---

<Callout type="important">
**Section 17 Summary**

**50 JEE Main Examples** covered all six kinematics categories:
- Cat 1 (1–10): Distance, displacement, average/instantaneous velocity
- Cat 2 (11–20): 1D equations of motion, free fall, nth-second formula
- Cat 3 (21–28): x-t and v-t graph reading, area and slope
- Cat 4 (29–38): Projectile — all standard configurations
- Cat 5 (39–44): Relative motion — river-boat, rain, inter-vehicle
- Cat 6 (45–50): Circular motion — UCM and non-UCM

**30 JEE Advanced Examples** covering multi-concept problems:
- Adv 1–6: Variable acceleration, exponential/SHM motion
- Adv 7–14: Projectile — height, collision in air, radius of curvature
- Adv 15–20: Minimum drift, closest approach, relative velocity
- Adv 21–25: Non-uniform circular motion, UCM proof
- Adv 26–30: Inclined range, simultaneous projection, parabola derivation

**Before Section 18:** For every example where you could not write the solution independently, redo it from scratch (blank paper) before continuing.
</Callout>

---

*— Section 17 complete. Say **"next"** for Section 18: Practice Problems (100 JEE Main + 75 JEE Advanced).*


## Section 18: Practice Problems

> **100 JEE Main + 75 JEE Advanced problems.** Attempt each problem before checking the answer key at the end of each block. Recommended time: 2 min per JEE Main problem, 5–8 min per JEE Advanced problem.

---

## Part A — JEE Main Practice (Problems 1–100)

*Single-correct MCQ. +4 for correct, −1 for wrong.*

---

### Block 1: Distance, Displacement, Speed, Velocity (1–15)

**1.** A particle moves along a semicircular arc of radius $R$. The ratio of distance traveled to magnitude of displacement is:

(A) $\pi/2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $2/\pi$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\pi$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $1$

---

**2.** A car travels 60 km at 40 km/h, then returns the same distance at 60 km/h. Average speed for the full round trip:

(A) 50 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 48 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 52 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 45 km/h

---

**3.** Position of a particle: $x(t) = 5t^2 - 3t + 2$ (SI). Velocity at $t = 3$ s:

(A) 24 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 27 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 30 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 33 m/s

---

**4.** $x(t) = t^3 - 6t^2 + 9t + 2$. The particle momentarily stops at:

(A) $t = 1$ s only &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $t = 3$ s only &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Both $t = 1$ s and $t = 3$ s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $t = 2$ s

---

**5.** A person walks 3 km north, 4 km east, then 3 km south. Displacement magnitude:

(A) 10 km &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 7 km &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 4 km &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\sqrt{34}$ km

---

**6.** $v(t) = 4 - 2t$ m/s. Distance traveled from $t = 0$ to $t = 4$ s:

(A) 0 &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 4 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 8 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 6 m

---

**7.** Car A has acceleration $a$; car B has acceleration $3a$. Both start from rest at the same point. Ratio of distance covered by A to B in the same time:

(A) $1:3$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $3:1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $1:9$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $1:\sqrt{3}$

---

**8.** A bullet enters a plank at 300 m/s and exits at 100 m/s after penetrating 0.1 m. Deceleration of the bullet:

(A) $4 \times 10^5$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $8 \times 10^4$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $4 \times 10^4$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $3 \times 10^5$ m/s²

---

**9.** $x = 6t - t^2$ (SI). The particle reverses direction at position:

(A) $x = 6$ m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $x = 9$ m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $x = 8$ m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $x = 12$ m

---

**10.** A particle moving with uniform acceleration covers $x_1$ in the first 2 s and $x_2$ in the next 2 s. Then $x_2 - x_1$ equals:

(A) $4a$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $8a$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $2a$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $6a$

---

**11.** Which graph represents uniformly accelerated motion as a straight line?

(A) $x$–$t$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $v$–$t$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $v^2$–$t$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $x$–$t^3$

---

**12.** Distance covered in the 5th second by a body starting from rest with acceleration $a = 2$ m/s²:

(A) 7 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 8 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 9 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 10 m

---

**13.** A particle starts from rest and reaches velocity $v$ after time $t$. The distance covered in the last second of motion (assume constant deceleration from $v$ to 0 takes time $t$):

(A) $\dfrac{v}{2t}(2t-1)$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\dfrac{v}{2t}$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\dfrac{v(2t+1)}{2t}$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $vt$

---

**14.** Displacement $s \propto t^{3/2}$. Acceleration is proportional to:

(A) $s^{1/3}$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $v^{1/3}$ (C) $v$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $s^{-1/3}$

---

**15.** A body starting from rest: distance in 1st second is $d_1$ and in 3rd second is $d_3$. Ratio $d_1 : d_3$:

(A) $1:5$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $1:3$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $5:1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $3:1$

---

### Block 2: 1D Equations of Motion (16–30)

**16.** A car accelerates uniformly from 20 m/s to 40 m/s while covering 100 m. Acceleration:

(A) 6 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 5 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 4 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 3 m/s²

---

**17.** A stone is thrown downward from a tower at 10 m/s. It reaches the ground in 3 s ($g = 10$ m/s²). Height of tower:

(A) 45 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 60 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 75 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 30 m

---

**18.** A body covers $S_1$ in the first $t$ seconds of uniform acceleration, and $S_2$ in the next $t$ seconds. The acceleration equals:

(A) $\dfrac{S_2-S_1}{t^2}$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\dfrac{2(S_2-S_1)}{t^2}$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\dfrac{S_2-S_1}{2t^2}$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\dfrac{S_1+S_2}{t^2}$

---

**19.** A ball is dropped from 80 m. At the same instant another is thrown up from ground at 40 m/s. Height at which they meet ($g = 10$ m/s²):

(A) 20 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 40 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 56 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 60 m

---

**20.** A particle starts from rest; acceleration $a = 10 - 2t$ m/s². Maximum velocity attained:

(A) 15 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 20 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 25 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 30 m/s

---

**21.** $v^2 = 4 + 4s$ (SI). Acceleration of the particle:

(A) 1 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 2 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 4 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 8 m/s²

---

**22.** Two trains approach each other; Train A at 72 km/h, Train B at 108 km/h, initial separation 1800 m. Time to meet:

(A) 20 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 30 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 36 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 48 s

---

**23.** A stone is projected vertically upward with $u$. At half the maximum height, its speed is:

(A) $u/2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $u/\sqrt{2}$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $u\sqrt{3}/2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $u/\sqrt{3}$

---

**24.** A ball is thrown upward at 20 m/s from the top of a 60 m cliff ($g = 10$ m/s²). Time to reach the ground:

(A) 4 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 5 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 6 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 8 s

---

**25.** Particle starts from rest; acceleration $= kt$ where $k = 2$ m/s³. Velocity at $t = 3$ s:

(A) 6 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 9 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 12 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 18 m/s

---

**26.** $v^2 = 4(1-x^2)$. The particle oscillates between:

(A) $x = -2$ and $x = 2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $x = -1$ and $x = 1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $x = 0$ and $x = 1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $x = -4$ and $x = 4$

---

**27.** A particle decelerates uniformly. At $t = 0$: $v = 30$ m/s. It stops at $t = 6$ s. Distance traveled in the last 2 s:

(A) 10 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 20 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 5 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 8 m

---

**28.** A particle covers distances in ratio $1:2:3$ in consecutive equal time intervals. The type of motion is:

(A) Uniform acceleration from rest &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Uniform acceleration, $u \neq 0$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Uniform velocity &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Non-uniform acceleration

---

**29.** Two bodies start simultaneously from rest. Body A with acceleration 2 m/s² and body B with acceleration 8 m/s². When B has traveled 4 times A's distance, their velocities are in ratio:

(A) $1:2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $1:4$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $1:2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $1:\sqrt{2}$

---

**30.** A body under constant retardation: initial speed $u$, stops after traveling distance $s$. At distance $s/4$ from start, speed is:

(A) $\sqrt{3}\,u/2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $u/2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $3u/4$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $u/\sqrt{2}$

---

### Block 3: Graphs in Kinematics (31–45)

**31.** A v-t graph shows velocity increasing uniformly from 0 to 20 m/s in 5 s. Displacement in this time:

(A) 25 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 50 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 100 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 20 m

---

**32.** In a v-t graph, the area between the curve and the time axis from $t_1$ to $t_2$ represents:

(A) Speed &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Acceleration &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Displacement &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Distance (always)

---

**33.** Slope of an x-t graph at any point gives:

(A) Average velocity &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Instantaneous velocity &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Average speed &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Acceleration

---

**34.** An x-t graph is a straight horizontal line. The particle is:

(A) Moving with constant velocity &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Decelerating &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) At rest &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Accelerating

---

**35.** A v-t graph is a straight line with negative slope, starting above the time axis and crossing it. This represents:

(A) Uniform deceleration, reversal, then uniform acceleration &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Uniform acceleration &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Uniform deceleration without reversal &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Uniform deceleration then reversal of direction

---

**36.** From a v-t graph: velocity is $+12$ m/s from $t = 0$ to $t = 3$ s, then $-8$ m/s from $t = 3$ s to $t = 6$ s. Distance traveled in 6 s:

(A) 4 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 12 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 60 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 36 m

---

**37.** Particle's velocity: $+10$ m/s for 2 s, then $-6$ m/s for 3 s. Total distance and displacement:

Distance: $20 + 18 = 38$ m. Displacement: $20 - 18 = 2$ m.

(A) Distance 38 m, Displacement 2 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Distance 2 m, Displacement 38 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Both 20 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Distance 20 m, Displacement 38 m

---

**38.** A $v^2$–$s$ graph is a straight line passing through origin with slope $k$. Acceleration:

(A) $k$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $k/2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $2k$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $k^2$

---

**39.** Two particles start from the same point. Their x-t graphs intersect at $t = 4$ s. At this moment:

(A) Their velocities are equal &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Their accelerations are equal &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) They are at the same position &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Both A and C

---

**40.** An x-t graph is concave downward ($\cap$ shape). The acceleration is:

(A) Zero &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Positive &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Negative &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Increasing

---

**41.** The slope of a v-t graph gives:

(A) Speed &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Displacement &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Acceleration &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Distance

---

**42.** A particle starts from $x = 10$ m, moves to $x = -5$ m in 3 s, then back to $x = 8$ m in next 4 s. Average velocity in 7 s:

(A) $\frac{2}{7}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $-\frac{2}{7}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 1 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\frac{23}{7}$ m/s

---

**43.** An acceleration–time graph is a horizontal line at $a = 5$ m/s². This means:

(A) Uniform acceleration &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Uniform velocity &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Non-uniform acceleration &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Circular motion

---

**44.** From a v-t graph with two phases: phase 1 — positive slope for 4 s reaching $v = 16$ m/s; phase 2 — negative slope, reaching $v = 0$ in next 8 s. Ratio of accelerations (phase1 : phase2):

(A) $2:1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $1:2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $4:1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $1:4$

---

**45.** The x-t graph of a particle passes through the origin with a positive slope that decreases over time. The particle is:

(A) Accelerating in positive direction &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Moving with constant velocity &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Decelerating in positive direction &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Moving in negative direction

---

### Block 4: Projectile Motion (46–65)

**46.** A ball launched at $u = 20$ m/s, $\theta = 45°$. Maximum height ($g = 10$ m/s²):

(A) 5 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 10 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 20 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 40 m

---

**47.** Horizontal range of a projectile equals maximum height. Launch angle $\theta$:

$R = H \Rightarrow \frac{u^2\sin2\theta}{g} = \frac{u^2\sin^2\theta}{2g}$

$2\sin\theta\cos\theta = \frac{\sin^2\theta}{2} \Rightarrow 4\cos\theta = \sin\theta \Rightarrow \tan\theta = 4$

(A) $\tan^{-1}(2)$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\tan^{-1}(4)$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $45°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\tan^{-1}(1/4)$

---

**48.** A ball is thrown horizontally at 10 m/s from 45 m height. Horizontal range ($g = 10$ m/s²):

(A) 10 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 20 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 30 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 45 m

---

**49.** A projectile has range $R$ and time of flight $T$. Initial vertical component of velocity:

(A) $gT/2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $gT$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $2gT$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $g/T$

---

**50.** Two balls are projected at angles $\theta$ and $(90° - \theta)$ with same speed. Ratio of their ranges:

(A) $1:1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\sin\theta : \cos\theta$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\cos\theta : \sin\theta$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\tan\theta : 1$

---

**51.** At the highest point of a projectile's trajectory, the speed is $u\cos\theta$. The acceleration at the highest point:

(A) Zero &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $g$ upward &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $g$ downward &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $g\cos\theta$ downward

---

**52.** A projectile's range is maximum at $\theta = 45°$. If range at $60°$ is $R_1$ and at $30°$ is $R_2$:

(A) $R_1 > R_2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $R_1 < R_2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $R_1 = R_2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Cannot determine

---

**53.** The equation of a projectile's trajectory is $y = \sqrt{3}\,x - x^2$ (SI, $g = 10$ m/s²). Find the launch speed $u$:

$y = x\tan\theta - \frac{g}{2u^2\cos^2\theta}x^2$. So $\tan\theta = \sqrt{3}$ ($\theta = 60°$) and $\frac{g}{2u^2\cos^2 60°} = 1$.

$\frac{10}{2u^2 \times 1/4} = 1 \Rightarrow \frac{10 \times 4}{2u^2} = 1 \Rightarrow u^2 = 20 \Rightarrow u = 2\sqrt{5}$ m/s.

(A) $2\sqrt{5}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $5\sqrt{2}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\sqrt{10}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $4$ m/s

---

**54.** A projectile is fired at $u = 50$ m/s and $\theta = 30°$. Time of flight ($g = 10$ m/s²):

(A) 2.5 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 5 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 4 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 3 s

---

**55.** Velocity of a projectile makes $45°$ with horizontal 2 s after launch. Vertical component at launch ($g = 10$ m/s²):

At $t = 2$: $v_y = u_y - 20$. Angle $45°$ means $|v_y| = v_x = u_x$.

If still ascending: $u_y - 20 = u_x \Rightarrow u_y - u_x = 20$.
If descending: $20 - u_y = u_x$.

For simplest case (still going up, angle below horizontal): $v_y = u_y - 20 < 0$ if past peak.

For 45° below horizontal: $-(u_y - 20) = u_x$, so $20 - u_y = u_x$.

For 45° above horizontal at $t = 2$: $u_y - 20 = u_x$, so $u_y - u_x = 20$.

(A) 30 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 20 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 25 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 40 m/s

---

**56.** A ball thrown at $45°$ has range 40 m. Maximum height:

$H = R/4$ for $\theta = 45°$: $H = 10$ m.

(A) 5 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 8 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 10 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 20 m

---

**57.** A stone projected at $\theta = 60°$ with $u = 20$ m/s. Speed at height 10 m ($g = 10$ m/s²):

$u_x = 10$ m/s; $u_y = 10\sqrt{3}$ m/s.

$v_y^2 = (10\sqrt{3})^2 - 2(10)(10) = 300 - 200 = 100 \Rightarrow v_y = 10$ m/s.

$v = \sqrt{10^2 + 10^2} = 10\sqrt{2}$ m/s.

(A) $10\sqrt{3}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $10\sqrt{2}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 10 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 20 m/s

---

**58.** A ball is thrown horizontally from height $h$. It lands at distance $d$. If thrown at speed $2u$ (horizontal), landing distance:

$d \propto u$ (time fixed by $h$), so $d$ doubles: $2d$.

(A) $d$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $2d$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $4d$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\sqrt{2}\,d$

---

**59.** At the highest point of a projectile, speed is $v_0$. Radius of curvature of the path at the highest point:

$\rho = v_0^2/g$

(A) $v_0^2/g$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $2v_0^2/g$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $v_0/g$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $g/v_0^2$

---

**60.** Two projectiles are fired simultaneously at the same speed, one at $30°$ and one at $60°$. Which quantity is the same for both?

The angles are complementary ($30° + 60° = 90°$), so they have the **same range**: $R = u^2\sin2\theta/g$, and $\sin(2\times30°) = \sin(2\times60°)$. Their times of flight and maximum heights differ.

(A) Same range &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Same height &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Same time &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Same velocity at impact

---

**61.** The horizontal range of a projectile is 4 times its maximum height. Launch angle:

$R = 4H$: $\frac{u^2\sin2\theta}{g} = \frac{4u^2\sin^2\theta}{2g}$

$2\sin\theta\cos\theta = 2\sin^2\theta \Rightarrow \cos\theta = \sin\theta \Rightarrow \theta = 45°$.

(A) $30°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $45°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $60°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\tan^{-1}(4)$

---

**62.** A projectile's vertical velocity becomes zero (it reaches its peak) 1 s after launch. Its total time of flight is ($g = 10$ m/s²):

Time to the peak is $t_{peak} = u_y/g = 1$ s, so $u_y = 10$ m/s. By symmetry, the time of flight is $T = 2\,t_{peak} = 2$ s.

(A) 1 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 2 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 3 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 4 s

---

**63.** A projectile is fired at $u$ and angle $\theta$. At the highest point its speed and the radius of curvature are respectively:

(A) $u\sin\theta$ and $u^2\cos^2\theta/g$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $u\cos\theta$ and $u^2\cos^2\theta/g$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $u\cos\theta$ and $u^2/g$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $u\sin\theta$ and $u^2\sin^2\theta/g$

---

**64.** A ball is kicked from the ground at $u = 20$ m/s, $\theta = 60°$. Speed when it makes $30°$ with horizontal ($g = 10$ m/s²):

$v_x = u\cos60° = 10$ m/s. At 30°: $v_x = v\cos30°$ → $v = 10/\cos30° = 20/\sqrt{3}$ m/s.

(A) $10\sqrt{3}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $20/\sqrt{3}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 10 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 20 m/s

---

**65.** A projectile is launched at $u = 10\sqrt{2}$ m/s at $45°$. The velocity vector becomes perpendicular to the initial velocity after time ($g = 10$ m/s²):

Initial velocity is $(10, 10)$ m/s; at time $t$ it is $(10,\ 10 - 10t)$. They are perpendicular when the dot product is zero:

$$10(10) + 10(10 - 10t) = 0 \Rightarrow 200 - 100t = 0 \Rightarrow t = 2 \text{ s}$$

(A) 1 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\sqrt{2}$ s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 2 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $2\sqrt{2}$ s

---

### Block 5: Relative Motion (66–80)

**66.** A river flows at 3 m/s; a boat moves at 4 m/s (relative to water). Width = 400 m. Minimum time to cross:

$t = 400/4 = 100$ s (aim perpendicular).

(A) 80 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 100 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 133 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 160 s

---

**67.** Rain falls vertically at 20 m/s. A cyclist moves eastward at $v_c$. The rain makes $45°$ with vertical as seen by the cyclist. Speed of cyclist:

$\tan45° = v_c/20 \Rightarrow v_c = 20$ m/s.

(A) 10 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 20 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 10$\sqrt{2}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 40 m/s

---

**68.** Two cars approach an intersection: Car A east at 10 m/s, Car B north at 10 m/s. Speed of A relative to B:

$\vec{v}_{A/B} = 10\hat{i} - 10\hat{j}$; $|\vec{v}_{A/B}| = 10\sqrt{2}$ m/s.

(A) 0 &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 10 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $10\sqrt{2}$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 20 m/s

---

**69.** A boat aims perpendicular to the bank in a river of width $d$, current speed $v_r$, boat speed $v_b$. Drift is:

(A) $v_r d / v_b$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $v_b d / v_r$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $d v_r v_b$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $d(v_r + v_b)/v_b$

---

**70.** For zero drift crossing ($v_b > v_r$), the boat must aim at angle $\alpha$ upstream where:

(A) $\sin\alpha = v_r/v_b$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\cos\alpha = v_r/v_b$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\tan\alpha = v_r/v_b$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\sin\alpha = v_b/v_r$

---

**71.** A train 200 m long passes a station platform 300 m long at 20 m/s. Time to pass:

Total distance = $200 + 300 = 500$ m; $t = 500/20 = 25$ s.

(A) 10 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 15 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 25 s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 50 s

---

**72.** Rain falls at angle $37°$ to vertical. To hold umbrella upright (vertical), the man must run:

If rain has vertical component $v_R$ and horizontal component $v_H = v_R\tan37°$. For rain to appear vertical, man must run at $v_H = v_R \times 3/4$ in rain's direction.

(A) at $v_R \times 3/4$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) at $v_R \times 4/3$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) at $v_R \times 3/5$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) stay still

---

**73.** Two particles A and B start from the same point. $\vec{v}_A = 6\hat{i}$ m/s, $\vec{v}_B = 6\hat{j}$ m/s. After 2 s, distance between them:

$\vec{r}_A = 12\hat{i}$, $\vec{r}_B = 12\hat{j}$; $d = \sqrt{12^2+12^2} = 12\sqrt{2}$ m.

(A) 24 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $12\sqrt{2}$ m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 12 m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $6\sqrt{2}$ m

---

**74.** A swimmer can swim at $2$ m/s in still water and the river current is $\sqrt{3}$ m/s. Minimum angle of his velocity (with bank) for crossing without being swept backward:

Swimmer must have a net perpendicular-to-bank component > 0. Minimum angle with bank when perpendicular velocity = 0 is when heading exactly upstream: $\sin\theta = v_r/v_s = \sqrt{3}/2 \Rightarrow \theta = 60°$ from bank (or $30°$ from perpendicular).

(A) $30°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $60°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $45°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $90°$

---

**75.** A man in a train moving at 60 km/h sees rain falling at $45°$ to vertical from his window. Actual rainfall speed if rain falls vertically:

Relative rain speed (horizontal) $= 60$ km/h. Vertical $= 60$ km/h (for 45°). Actual rain speed $= 60$ km/h (purely vertical).

(A) 60 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $60\sqrt{2}$ km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 30 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 120 km/h

---

**76.** River width = 100 m, current = 4 m/s, boat speed = 3 m/s (relative to water). Minimum drift possible:

Since $v_b < v_r$, drift cannot be zero. Minimum drift $= d\sqrt{v_r^2-v_b^2}/v_b = 100\sqrt{16-9}/3 = 100\sqrt{7}/3 \approx 88.2$ m.

(A) 0 &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\frac{100\sqrt{7}}{3}$ m &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\frac{400}{3}$ m &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 75 m

---

**77.** A car A moves at 30 m/s east. Car B moves at 40 m/s north. Speed of A relative to B:

$\vec{v}_{A/B} = 30\hat{i} - 40\hat{j}$; magnitude $= \sqrt{900+1600} = 50$ m/s.

(A) 10 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 50 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 70 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $10\sqrt{2}$ m/s

---

**78.** Two trains on parallel tracks: A moving at 54 km/h (east), B moving at 90 km/h (west). Relative velocity of A with respect to B:

$v_{A/B} = 54 - (-90) = 144$ km/h (east) $= 40$ m/s.

(A) 36 km/h east &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 144 km/h west &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 144 km/h east &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 36 km/h west

---

**79.** A boat crosses a 200 m wide river to reach directly opposite in 50 s. River current is 3 m/s. Boat speed relative to water:

Crossing time: $t = 50$ s, perpendicular speed $= 200/50 = 4$ m/s.

Boat speed $= \sqrt{4^2 + 3^2} = 5$ m/s (heading upstream to compensate).

(A) 3 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 4 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 5 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 7 m/s

---

**80.** A man rows 12 km upstream in 3 h, then 12 km downstream in 1.5 h. Find the speed of the river current.

Upstream speed $= 12/3 = 4$ km/h $= u - v_r$; downstream speed $= 12/1.5 = 8$ km/h $= u + v_r$. Subtracting:

$$v_r = \frac{8 - 4}{2} = 2 \text{ km/h} \quad (\text{still-water speed } u = 6 \text{ km/h})$$

(A) 1 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 2 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 3 km/h &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 4 km/h

---

### Block 6: Circular Motion (81–100)

**81.** A particle moves in a circle of radius 0.5 m at 2 rev/s. Centripetal acceleration:

$a_c = \omega^2 r = (4\pi)^2 \times 0.5 = 8\pi^2 \approx 79$ m/s².

(A) $4\pi^2$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $8\pi^2$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $16\pi^2$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $2\pi^2$ m/s²

---

**82.** Period of circular motion doubles. Centripetal acceleration becomes (speed kept same, radius changes):

$a_c = v^2/r$. If $T$ doubles: $r = vT/2\pi$ doubles. So $a_c = v^2/(2r_0) = a_0/2$.

(A) Doubles &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Halves &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Quadruples &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Unchanged

---

**83.** A particle moves in a circle: $\omega = 3t + 2$ rad/s. Angular acceleration at $t = 2$ s:

$\alpha = d\omega/dt = 3$ rad/s² (constant).

(A) 8 rad/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 3 rad/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 6 rad/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 2 rad/s²

---

**84.** Speed of a particle in circular motion of radius 2 m is $4$ m/s. Centripetal acceleration:

$a_c = 16/2 = 8$ m/s².

(A) 2 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 4 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 8 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 16 m/s²

---

**85.** In uniform circular motion, which quantity is constant?

(A) Velocity &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Speed &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) Acceleration &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) Both A and C

---

**86.** Wheel of radius 0.5 m rotates at 300 rpm. Speed of a point on the rim:

$v = \omega r = (300 \times 2\pi/60) \times 0.5 = 10\pi \times 0.5 = 5\pi$ m/s.

(A) $\pi$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $2.5\pi$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $5\pi$ m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $10\pi$ m/s

---

**87.** A particle in circular motion has $a_t = 3$ m/s² and $a_c = 4$ m/s². Net acceleration:

$|a| = \sqrt{9+16} = 5$ m/s².

(A) 1 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 3.5 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 5 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 7 m/s²

---

**88.** A car rounds a circular curve of radius 50 m at constant speed 20 m/s. Centripetal acceleration:

$a_c = 400/50 = 8$ m/s².

(A) 4 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 6 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 8 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) 10 m/s²

---

**89.** In uniform circular motion, the angle between velocity and acceleration is always:

(A) $0°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $45°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $90°$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $180°$

---

**90.** A particle completes one revolution in time $T$. Angular velocity:

(A) $T/2\pi$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $2\pi/T$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $\pi/T$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $T/\pi$

---

**91.** $x = 5\cos(2t)$, $y = 5\sin(2t)$ (SI). Speed of the particle:

$v_x = -10\sin2t$, $v_y = 10\cos2t$; $|v| = 10$ m/s (constant).

(A) 5 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) 10 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 20 m/s &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $5\sqrt{2}$ m/s

---

**92.** In $x = R\cos\omega t$, $y = R\sin\omega t$, the centripetal acceleration has magnitude:

(A) $\omega R$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $\omega^2 R$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $R/\omega^2$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $R/\omega$

---

**93.** A particle starts from rest on a circular path (radius 4 m). Tangential acceleration $= 2$ m/s². After 2 s, net acceleration:

$v = 0 + 2(2) = 4$ m/s; $a_c = 16/4 = 4$ m/s²; $a_t = 2$ m/s².

$|a| = \sqrt{16+4} = \sqrt{20} = 2\sqrt{5}$ m/s².

(A) 4 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $2\sqrt{5}$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) 6 m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $\sqrt{20}$ m/s²

---

**94.** The ratio of angular velocities of minute hand to hour hand of a clock:

$\omega_{min} = 2\pi/3600$ rad/s; $\omega_{hour} = 2\pi/43200$ rad/s. Ratio $= 12:1$.

(A) $12:1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $1:12$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $60:1$ &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $1:60$

---

**95.** Earth completes one orbit in 365 days ($r = 1.5 \times 10^{11}$ m). Approximate centripetal acceleration:

$\omega = 2\pi/(365 \times 86400)$; $a = \omega^2 r \approx 6 \times 10^{-3}$ m/s².

(A) $6 \times 10^{-3}$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) $6 \times 10^{-2}$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) $9.8$ m/s² &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) $3 \times 10^{-3}$ m/s²

---

**96.** For a particle in UCM, when (after $t = 0$) is its velocity parallel to its displacement measured from the starting point?

The displacement from the start is a chord, while the velocity is always tangent to the circle. A chord and the tangent coincide only in the limit $t \to 0$; for any finite arc they are never parallel. (By contrast, the velocity is always *perpendicular* to the position vector measured from the centre.)

(A) Always &nbsp;&nbsp; (B) Never (after $t = 0$) &nbsp;&nbsp; (C) After half revolution &nbsp;&nbsp; (D) After full revolution

---
